THE RED PAGE
Letter to a Bushfellow.
MY DEAR JOHN, —I have just seen
Boule de Suif ! Positively ! She
left the North Sydney tram- at
Walker-street, and trotted across
the road, in full view of my delighted
eyes. She has not changed one whit
since Maupassant described her and Thdvenot
pictured her. Such a roly-poly ! a real suet-
ball, ball of fat, dumpy, podgy, with plump
little fingers squeezed in at the joints like
rows of short sausages—rather too tightly-
stretched and shiny, perhaps—the bust'enor-
mous ; yet withal so wholesomely, temptingly,
fresh and appetising that it was a pleasure to
look at her. Her face was turned away ; I
did not see her face ; but Maupassant tells
of it—it was
like a ruddy apple—a peony rose just burst, into
bloom—and out of it gazed a pair of magnificent
dark eyes overshadowed by long thick lashes that
deepened their blackness ; and lower down, a
charming little mouth ; dewy to the kiss, and
furnished with a row of tiny milk-white teeth.
Over and above all this she was, they said, full of
inestimable qualities.
Enticing, was she not—to a parched bush-
fellow, for example—hey ?
* * * *
I have quoted the translation in Heine-
mann’s last-year edition of Maupassant’s
story, with the introduction by Arthur
Symons. 'There is the first place in which I
have profoundly disagreed with Arthur
Symons, the man of verse and prose whom
you know as one of the brightest and most
stimulating,of younger English writers. (I
am beginning to fancy that he has passed
his writer’s prime ; and the verses in the
November Dome certainly lacked his. old-
time freshness and brilliancy—they were
not edged.) Maupassant, to my mind as to
others’, is in view of the quantity and quality
of his work chief among French writers of
the conte —which is a more personal thing
than the short story. There are others
who have written good short stories—Balzac,
for example ; Merimee and (lautier and
About ; Copp6e and Merides and Ricliepin
and Anatole France ; —but no one of
them has written contes so-many and so-good
as Maupassant. Merimee is the doubtful ex-
ception ; and certainly Merimee is fine-
more vibrant, perhaps, than Maupassant,
but less pregnant, less subtle, less seducing.
And I apologise for reminding you that Mau-
passant was “ the pupil of Flaubert” ;
that he fought in ’7l ; that he wrote witli that
assiduity of painstaking art displayed no-
where so much as in France ; and that his
best contes (he wrote romans also, of which
we may talk on another occasion) remain
the admiration and despair of rivals. Add
that Maupassant lived from 1850 to 1893 ;
that he lived a life of nervous exaltation and
stimulated his brain till paralysis swamped
it ; and you have an outline of the author of
half-a-dozen novels, a dozen volumes of
shorter stories, two books of travels, a play
or two, and a book of verse—the where-
withal of Fame.
* * * *
Now, Arthur Symons, criticising this man,
begins badly. He says (and in 1899 !)
The first aim of art, no doubt, is the representa-
tion of things as they are.
But it is obviously nothing of the sort—as
has been many times decided for debaters.
The representation of things as they are is
photography, and art comes in the addition
of the human mind. Yet Symons under-
stands that too, for he adds that “ things
are as our eyes see them and as our minds
make them”—so that it is his language which
is at fault. For there is no profit in confusing
the thing in itself—the object, with the ob-
server’s eye and mind ; and a sentence like—
The soul, for instance, is at heart as real as the
body;
is no less confusing; since its value depends
on the fetichistic term “soul,” which comes
trailing a legacy of misapprehensions behind
it, and, though retaining a vague poetic sig-
nificance, has no critical meaning whatever.
So far as artistic consciousness is concerned,
there is no soul, no heart, no spirit—nothing
but a brain. What the brain does not con-
sciously recognise can never be formulated
in art.
• * * *
This paragraph is better, though it deals
only with half-truths—
A story of Maupassant, more than almost any-
thing else in the world, gives you the impression
of manual dexterity. It is adequately thought out,
but it does not impress you by its thought; it is
clearly seen, but it does not impress you specially
by the fidelity of its detail ; it has just enough of
ordinary human feeling for the limits it has im-
posed on itself. What impresses you is the
extreme ingenuity of its handling ; the way in
which this juggler keeps his billiard-balls har-
moniously rising and falling in the air.
That is much more true of “ Boule de
Suif ’’ than it is true of Maupassant’s short-
stories taken in mass. As a generalisation,
most readers of Maupassant will probably
agree that it is false. All through his
preface, indeed, Symons reasons from the
story which he is prefacing—and unjustly,
since that exhibits only one of Mau-
passant’s methods. Nor is the label of
“manual dexterity ” fitting, since all man-
ual dexterity depends on intellectual
dexterity, cerebral dexterity. Clever fingers
mean nothing else but clever brains special-
■rticular centres which give the
tt adjustments, clean strokes,
idences between what the eye
hand executes. Take a car-
penter or joiner, for example. Every em-
ployer knows that one man will bring two
surfaces of wood together so that the eye
cannot detect their point of union ; another,
do what he will, try as he may, can never
hide a gaping joint. But it is only the effect
of the dexterity which is in the hand ; the
camels in the brain and nerves which con-
trol the hand. And this is none the less true
because some hands are better shaped, for
delicate work than others.
* * * *
Yet, in respect of “ Boule de Suif,”
Symons’s meaning is clear and just. “ Boule
de Suif” is one of Maupassant’s most dex-
terous, most workmanlike stories ; and it is
partly this obvious predominance of dexterity
which makes it less satisfactory on the whole
than others such as “La Maison Tellier,”
where the antithesis which Maupassant
loved as a ground-work of plot has a deeper,
more epic significance. The central incident
of “ Boule de Suif ” would usually be held
prohibited to a story-teller in English ; but
the constant wanderings round the sex-
subject in English fiction, the suggestions
and innuendoes, the concealments and with-
drawals, are infinitely more unhealthy, for
writer and reader than the frank French ac-
ceptance of sex as a cardin il force in life, and
of the manifestations t f sex as manifestations
of life, and consequently legitimate subjects
for an art which takes life for its province.
Maupassant writes of sex-passion, when it
comes to his pen, as naturally as he writes
of the passion of avarice or the lust of battle,
—as naturally as he describes scenery and
the emotions received from scenery, —as
naturally as he deals with a hundred phases
of the human enigma. The central incident
of “ Boule de Suif ” is for him artistic
material in the same fashion as any other
incident which stimulates interest, which
gives to smile or weep or ponder. And
as you say, my dear John, bother this
preface : let me briefly indicate the story.
• * * •
Winter in Normandy, time of the Franco-
Prussian war. The town fear-stricken and
sullen under the domination of the con-
querors. But as time passes, the grip re-
laxes a little, permission is given to travel ;
and one morning a coach draws up in the
courtyard of the Hotel de Normandie, ready
for the long two days’ drive to Dieppe. The
passengers get in—an aristocrat and his wife,
a tradesman and his wife, two Sisters of
Mercy, Cornudet the democrat —and Boule
de Suif. Poor Boule de Suif! The day
freezes her ; hut it is nothing like the frost
of the other ladies. They know themselves
so proper, so virtuous ; and——poor Boule
de Suif ! And through the snow and the
slush the coach creaks on ; but slowly, so
slowly. They were to reach Totes at
dinner-time ; but they are a long way
from Totes.. And alone of them all
Boule de Suif has had the forethought to
bring her lunch—an ample lunch—fowls in
jelly, wine, pastry, fruit ; enough for a long
journey. She begins eating daintily ; and
the others are so hungry. How they hate
her ! The good-hearted girl offers to share
her provisions ; they reject—but it is three
o’clock, their hunger is ferocious, they
accept. And as they cannot well eat the
young woman’s provisions and not speak to
her, they converse—stiffly at first, soon
with less reserve, as good Boule de Suif does
not presume, then amicably. And at last
they reach Totes.
A German officer comes to scrutinise them,
a tall, slender, blonde young man with
moustaches waxed to such a point that you
can’t say where they end. He checks the
names and professions on the waybill, regards
the passengers attentively, passes them—
“ All right.” Then supper—how delightful!
and a fire. Suddenly the innkeeper enters.
“ Mademoiselle Elizabeth Rcnisset? ” That
is Boule de Suif. “The Prussian officer
wishes to speak to you at once.” She will
not go ; the others persuade her ; she goes—
and returns in five minutes, oh ! crimson
with rage. “ The low blackguard ! ” The
others are concerned ; but Boule de Suif
will not explain. They sup ; they go to bed.
In the morning—no horses to the coach.
Perplexity—what ? how ? why ? They seek
the driver. “The Prussian officer ordered
me not to put the horses in.” Why ? how ?
what ?—consternation. They seek the officer.
He will see them after lunch. They find
him in his dressing-gown, with his feet on
the mantelpiece, smoking his long porcelain
pjpe__“a brilliant sample of the victorious
military cad,” says patriot Maupassant.
“ Yat do you vant ? ” “We wish to leave.”
“No.” “ May we take the liberty of asking
why?” “Pecause Ido not shoose ” (in his
Alsatian French) ; “you can co away.”
A miserable afternoon ; everybody grum-
bles, nobody understands. As they sit
down to supper, enter the innkeeper once
more. “The Prussian officer wishes to
know if Mademoiselle Elisabeth Rousset has
changed her mind yet?” “Tell the low
scum —never ! ” And she explains to her
fellow-passengers. They are so shocked—
particularly the ladies : Boule de Suif is a
real heroine.
But a day passes; and another day; and im-
perceptibly the atmosphere changes. “This
girl is keeping us here”—and how much
longer ? At every supper-time the inn-
keeoer with his question. Poor Boule de
Suit’ ! —-ghey conspire against her. They per-
suade, they argue ; the two holy Sisters
recite irrelevant tales of people who have
sacrificed themselves for others, of evil done
that good may come—“ for we know that
God pardons the deed if the motive be
pure.” Poor Boule de Suif ! And the
long, wretched days—and the supper-time
innkeeper. And the incessant pleading and
pressure.
On the fifth day Boule de Suif does not
appear at supper. Poor Boule de Suif !
The others are delighted, joyous; only
Cornudet is found to declare against this
Infamy. Poor Boule de Suif ! In the morn-
ing the horses are in the coach. Poor Boule
de Suif ! The others are waiting for her ;
but they do not recognise her, do not see
ber. She gets in timidly; and the coach
begins the long day to Dieppe.
By-and-by the passengers are hungry.
The aristocrat and his wife unpack a roast
hare ; the tradesman and his wife have some
veal; the holy Sisters have sausages ; Cornu-
det has some hard-boiled eggs. Boule de
Suif has nothing—she has forgotten in the
hurry and confusion. The others munch
away placidly, unheeding her, never looking
at-her. She thinks of the basket full of
things she shared, and the tears come
into her eyes—though she does her best to
keep them back. They gather and fall, fall
. . . and she sits up very straight, her gaze
fixed, her face pale and rigid, hoping nobody
will notice.
But the Countess nudges her husband ;
the tradesman’s wife chuckles ; and the two
Sisters of Mercy resume their devotions after
carefully wrapping up the remnant of their
sausages And Cornudet, has an idea—he
whistles the Marseillaise. “ Patriots ! give
yourselves for your country. Liberty 1 fight
on the side of thy defenders.” Paces cloud;
the passengers become nervous, irritable ;
the air is unpleasing. Cornudet takes no
notice.
And all the way to Dieppe, through the
long dull hours, through the jolting and rat-
tling, through the day and through the even-
ing, and later through the night, Cornudet
whistles, forcing the others to follow the
song front end to end, and to remember
every word that corresponds to each note.
“And Boule de Suif wept on, and at
times a sob which She could not repress broke
out between two couplets in the darkness.”
* * * *
That, my dear John, is a skeleton of the
story, museumed for your curiosity. And
I positively saw Boule de Suif getting
out of the tram at Walker-street, with the
face of a rose and the figure of a dumpling—
poor Boule de Suif !
Some correspondents—maddened by the
thought of the Bookfellow’s Mixture, the
Ideal, the Divinity whose veil hath never
been lifted—puff, and puff, and end, alas ! in
smoke. It is “W.L.8.” who speaks—
I read Frank Morton’s article, and have bought
the ingredients of his Mixture. It sounds good— l
and I have made my will. But the pleasure of
smoking is so subtly individualised,—depends so
upon one’s mood, constitution, ancestors, women-
folk, —that you cannot find a rule to fit
all cases. My own cigar - taste runs to
Flor di Cuba soaked in water, and stored
in the ice - chest until just dry enough to
keep alight. Even among experienced smokers
there are few who appreciate the flavour and
aroma of a green cigar as they deserve. When I
was in Teneriffe, famous for its glorious climate,
more glorious mountain, most glorious green
cigars, there were some who talked of Tene-
ruffians. T was a libel; as every smoker of taste
admitted after a week’s trial.
Yet it is the Pipe which is nearer perfec-
tion. Get a good pipe with two stems ; lend
it to your gardener, say, to Smoke for a month
incessantly; then take it back, change the
stem, and enter the smoker’s Paradise. After
three months, hand it to an engineer or enginfe-
driver and let him blow it out with steam ; scrape
it slightly ; and be a God for another three months.
If properly done, the steaming improves the
colour and polish.
Don’t think I am forgetting cigarettes’: they
have a well-defined rank in the smoker’s service.
Get a box of well-dried Turkish, and smoke one
before a cigar: you will find it the entree, the
appetiser before the meat. Have you ever noticed,
when smoking two pipes consecutively, that you
enjoy the second better than the first? The first
seems somehow too rash, too ill-advised, too
sudden; and you attack it too abruptly, it is gone
before you are aware. The second you savour
slowly, enjoying to the full. It is the same, if
you notice,, with
* « * *
No, W.L.B., —for what would Mrs. Grundy
say ? Besides, you were talking of Tobacco,
and must stick to your topic. “Jack Shay ”
sticks—until near the end, anyway—
There exists, somehow, a widespread delusion
that smoking has of necessity some connection
with tobacco ; and, as a consequence, that a good
smoke can only be had when good tobacco is
obtainable. But: “My Lady Nicotine” —like
many other “ Miladies ” nowadays—is often a
patent fraud, a thing of tricks and shams, of
paint, and powder, and padding, and scent—
pshaw! Your “excellent” Havana or Manila
cigar may be made in Hamburg, or Liverpool, or
Hongkong, or Surry Hills ; and owe its flavour to
the chemist, its beautiful colour or shape to a dyer
or a modeller. Your Aromatic plug, or strong or
mild-flavoured Mixture —may be cabbage-leaves
flavoured with aniseed, or bergamot, or rum, or
sugar, or some odoriferous resin. And cigarettes
—well, Egyptian and Turkish cigarettes are made
in Sydney.
This proves that Beauty is in the eye of the
gazer, the mouth of the smoker —what you think
the best tobacco, that is the best tobacco. The
true smoker, like the true toper or the true artist,
is Catholic in his tastes. Smoking the universal
religion (it is a religion) he follows faithfully. As
Roman Catholics kneel to God, as Nonconformists
stand up, as Mohammedans lie down, so the true
smoker—he pipes, he cigars, he cigarettes—and
his smoke is always the one religion. If he can
afford guinea cigars, he will smoke and praise
smoke: if he can only get a cigarette of news-
paper and dry pine-needles, he will smoke and
praise smoke. He is the true votary. If he
smoke—it is enough. And the cigarette of news-
paper and pine-needles is maybe his highest form
of worship.
But, if you insist on the Best Mixture, take a
man for strength, a woman for delicacy, a few
kisses for flavour, and a cigarette to be puffed
alternately. Two hearts beating as one—how
merely poetic ! Two smokers _ smoking as one—
the submergence of the human in the Divine. . . .
And the coiling wreaths of smoke twist and
turn, vague and indefinite, odourless, flavourless,
almost colourless . . . “Pass, Jack; it’s my
puff! ”
All smoke !
THE BOOKEFELLOW.
Reprints.
EPITAPHIUM CITHARISTRIE.
Stand not uttering sedately
Trite oblivious praise above her !
Rather say you saw her lately
Lightly kissing her last lover.
Whisper not, “ There is a reason
Why we bring her no white blossom ” ;
Since the snowy bloom’s in season
Strow it on her sleepy bosom.
Oh, for it would be a pity
To o’erpraise her or to flout her ;
She was wild, and sweet, and witty—
Let’s not say dull things about her.
VICTOR PLARR.
FORESHADOWINGS.
The mirrored stars lit all the bulrush spears,
And all the flags and broad-leaved lily-isles ;
The ripples shook the stars to golden smiles,
Then smoothed them back to happy golden
spheres.
We rowed, we sang; her voice seemed in mine
ears
An angels, yet with woman’s dearest wiles ;
But shadows fell from gathering cloudy piles,
And ripples shook the stars to fiery tears.
God shaped the shadows like a phantom boat
Where sate her soul and mine in Doom’s attire;
Along the lily-isles I saw it float
Where ripples shook the stars to symbols dire ;
We wept—we kissed—while starry fingers wrote,
And ripples shook the stars to a snake of fire.
THEODORE WATTS.
I hear the drum roll, rub-dub-dub,
And the piccolo’s shrill refrain ;
The boys in blue with hearts so true
Are marching home again.
I hear the drum, but it beats for me
Despair and grief’s tattoo ;
I’d be so glad if our only lad—
Our Jim—poor Jim—marched too.
I hear the tramp—the tramp, tramp, tramp
Of the army marching by;
Brave soldiers all, at their country’s call
They went to tight and die.
Their task is done : with heads erect
They pass there in review ;
Instead of tears I’d give them cheers
If Jim—poor Jim—marched too.
I hear the clank, the clank, clank, clank
Of the swords of the captains gay ;
But my worn eyes rest on the blood-stained crest
Of. a hill far, far away.
They left him there where the weeping winds
Sing dirges faint and few —
They’re home—God’s light ! how grand the sight
If Jim— poor Jim —marched too.
Gkorge Hobart, in “ Baltimore American .”
A SWAN-SONG.
I am glad I have known what it is to be glad,
To dive, and to drift, and to whirl, in the foam,
To hoard the gold suns of the Summers I had,
Brief, beautiful Summers, to idle, to roam,
To preen and to kiss and to build up a home
For my Love, in the reeds, where no trouble could
come.
I am glad I have known what it is to be glad.
I am glad I have known what it is to be strong,
To breast billows, fight winds, bend with foe in
the fray, . _
I am glad I have known what it is to be strong.
I rejoice to have known what it is to be sad,
To lie in the shallows and watch mighty wrong,
To call when my Love answers not through the
spray.
I am glad to have known what it is to be sad.
I am glad that to soar I am given at length.
The arrow that wounded n;e hurls me along ;
I am glad I may sing of the glory of strength ;
I am glad I may sing though I die with my song.
I am glad that'the music shall rise to the Giver
Of all—and that, dying, I fall to the breast of my
river,
My silver-smooth river that taught me this song.

Propinquity.
(AN IDYLL.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
0 Nymphs whose charms arc on the wane,
And maidens who are plain to tee,
To Cupid pray no more in vain,
But reverently bend the knee
To One who dwells in nearer fane,
And will not your appeals disdain l
The strongest God of Love is he,
The kindly god—Propinquity /
A year ago—one little year—
Each afternoon at 4 o’clock,
When Arthur Percy Brown was her®
His presence glorified the Block.
The Block is still the hub of Town
But where is Arthur Percy Brown?
Of all the bank-clerks superfine
Who walk about to ornament
The City, when the sun doth shine,
He was the choicest-looking gentj
And, though his head was like a pear,
He had a most distinguished air.
The fairest Nymphs who come to Town,
With colours gay to cheer the street,
They smiled on Arthur Percy Brown,
But he was cold as widows’ feet—
Ho passed them by with frigid stare,
For all his thoughts were otherwhere.
His dignity was firmly set.
lie held it up as, you may note,
A lady, when the streets are wet,
Holds up a satin petticoat;
With dainty clutch she grabs the skirt,
To keep it from the common dirt.
With Fancy’s eye he saw his Bride
That was to be—a damsel fair,
The heiress of a country-side—
And kept a stainless crest for Her.
He meant to give her fame and rank*
This modest orchid of the l’ank.
But fat directors, full of meat,
For bank-clerks’ dreams have small Regard.
With fl at, remorseless, shovel feet ■
On voung Romance they trample— hard.
And Mr. Arthur Percy Brown
Was sent to dwell at Dingotcwn.
Now Dingotown is in the West —
As wildly West, as West can be—
Its only spots of interest,
They are the local cemet’ry—
Which twice a year by flood is drowned—
The pub’s, the lock-up, and the Pound,
The lone, long street of Dingotown
Is jagged with stumps of primal trees,
An I there walked Arthur Percy Brown,
An exiled Alcibiades.
He walked alone, for he was proud,
And scorned the crude bucolic crowd.
The g rls of Dingotown were good
As girls have any need to be ;
But then, their health it was so rude
To him it seemed ferocity.
Their feet were large ; their hips were wide ;
They filled the view from side to side.
Yet no young man can moon alone,
From day to day, with girls around ;
So Arthur Percy dropped his tone
Exclusive, and a sweetheart found —
A temporary amour he
Had then in mind, had Arthur P.
She was the Belle of Dingotown—
A short, square girl with sleepy eyes
But, somehow, Arthur Percy Brown
At first thought little of his prize.
Her accent made, him shudder—yet
One takes the best that one can get.
So when his work was done, and he
Had all the hours of eve to spare,
The twain walked out in company,
And he made lightsome love to her,
And marked not, when he chucked her chin,
The solid jaw beneath the skiu.
But objects near the vision fill,
When one forgets the things afar }
A jam-tin on the nearest hill
When touched by sunlight seems a star—
And so it was with A. P. Brown
And that shy Belle of Dingotown.
For dny-by-day, as days went by,
And lie forgiot his City art
He looked on her with kindlier eye,
Until, at last—she had his heart.
She had his heart—her grip was grim—
And there was no escape for him.
So, bit by bit, and day by day,
lie did to his surroundings bow ;
His tio was knotted anyway ;
Ilis hair was parted anyhow :
He slouched, and, as the time went on,
He walked like a diprotodon.
A year ago—one little year—
Each afternoon at four o’clock,
When Arthur Percy Brown was here
His presence glorified the Block —
The Block is still the hub of Town—
But where is Arthur Percy Brown ?
In Dingotown is A. P. Brown,
In safe, strong hands of Agnes Neale ;
The bells rang up, the bells rang down,
On Sunday last, their wedding-peal.
The Heiress-Bride—the stainless crest —
Will soon become a Nuptial Jest.
So Nymphs whose charms are on the wane,
And maidens who aire plain to see,
To Cupid pray no more in vain
But seek the God—Propinquity.
To any girl who knows her trade
The nearest can be Dearest made.
CREEVE ROE.

1. Frame your roof of rafters 2ft.
lOin. apart, centre to centre.
2. Provide Galvanised Wire Net-
ting 3ft. wide, 3in. mesh, and
16 gauge, which should be fixed
by nailing the selvage (drawn
straight) to the end rafter from
ridge to eaves with small clout
nails. Then stretch it tight
and nail the other selvage to
the second rafter, after which
secure the ends to ridge and
eaves.
3. When one set is laid with net-
ting lay the first sheet of
'Ruberoid, starting at the top
and allowing 3in. to go over the
ridge on to the other side. Nail
the. Ruberoid permanently with
the nails provided along the
ridge and down the first rafter
leaving the other edge free, the
nails being 2in. apart at points.
4. Provide 3in. x lin, battens and
nail securely to the under-side
of the rafters (about 4ft. apart),
first spreading the rafters (with
wedges) in order to make the
netting perfectly true and firm.

leaving one edge of the Ruberoid free. Do not secure more than one length of netting at a time so you will be able to lean over to nail the free edge of the Kuberoici on tne
7. For vertical walls, set the studs 2ft. lOin apart, and the battens 3ft. apart (centre to centre), sunk flush with the outside of your studs over which the whole of the netting may be
laid at once. Lay your Ruberoid vertically and secure the same as on the roof. _
8. When roofs are not'walled in (as in the case of stack roofs, &c.) ordinary fencing wire may be laced diagonally both ways (three feet apart) on top of the roof, and should he secured
to each rafter with a staple.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 27, 1900.
Westralian Separation.
Since Big John Forrest did his curious
double shuffle over the Federation move-
ment, and Westralia’s clumsy-footed Legis-
lative Council threw out both Federation
schemes and put a large local hoof on the
whole idea of union, it has been evident
that trouble of some kind was impend-
ing in the land of the Sandgropers.
The trouble has since resolved itself
into a defiuite demand on the part
of the Goldfields for separation. They
are tired of big, foolish Backdown John—
John always backs down to the pressure
that is nearest to' him, and the little paro-
chial fragment that cries out at his back-door
in Perth seems larger to him than the
majority that is further off. They are weary
of Forrest’s spiritual nigger - driver,
Hackett, the man who keeps Big John’s
soul in his tail-pocket. They are weary of
the theory that the hub of the universe is at
Bunbury, and that the world revolves round
Harper’s front-door handle. They are
especially weary of the theory that an
elector at Kalgoorlie, merely because
he is a miner, should have only one-
hundredth part the political power of
an elector at Ashburton, because he keeps
a few gaunt bullocks on a patch of sand.
The majority wants to join the Australian
Commonwealth, and help to form a nation
whose whole soul isn't buried in Bunbury ;
and as a small minority, aided by an in-
famously narrow and stupid Parliamentary
system, blocks the road, the majority wants
to go its own way, and leave the minority to
moulder by itself.
m * m •
If there is anything in the alleged rights
of man, in the alleged self - governing
privileges of British subjects, in the alleged
“British fairplay,” in the claim of the
majority to govern, and in all the beautiful
principles in the name whereof Australia is
helping bo bash a small and strictly Pro-
testant nation in South Africa, the case
of the Westralian Goldfields for separa-
tion is too strong to be disputed. Almost
every bad thing that Kruger has
done, or been accused of doing, John
Forrest and his political pimps have
accomplished, and more. Kruger is charged
with having levied a heavy tariff chiefly de-
signed to benefit the old inhabitants at the
expense of the Outlanders ; the John For-
rest Government levied one almost exactly
twice as heavy. The Kruger Government
levied direct taxation on the gold-finders ;
the Forrest Government’s direct taxation
was much heavier. Kruger so exploited
the new industries of the Transvaal that he
raised the public revenue from something
very modest per head to an amount which
“loyal ” papers in Australia speak of with a
shudder ; John Forrest, whose country
also raised a very small revenue per head
before the .gold-finders came, screwed them
till he exacted more per inhabitant than
Kruger ever did. And in the matter of the
suffrage, Paul Kruger was a liberal-minded
old Dopper by comparison „ with John
Forrest, and the old, hard-fighting
veteran compared very favorably with the
flkbby politician who fled for his life
when accidentally prodded with his secre-
tary’s gamp. The man with the gun refused
what was called “ adequate representation ”
td aliens who, by all the laws of nations,
ltyve no claim to any share whatever in the
government of the country. The man with
tlje umbrella-bruise refused it to citizens who
have every right to a full share in the control
of public affairs. Under the Westralian
political system, which reckons that an Out-
sider electorate should have about one-tenth
th[e amount of representation per head which
by an electorate of agricultural
Sandgropers, Kalgoorlie is just about as
effectively disfranchised as Johannesburg
was. The situation may not look quite so
bad, but it is equally bad. If in a House of,
say, 40 members, one-half the population,
representing one distinct set of interests, has
five or even ten members, and the other
half, representing a different set of interests,
has 30 or 35, the former crowd is as certainly
out-voted, and has as little say in the making
of laws, or the control of expenditure, as if
it had no members at all.
• • * •
In the Westralian Constitution there is a
most unusual proviso to the effect that the
British Sovereign reserves the absolute right
to grant Separation to any part or parts of
the province. The Colonial Boundaries Act
of later date declares that no self-governing
dependency shall be cut up without the con-
sent of its own Legislature. But this Act
doesn’t profess to repeal the clause in the
Westralian Constitution above-mentioned.
Moreover, it seems hardly possible that it
can be held to apply to Westralia, for it
would be an unprecedented thing for the
British Government to radically alter the
Constitution of any self-governing province
without consulting it in the matter. Such a
course of action would imply the power to
make any kind of alteration—to abolish one-
man-one-vote, or to wipe out the Assembly
and hand over all power to the Upper
House, or to abrogate Parliament’s con-
trol of the finances, or to patch,
upset or altogether withdraw the Constitu-
tion at pleasure. If the Westralian
Legislature had passed an Act abolishing
the Queen’s power of granting Separation,
and that amending Act had been sent to
England and assented to there in the usual
way, the power of Separation would un-
doubtedly be gone. And then, without
doubt, the Colonial Boundaries Act would
apply to Westralia. As matters stand, it
seems very unlikely that Act will be held to
apply to Westralia. The British Parlia-
ment doesn’t usually put its foot through
the Constitution of a self-governing province
in that off-hand fashion.
* * * *
Therefore there is good and reasonable
cause to believe that the British Government
will hold that the power of granting Separa-
tion still exists, and can be exercised with-
out any reference at all to the Ministry of
John of Bunbury. If it does, the chances
in favor of the Separationists are very great.
The British Government is strongly in
sympathy with the Federation movement,
and the mean and clumsy dodges by which
the Forrest crowd prevented any reference
to the people on that question are not likely
to excite sympathy in Britain. If it is-
possible to strain a point in favour of the
Separationists the chances are that the
British Government will strain it, if only
because the proposed new province is
strongly in favor of joining the Common-
wealth, and separation will help on a move-
ment in which the present Ministry in Lon-
don is understood to take a deep interest.
The vast array of names on the petition will
show that the demand for severance is
almost universal on the Goldfields. Also
an exposition of the Forrest party’s dis-
graceful methods of faking the franchise and
allotting the Parliamentary representation
almost wholly among its own conservative
little crowd will fully explain why this huge
section of the population finds it necessary
to appeal to the British Government for jus-
tice, instead of supplying itself with the
article locally. Moreover, in the interests
of the Commonwealth, the petition will
doubtless have all the advocacy that can be
given it by the Federal delegation which is
about to proceed to England. No doubt the
Forrest Government will indite all the
reasons it can adduce on the other side, but
the Forrest Government is slow of speech
and confused in ideas, and makes pothooks
and hangers on a large sheet of paper in a
laborious and unconvincing fashion. If the
expediency of the proposed change becomes
a matter of argument, with Forrest and
Wittenoom representing one interest and
Edmund Barton and Alfred Deakin the
other, it will be a fight with a coal-shovel as
against a Maxim gun.
* m m *
Forrest and his supporter, Hackett of
the West Australian, both view the Separ-
ation movement with contempt, which is,
perhaps, something in its favor. The dull-
witted Premier and the rather dull-witted
boss of a very dull paper are men
whose capacity for being wrong is
quite remarkable. Even if, as they
too confidently assume, it is decided that
the letter of the law is against the
Separationist party, it by no means follows
(as Hackett and Forrest apparently
imagine) that the matter ends there. The
letter of the law is a'very beautiful thing
printed in nice large type tin nice white
paper with a wide margin, but even the most
beautifully-printed law has often proved to
be only ink and paper after all. If the
present movement failed, and, in the pride
of their large swelled heads and their small
minority, the Forrest party still refused
to give the Goldfields adequate representa-
tion, and refused to refer the Federation
Bill to the people, and insisted that the
province which comprises one-third of Aus-
tralia should still revolve round Bunbury,—
many things might happen. If a represen-
tative Convention, elected by the people of
the would-be seceding districts, intimated to
the British Government that it was pro-
foundly loyal to the Crown, and yearned to
perish, if need be, in the interests of the
empire, but that it would no longer submit
to be practically disfranchised by Forrest s
one-horse oligarchy, it might possibly get the
ear of the British Government in a serious
moment. When the British Government
was fully convinced that the movement was
in no sense directed against it, but solely
against a petty parochial boss, it is very un-
likely that it would risk civil commotion in a
hitherto peaceful and loyal, not to say grovel-
some land, merely to uphold that small
boss’s local supremacy and incapacity. John
Forrest isn’t essential to the empire, or very
useful to it, or even very ornamental in it.
• -* * *
The case of Westralia is not at all like that
of Queensland. In Queensland a small
minority demanded separation, and went the
way of most minorities. In Westralia it
will probably prove to be a case of a majority,
or at least a fair half, of the population de-
manding severance, and a sheet of paper
with a statute printed on it is a weak armour
against a majority if once it moves in dead
earnest. If the British Government is
convinced that the demand for separation
is made in dead earnest, that some-
thing large must needs happen if it isn’t
granted, that the majority is every whit as
loyal as the minority, and that opposition
isn’t a case of defending British interests,
but merely of defending a clumsy and tact-
less politician against the natural results of
his own clumsiness, it may possibly tear up
a good many things in the interests of peace.
It may begin by tearing up Forrest,
which can be done by the simple process
of the British viceroy giving him the sack
and inviting someone who seems blessed
with more horse-sense to form a Ministry.
The “Age’s” Reasons for War.
Just now, as at all times when a big war is in
progress, there is a demand for all kind of litera-
ture, no matter how false or silly, abusing the
enemy. And where there is a demand there is
also a, supply. If circumstances caused a sudden
opening for books scandalising Queen Victoria,
the British and foreign press would probably
teem in like manner with books proving that that
ordinary and perfectly respectable old lady had been
guilty of almost everything. If the demand was
sufficient, books would probably come out proving
that she had been guilty of quite everything. At
present, however, the hunger is for works which
heave the most irrelevant and mixed and remark-
able charges at the Transvaal people and Govern-
ment. The conscience of the people wants to be
salved by assurances that the Boers don’t wash,
that they chew tobacco, that they swindle, that
their piety isn’t genuine, that they are ignorant
and don’t wear their hair the way they should do,
and all manner of things. One Fitzpatrick, who
was imprisoned in the Transvaal for treason,
wherefore he is naturally a perfectly unbiassed
witness, has published a book of this kind, and
Melbourne Age endeavors to crowd the publication
down the public throat in the following fashion.
* * * *
Mr. Fitzpatrick gives an enormous mass of facts and
figures which conclusively prove that President Krugbr’s
treatment of the Outlanders was nothing more patriotic
than an organised system of plunder and blackmail. The
Transvaal employs in its public service none but Boers and
Germans, In 1886, when the immigration of Outlanders
began, the fixed salaries of Government officials amounted
to £51,831. In 1899 their total was no less than £1,216,394,
or 24 times as much, and is equal to five times the whole
revenue in 1886. This sum, if equally distributed, would
give £4O per head per annum to the total Transvaal Boer
population. It must be remembered that nine-tenths of
the revenue thus squandered is paid by the Outlanders.
So with education. In spite of all promises, the actual
facts about the State schools are that in 1896, out of a
total expenditure of £63,000, the sum spent on Outlander
children was £650, or Is. lOd. per head, while £8 6s. Id.
per head was spent on the Dutch children. The foreigners
naturally pay nearly all the revenue of the Netherlands
Railway Company, whose line is fed by those from Cape-
town, Natal, and Delagoa Bay, which is entirely in Ger-
man, Hollander and Boer hands. Its debentures were
issued at £9O 155., when loans on the same interest and
security were quoted at par ; and for whole sections of the
earthworks a Hollander firm was paid £23,500 per mile,
when the market price was under £BOOO a mile. The
financing of this company by the monopolists involves
a loss of £160,000 in interest alone. Yet it
pays 12J per cent, per annum, owing to the
enormous freights levied, for instance, on coal. If the
rates were brought down to three times as much as the
Cape railways charge, the industries of the country, all
in Outlander hands, would be saved £150,000 per annum.
Goods coming on to the Transvaal railways from the
Cape are charged B|d. per ton per mile, more than twice
as much as the rate on goods coming by the other lines.
We already knew that the dynamite monopoly took
£600,000 a year out of the mines and put it into the
pockets of the Dutch concessionaire. Mr. Fitzpatrick
gives chapter and verse to show that everything has been
turned into an instrument for looting the Outlander on
the same colossal scale.
Definite proof is also available now of innumerable in-
stances of wholesale bribery and corruption. Kruger
himself in 1889 accepted a loan of £7OOO from the
Treasury at 21 per cent, interest. In 1892 he had a dam
constructed on his own farm out of State funds at a cost
of £4500, and a road, estimated to cost £l5OO, which ac-
tually cost £5OOO. His son-in-law, Eloff, pegged out some
valueless ground as a mining claim, and when the Volks-
raad refused to confirm the action of the Government in
converting this ground into residential “stands” or
blocks, the Government promptly paid £25,000 to Mr.
Eloff for it as a coolie location. The Standard and
Diggers’ News has been represented in Australia as
a Labor journal. It is interesting to learn that its
proprietor, a Mr. Emmanuel Mendelssohn, was granted
by the Government, over the head of the Johannesburg
municipality, a concession for the sewering of the town,
which this strange advocate of labor boasted was worth
a million sterling, though he paid nothing for it beyond
the political services rendered to Kruger by his paper.
Some of the • contracts given to friends of the oligarchy
are even more astonishing. A Mr. Barend Vorster was
one of the greediest brigands. He got a contract for sup-
plying, donkeys at a certain figure, and procured them in
Ireland and South America at about one-sixth of the con-
tract price. Then, averring that he had no means of his
own, he secured an advance of three-quarters of the con-
tract-money—this is, for every £lOO he really had to pay,
he received an unsecured advance of £450. This ingenious
gentleman indeed touched high-water mark in the con-
cession he secured for the Selati Railway, which em-
powered him to get the line built at £9600 a mile. Within
two days the contract was sublet at £7002, a “clean
steal ” of over half a million, &c., &c.
* * * *
The first thing that is worth noticing in Mr.
Fitzpatrick’s book is that he doesn’t give what
can fairly be called “ definite proof ” of anything.
In most cases he quotes no authority for his state-
ment, but just dumps it down off-hand, as any
other person might dump down the statement that
Australian politicians were given to cannibalism.
In the beginning he (or the Age) is convicted of
absolute- falsehood on his (or its) own figures. If
the Transvaal official salaries were, in 1899,
£1,216,394 that wouldn’t be, if equally distributed,
£4O per Transvaal Boer (there are 70,000 of
them or a little more), but much less than half
that sum. Possibly, the statement about the
amount of the salaries is the same taradiddle as
t he calculation which follows it.
Next there is the horrid accusation (which ap-
pears to be advanced as a reason for the war)
that the Netherlands Railway Co. issued its £IOO
debentures at £9O 15s. Why the price of
Transvaal railway debentures should be an Aus-
tralian grievance isn’t explained, and seeing that
Victoria, the province where the Age is published,
sold its £IOO Government debentures at £9olos. 6d.
net as late as ’92, it ill becomes a Victorian paper
to want the blood of a small State because a
railway co. therein located sold its much worse
securities at a higher price.
■ * * * *
Then the indictment goes on to call, by the
clearest implication, for blood and battle and con-
quest and sudden death, on the ground thatthesame
Transvaal railway co. paid £23,500 per mile for
earthworks on its line when the market price was
only £BOOO. As the only important line which the
Netherlands Co. built (it bought the rest from the
Cape Colony and Natal Governments) struggles
over the great Drakenberg range, it is possibly
rock-blasting and not earthworks that are
meant. Somewhat similar mountain railways built
by good engineers in Peru cost £40,000 a mile ;
and in New South Wales a less formidable rail-
way cost over £30,000 per mile. At all
events Australia is asked to howl with
a vague sense of deep injurv because this far-off
railway co. is alleged to have thus foolishly
scattered its own money. Why Australia should be
lashed into warlike fury against any country because
a railway syndicate therein thus made a fool of
itself with its own money doesn’t appear. But it
is vaguely understood that the swindle was this
The Outlanders were so oppressed by the high
rates charged by the Netherlands Railway Co.
(apparently before it was made) that they urged
the Government to take it over, and the co. there-
fore swelled the price of the line by paying these
exorbitant sums for the making of it, on the off-
chance that the Government would buy it at cost,
and it might thus get its lost cash back. And the
Government, it seems, connived at this fraud so
that, if it took over the line at cost, it would have
to pay more for it. The motives seem a trifle mixed
all round, but the Age intelligence, when it is
howling for Blood, rises superior to mixed motives.
The Bulletin, personally, has no knowledge at
all of Transvaal railway contracts ; and never hav-
ing been in gaol in that country, it has no reason
for getting square with Transvaal officials. But it
refers to the latest edition (issued only a few
months ago) of the well-known “Dictionary of
Statistics,” published in London by Michael G.
Mulhall, a Fellow of the Royal Statistical
Society, and author of many statistical works,
which have been looked upon for years as standard
authorities. _ He alleges (on page 795) that the
Transvaal railways (practically all owned now by
this alleged swindling co.) were built at an aver-
age cost of £IO,OOO per mile, as compared with
£10,200 per mile in Cape Colony, £10,200 per mile
in Australia, £II,OOO in Canada, £12,900 in the
United States, £57,300 per mile in England—in
fact he alleges that among all the countries in the
world (Australasia being taken as a single coun-
try), the only ones which built their lines more
cheaply than the Transvaal were Sweden, Nor-
way, Chili, and Brazil. And either this great and
long-standing statistical authority is a liar or a
fool—or else the whole vague story about the
Transvaal railway swindle fathered by the obscure
Fitzpatrick (whose imprisonment at Pretoria
gives him at least an understandable motive for not
feeling good towards the Boer authorities), and
circulated by the Age as a reason why Australia
should go Boer-prodding with a glad heart, falls
through. There certainty can’t be room for much
swindle at £23,500 per mile if the whole work of
railway construction costs only £IO,OOO per mile.
And it is notable that Fitzpatrick’s own book
says that the entire capital and borrowings of the
Netherlands Railway Co. are £7,000,000, which,
on its rather over 700 miles of line, pans out rather
under the £IO,OOO per mile of Mulhall.
* * * *
There is nothing in Mulhall about Transvaal
railway rates (which may, either be taken for
granted or viewed in the light of Fitzpatrick’s
inaccuracy on other points), nor about how much
profit per cent, the Transvaal railways screw out
of their unfortunate customers. The Age says (as
a reason for the war) that it is the very high
figure of 12£ per cent. Coghlan, in his “Seven
Colonies ” (1898), says that Cape Colony charges
such high rates that it makes over 10 per cent,
interest on its capital. Natal, a British territory
adjacent to the Transvaal, fleeces its people to
such an extent that its railways produce nearly
per cent, profit. There isn’t much difference
between these figures and those of the hated Boer
next door. And, to come nearer home, there is in
N.S.W. one Victorian-owned private railway (at
the Barrier) which wrings such enormous rates out
of the miserable Cornstalk producer that it is able
to pay dividends of 40 per cent.—it has paid as
much as 50 per cent. —per annum. If Australia is
expected to goad itself into a fury against the
Transvaal over that 12J per cent., N.S.W. has
more than three times as good reason to start by
burning Melbourne on account of the Silverton
Tramway Co.’s exactions.
• * » •
As regards the dynamite monopoly The Bul-
letin has no reliable information, and when it
hasn’t good authority either way it doesn’t argue.
Presumably the figures in this matter are at least
as reliable as Mr. Fitzpatrick’s (or the Age’s)
other figures. But it is noticeable that Australia,
after being urged for months and months to loathe
the Boer because of his ignorance and hatred of
education, is now invited to loathe him still worse
because he spends the liberal sum of £8 6s. Id. per
Boer child annually on education, and because the
amount spent on Outlander children (who, as a
rule, don’t go to Boer State schools) is very much
less. And it is expected to. grow frantic over a
number of alleged jobs which, if true, are bad
enough, though they don’t look very much com-
pared to the Vic. railway land resumptions of a
few years ago, and the N.S.W. Darling Island
purchase. When almost every great Australian
landholder gets the State or the local authority to
pay for roads through his property, the fact that
Kruger got one road made through his looks like
amazing modesty. Did David Syme personally
make the roads on his Lilydale property,- and did
any one expect him to ? And when a Tasmanian
(and partly Victorian) railway syndicate is trying
to sell the Great Western Railway concession at a
profit of something like £BOO,OOO, and after the
great Londonderry mining “cat,” this country is
expected to go out in search of gore, because a
Boer sharper got a railway contract at £9600 per
mile and sub-let it at £7OOO. After the sale of the
great ’Derry “cat” for £650,000 that half-million
fraud looks almost humble. In a land where rail-
way earth-works alone are said to be built at a
swindle of £23,500 per mile, he should have done
better.
« * • •
These remarks are doubtless “disloyal” ; in the
present time of hysteria to disprove any anti-Boer
statement is a crime calling for execution. ‘ ‘ British
fair play” is understood to be turned off at the
meter just at present. But even if the foolish and
frantic Melbourne rag, and all other such rags, br


lieve in the justifiableness of the present war to
the bottom of their souls, the policy of lashing
the Australian people to wilder frenzy and bigger
Contingents on the ground that a Transvaal rail-
way co. grinds out nearly one-third as much per
cent, on its capital as a Victorian one does, looks a
trifle ridiculous. The great art of being an ass is
carefully cultivated just now by a vast proportion
of the Australian people. And some of their
leaders, in their yearning to prove themselves
“loyal” by heaving all manner of charges at
Britain’s opponent, seem in danger of cultivating
the great art of being something much worse than
a mere honest ass.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Night in Victoria’s Upper House.
It is becoming increasingly evident that before
any progressive legislation can be accomplished in
Victoria that province’s House of 48 Rich Land-
lords will have to be summarily reformed—if
possible. Tuesday before last the gathering of
Incapables remained seated from 5 to 10 p.m.,
arid a summary of the intervening hours of alleged
debate is painfully interesting. Proceedings
began by the “man with the jaw” (Embling, of
St. Kilda) demanding of J. M. Davies that the
law should empower the common or honorarium
J.P. to order a flogging on summary conviction.
Then Davies, as Solicitor-General, moved the
second reading of the amending Factories Act.
It rained interjections and sneers and snorts for
the duration of Davies’s address. He finished,
and Nathaniel Levi rose up and ponderously
observed that the legislation was altogether too
experimental. The Government should have
listened to the demand of the Chamber of
Manufactures for an inquiry, that Chamber
being entitled to consideration, as its mem-
bers represented from 12,000 to 15,000 employes
—which is perfectly true, in the sense that
the wolf “ represents ” the lamb he has just swal-
lowed, and the snake “ represents ” the rabbit
whom he temporarily includes. Levi further
maundered about the restriction of trade and the
hampering of all freedom in the carrying on of
“ operations ” —which last is a quite new synonym
for sweating. The wearisome nonentity fur-
ther proposed to leave the whole matter for the
Federal Parliament to deal with, and in return for
this concession, he would be willing to extend the
present Act until June, 1901. Other Fats, includ-
ing Gkimwade, Embling, Sachse, Pratt, and
Winter-leving followed in the same strain, urg-
ing that there had been no demand for any exten-
sion of the Act (apparently a demand needs to be
emphasized with a half-brick before they notice it),
that it was quite monstrous and wicked to handi-
cap employers in this fashion, and that such ex-
perimental legislation should always be viewed
with extreme caution. Also, they complained
about the Bill, which has been before Parliament
for many months, being sprung on them
suddenly, .and their being asked to rush it
through without tremendous consideration
and thought. They were conveniently oblivious of
the fact that they had, a night or two pre-
viously, rushed the second Transvaal Contingent
Bill through in half-an-hour with great enthusiasm.
Having got so far they adjourned the matter for a
week; and, being next invited to consider the
Immigration Restriction Bill (for the second time),
they relieved their feelings by throwing that out
into the gutter in great haste. And these men
imagine they are necessary to the good govern-
ment of the province, and that without them and
their portly dreamings the Constitution would be
a wreck.
♦
An Uprising of the Tail.
There is no blushful modesty about the Provincial
Party in these provinces. That little tattered mob
proposes to send representatives to London to
fight against the Commonwealth Bill, and to
demand in the name of the small minority that
the Bill be either rejected or so altered that the
majority won’t know it again. The plea advanced
is that the Federal majority is to be represented
by a delegate in London, and therefore the anti-
Federal scrap should be there also to explain (what
everybody already knows) that the majority’s
views are not the scrap’s views likewise. One
notable advocate of this theory is John Haynes,
M.L.A. (N.S.W.) —the man who looks at a short
distance like a compressed and untidy duplicate of
Edmund Barton —and it seems a pity that
that short and riotous person doesn’t act
up to his alleged opinions. For by the
same process of logic the minority m his
own electorate (the minority which doesn t elect
John) should have a representative m 1 ariiament
a member who would have no vote and no right
to make speeches on his own account, but who
would sit beside John like a shadow or a badilt,
and assure the House every time he spoke, and at
the end of every one of his sentences, that a strong
minority in John’s electorate repudiated the
sentiments just uttered as if they were a serpent.
If the Parochial Party does send an agent to wring
his hands and cry lamentation on Chamberlain s
door-mat that person will be in a curious situation.
He is to be appointed at a public meeting, but as
the public meeting has no authority from those
electors who voted “No” to appoint any one to
represent them, he can only say that he represents
a nail-full (or quarter full) of people in one city.
The Federal delegates are going to England to do
their best to get the Commonwealth Bill passed
with every line intact just as it was accepted by
the majority in five provinces, so all the opposing
delegation can do is to state that it is opposed to
the passing of the Bill which the majority
accepted, and that it thinks the Tail should pre-
vail over the Dog. Probably the only reply which
a hurried British Minister will have time to make
will be something to the effect that, in many other
highly-respectable and well-conducted asylums,
the same view appears to be quite common. The
average British Minister never fully comprehends
the awful dignity of the separate little provinces
of Australasia. When he looks at the totals, and
sees that the vote for the Bill was 377,614, and
the one against it only 141,499, and that all five
provinces accepted, the fact that one province
accepted by a smaller majority than another will
probably strike him as little as would the. fact
that Cow Flat wasn’t quite so enthusiastic as
Cow Gully, or vice versa. And if he has
patience enough to ask the Tail’s delegation
what it wants instead of the accepted Bill
then there will be chaos. For though the
Tail is unanimously against the present Bill,
it is unanimous on nothing else. Some of it wants
permanent Disunion; some of it wants less repre-
sentation for the small provinces in the Federal
Upper House ; some of it wants more; a section
is only troubled about the rivers, and another is
only against the Interstate Commission, and some
of it wants other things, and some of it wants
whisky. The Parochial Delegation promises to
be the most humorous thing of its kind since the
Victorian Tories, in the glorious old days of
champagne and gout, devised the scheme of send-
ing a nigger newspaper-vendor to England to
speak in its interests in opposition to the Berry
delegation. The fact that the colored gentleman
with the astonishing white hat didn’t go after all,
only proves that there was a gleam of horse-sense
at the back of that old frivolity which appears to
be wanting on the present occasion.
Loyalty and Misappropriation.
The present explosion of loyalty has apparently
undermined the honesty of the Victorian Local
Government system beyond repair, as witness this
circular sent out by Echuca Shire Council to the
other municipal bodies of the province :
Shire Hall, Rochester, 12th January, 1900.
Gentlemen,—l have the honor to bring under your
notice the following resolutions adopted by the Echuca
Shire Council at a special meeting, held 11th January,
1900, viz.: —
1. “That this council donates the sum of £3OO to a
fund to provide the expenses of sending a third contin-
gent of bushmen or other troops to South Africa.”.
2. “ That donations be solicited from all British sub-
jects within the shire for the above purpose.”
3. “ That copies of the foregoing resolutions he for-
warded to all municipal councils of Victoria, soliciting
their immediate co-operation in forming a joint municipal
fund, and that the Mayor of Melbourne be requested
to act as treasurer to such fund.”
My council trusts that you will give the matter your
earnest consideration, with a view of making the effort
worthy of the municipal councils of Victoria.
N.B. —The Minister has promised to indemnify councils
voting their funds for this patriotic purpose.—l have, &e.,
H. T. DOWNE, Shire Secretary.
Also this report of municipal proceedings :
At the Deakin shire council Or. Watson moved, and Or.
Day seconded, “ That in the event of a third Contingent
being sent to South Africa from Victoria to assist the
British in the present war, this council, with the approval
of the Governor in Council and the consent of Parliament,
shall vote out of the municipal fund of the shire the sum
of £250 towards the expenses.” A letter was received
from the Echuca shire council stating that the Govern-
ment had undertaken to indemnify councillors voting
funds for this purpose.
The Bulletin has no desire to speak disrespect-
fully of shire councils and other august bodies.
All it desires to say in this matter is that
the shire funds are supplied by the ratepayers
to be used for certain purposes—roads, bridges,
and other local works, payment of interest
on local debts, salaries and wages of
necessary local officers, and similar definite ob-
jects of local utility specified in the Act. The
councillors are elected by the ratepayers under the
Act, to apply their money as the Act authorises,
and in no other way. When the councillors apply
the ratepayers’ funds to help pay for African wars,
it amounts just as much to misappropriation of
the cash as if a benevolent bank cashier stole the
bank’s money to give it to the local hospital. And
when the Temporary Government promises to
pass a retrospective law holding the councillors
harmless in the matter, it ought logically to add a
clause thereto decreeing that any bank official
who peculated some of the deposits and gave them
to the war fund, should also be held to have done
a perfeetly lawful act. The councillors have an
undoubted right to give their own money
to the war fund—a man may give his own
money to any bad purpose. But when
they come to giving other people’s money—
money entrusted to them for quite a different use
—the war fund becomes very much like a receiver
of stolen property. As for the Government which
promises to authorise the misappropriation, the
only cheerful feature about its share of the busi-
ness is that it may not last long enough to effect
its dishonest purpose, and its successors may, per-
haps, have sufficient moral stamina to repudiate
the whole matter, and make the councillors replace
the missing coin out of their own pockets. For
the honesty and decency of Victoria’s municipal
institutions it is to be sincerely hoped that they
will.

The Truth About Victorian Tobacco.
The Victorian tobacco-growing industry appears
to have got down to a point where it is no longer
worth serious discussion as> a smoking question,
inasmuch as the number of acres under cultivation
is only 78, and the total crop of leaf last season
could barely turn the scale at 1901 b. These
figures are ridiculous in themselves from a national
industry point of view, and they look more absurd
in conjunction with the fact that a Government
expert has been drawing £5OO a year for bossing
the tobacco show, and that some of the 1901 b.
were exported to London, the exporters receiving
a threepenny bonus for the encouragement of their
blessed enterprise. The further fact that the
Govt.’s bonus of 3d. per lb. has always exceeded
the average selling price of Victorian tobacco may
be mentioned in parenthesis, although it has
nothing to do with the question whether the
unfragrant Victorian weed is worth growing for
local manufacture and consumption. Seemingly
there can be no doubt that it is not worth growing,
unless some perfectly new way of growing it is
discovered. The Govt, expert (who has just
resigned) was a hopeless expense all the time;
the bonus on exported leaf was a huge
political delusion, arising out of the success
of the bonused butter industry. Melb. Age,
however, has raised a cry of “departmental
mismanagement,” and figures intended to show
that Victorian tobacco is a great _ possi-
bility have been hurled at a sanguine public. The
public are told that in 1881 the country produced
nearly two million pounds weight of tobacco leaf,
and would have gone on increasing its crop but for
the sudden imposition of an excise duty. Un-
doubtedly the excise tax —one shilling per lb.—
served to kill the business sooner than it would other-
wise have perished, but as a matter of fact it had
to go unless the quality improved. If any one could
find out a way of greatly improving the quality,
the industry would go on and prosper despite the
excuse. Tailing that, it must needs die sooner or
later in any case. The Government has hired a
costly expert to grow good tobacco in Victoria,
and he has failed to make any improvement.
Either he was the wrong kind of expert or the
Victorian soil can’t grow tobacco of the kind that
any respectable white citizen desires to put in his
pipe.
Snakes.
The great dailies on the South African war are
calculated to make one tired. No matter what
happens at the front it all works for the good of
such as publish daily papers. When a few hun-
dred British soldiers, operating in a strange
locality, are cut off and made prisoners, the heavy
organs issue huge placards of blood-red, splashed
over with horrible gouts of black, that shout and
yell at the startled observers “A Grave Situa-
tion,” “The Tide of Disaster,” &c., &c. Then,
when Canada and all the Australian reared up and
between them sent as many men to the war as
Sydney Labor Bureau used to send in one week to
the sand-shifting job, the S. M. Herald spoke, m
deep black tones with the bell-topper of gravity
upon them about “the Empire’s Call to Arms,
and Sydney D.T., drunk with fury, and picking
red lancers off its sleeves, talked about “Stand-
ing by the Empire.” It’s disgusting in its
hysterical Frenchiness. The whole of Greater
Britain and New Britannia (excepting India)
and all the rest of it have not sent
enough men to serve as food for the cannon
in a week’s moderate fighting, and their
actual appreciable difference is that of a bucketful
in the ocean ; and already the Australian dailies
are talking as though we had saved the Empire.
Saved the old man’s life by sending him a pound
of beefsteak for his eye after the Boer had got one
home on it. It is the wild enthusiasm and perki-
ness of the small boy who has got into trousers for
the first time—and when they come back it will be
the exultation of the youth who has fired a gun
for the first time and killed something. The yell
about the Empire’s danger gives an excuse for
much whoop over the loyal Australians who have
“saved” the reeling titan. But, as a matter of
fact, the Empire was never in any danger, and
the “ Grave Situation ” is merely another snake in
this excited country’s boots. Various gentlemen
in long coats and tall hats have said silly things
about the moral effect of the Australian contin-
gents —how they will impress Europe and teach
certain bloated military powers to keep their
military aspirations in their pockets. It looks
much more like a horribly bad advertisement for
the military resources of these provinces. The
big military powers who can heave half-a-million
of men on to any of their frontiers in a fortnight,
must be much impressed with the military un-
readiness of the great self-governing country
which has taken about three months to raise a
force of 3000 men, including camp followers and
cooks and all sorts. They probably reckoned on
a population basis that Australia was able to put
in the field at least 200,000 or 300,000 men, but
when it takes such a time to get such a handful
together, it looks as though we had practically no
military organisation. And we haven’t.

“ Paunch ” on Turner.
Melbourne Paunch writes George Turner’s
political obituary:
Statesmanship is practically unknown in the Assembly.
George Turner, who is apparently the best available man,
is nothing of a statesman. His best achievement during
his term of office, and the one upon which he prides him-
self most—the putting in order of the national finances
after the boom collapse—was not accomplished by any
show of the genius of statecraft, but by a somewhat
miserable and narrow policy of cheeseparing frugality.
He simply cut and saved, and was enabled in due time to
show a surplus in place of the customary deficit. This is
not statesmanship ; it is merely the commonplace tactics
of the politician. He worked hard, and receives much
credit for perspiring coatless over his papers in the dog
days. This, again, is only the policy of the conscientious
politician. The statesman does his best when he sets
others working, and sees that their work is excel-
lent and on generous lines. The Turner policy
squared the ledger, but at what expense ? It
resulted in an enormous loss of population to the country.
The statesman would not save as the ordinary acquisitive
housewife does, but would have put the country on its
legs by a brave policy of development. He would have
saved Where saving entailed no risk of stagnation, and
would have spent liberally where expenditure meant
staying and strengthening the country ; but we have no
statesmen left, and the times and the manners do not
seem favorable to their growth.
This is the reception which a decently-honest
politician always gets in Australia. Every paper
says, in a perfunctory sort of way, that excessive
borrowing is bad and should be put down. And
when the very rare honest politician asks for more
taxation so that he may reduce borrowing, he is
told with a ;shriek of horror that this alleged
.great, rich and lightly-taxed country can’t stand
vany more burdens. And then he is cursed for
not having a fine, large, broad policy of
development which means a fine, large
-broad policy of shovelling out money. Melbourne
' Paunch howled for years at Turner for not having
a fine, large, broad, statesmanlike policy—though
it' never defined that policy, for the sufficient
reason that it never had the faintest idea what it
meant, unless it was a return to the Gillies
system of huge loans and huge booms. Every
Premier is told, in the papers, that lie should
economise, and when he does it he is called a
cheeseparer and a small shopkeeper. Also, wher-
ever he economises he is told that he is economising
in the wrong place and showing himself a narrow-
minded pedlar. After all, Australia gets about as
good politicians as it deserves —winch is saying a
great deal.
Waxellenoy John Madden’s appeal to Vic-
torians for a “Bushmen’s Corps ” subscription is a
warm, flattering sermon with some truth and much
nonsense in it. Says the dapper barracker
We can easily understand what a hopeless, impossible
business this South African country must appear to a
cavalryman trained in a settled country like England, so
ar as keeping touch with a wily foe familiar with all its
leatures is concerned. To our stockmen —cool-headed,
self-contained men of the woods and plains, prepared to
set off for a three or four days’ ride, with no encumbrance
that their easy, roomy saddles will not carry —
to whom it has been an instinct from their childhood to
muster up war rivals, to dodge “ duffers,” and many times
a year to gather in from vast pathless and untrodden
woods and forests and mountains herds as wild as savage
animals, it would be an easy 'piece of business to round up
big bodies of Boers.
The superiority of the resourceful Australian bush-
man over the mechanical horse-soldier for purposes
of scouting and guerilla warfare is probable enough.
For that matter any mounted adventurer
who has had only a little up-country experience
knows enough to give many points to a dragoon or
hussar person on the Boer-track. But Madden’s
wisdom seems to terminate at the suggestion that
a Bushmen Corps would be a boon to the British
army in South Africa. When he talks about
woods and forests and “ rounding up ” Boers as
though they were wild cattle Madden is a dan-
gerous delusive enthusiast. There are no forests in
the South African war case, neither does the Boer
go browsing around without a rifle. Moreover the
Boer shoots villainously straight as a rule, and is
himself a remarkable adept at rounding-up the
enemy when he isn’t employed in forming an un-
cattlelike ambuscade. As a boon for the Imperial
Forces, a Bushmen Corps mustn’t expect to go
quite as far as the full limit of John Madden’s
imagination. It happened that on the day when
his Waxellency’s appeal was published, there
came the news of how 16 smart and wary Aus-
tralian troopers fell unexpectedly among Boers
who were waiting to be “rounded-up.” Some of
them, alas ! fell dead, or wounded ; two of them
got away, which was probably better than British
cavalrymen would have done under the circum-
stances, and the others were rounded-up them-
selves.
Seeing tlie Contingent Off.
[Fob Tub Bulletin.]
At 2.15, or thereabout, the writer made one of a
mighty mob in the glaring sun and hurtling dust
that awaited the emergence of the pageant from
the Paddington Barracks. The heat was intense.
Trams rumbled and ’buses rattled to and from the
city. Crowds of noisy hoodlums in coster carts
blundered through the packed multitude. Cabs
plied eagerly for fares. Anofficerpersonina bottle-
green uniform backed his charger remorselessly into
groups of women and children. Men smoked and
swore and spat and sweltered in the sun. At last,
a small urchin on a cross-arm of a lamp-post
yelped, with reckless disregard of truth, ’Ere
they come 1 ” There was a mighty surge forward.
The galvanized roofs of over-pathway shops
groaned with an extra pressure of heavy-booted
umans, and the Austral-British person rasped a
dusty cheer. It was a false alarm; but it gave the
officer in bottle-green an opportunity of riding
down a boy or two. The mob fell back with sun-
scorched impatience, and the domain dosser who
had adventured from his lair swore several
vehement prayers at the toe of his broken boot. _
Eventually, the helmets of some mounted police
loomed in the gate archway. They always employ
mounted police for gubernatorial functions in
sunny New South Wales. It keeps up the tradi-
tion of its past, when His Excellency was. the
province’s chief gaoler. Forth came the “bobbies,”
white and blue and black, shiny and neat as a
French-folded serviette. They were, followed by
the bottle-green men of the Australian Horse.
Next came the Governor’s barouche, with his
Excellent Nibs in the uniform of honorary colonel
of that regiment. And well he looked as holiday
soldier. His more business-looking aide wore the
blastiferous bearskin of the Grenadiers. The cor-
tege was brought up by the rear escort of the
bottle-green troopers—and then followed sixteen
bars rest. More heat, more dust, more profanity,
more smoking and spitting and sweating, more
trams, ’buses, carts, cabs, and ribaldry of coster
hoodlums. A woman fainted, and was thought-
fully carried by her friends into the chemist shop
of the ill-conditioned —a close-by “ pub.”
The boy on the cross-arm of the lamp-post again
yelped, “’Ere they are!” But no one paid
attention. They had been “ had.” But they were
there this time. A large policeman, with an
important grey moustache, bustled about the
crowd and besought people to stand back “like a
good chap.” There are some decent “bobbies”!
The officer person in bottle-green was equinely
offensive, and one girl publicly thanked God that
she didn’t wear nines in boots !
• • * •
Police troopers headed the procession. Then
came the Lancer half-squadron with fluttering
pennons, and some confoundedly hunched-up,
slouchy horsemen. The Lancers’ band, on the
white horses that gave away an old-time acquaint-
ance with a bankrupt circus, followed. Next the
Contingent—the Mounted Infantry unit leading
the van, succeeded in order by the Australian
Horse squadron, the Army Medical Corps, the
Nurses, a Cape-bound detachment of Lancers, the
Premier and the Cow-shaking Ministry, Colonel
French and his staff, more band, and the Queens-
land Contingent. There was a deal of military
rabble after this, which doesn’t signify; for it
didn’t go away to bleed for its what d’ye-call-it,
and eat half-pound tins of Queen-donated choco-
late.
What were the men of the Contingent honestly
like ? Well, there is precious little good specialis-
ing. They were pretty well alike —deuced unsol-
dierly. There were some fine, smart, well-set-up,
military-looking men among them, of course.
These were Boys of the Old Brigade. They had
seen service. Some of them wore one, two, and
three medal-ribbons apiece. They marched well,
and held their heads erect. Then a practised eye
could pick out the erstwhile policeman or Jack
Tar, neither of whom shaped badly in khaki and
putties. But there we e a lot of slouchers in the
ranks; a few grizzled old fellows of apparent
toughness; many mere boys, immature and already
fagged with their light camp-training. Some tall
young men were loose-hung in the limbs, flat-
chested and round-shouldered. Others, at the
hour of setting forth, shambled along bear fashion,
already overcome with the heat and the weight of
their accoutrements. A few were evidently under
“ the influence.” Taking it all round the pageant
walked in every way under the sun.
• • » •
People swarmed, like ants, everywhere: on
verandahs, up lamp-posts, in carts, on ’bus-tops,
in the branches of trees, in the openings of win-
dows, on the very roofs of the houses along the
line of march. Shouts, cheers, hoarse and dust-
strangled, throttled the martial strains of the
bands. Greetings, brutal and cheerful, and gory
and assorted, were flung at the passing troops.
One little ghoul shouted with glee in his baby-
yelp, like a wolf-cub eager for gore, “ Go it, boys!
Slaughter old Kruger. Give’em steel!”: There
was humor, too, in the comments of the crowd.
“There goes Billy So-and-so; I’ll never see that
five bob again I lent him !” “Look, mother,
there’s our milk-boy in a mustard helmet! ” Pass
then a few men of the Australian Horse, in their
freen tunics and breeches. A stout lady from
Iriu, with enthusiasm, said, “God bless
Ode to the Fat Man.
They sing of the pride of battle,
They sing of the Dogs of War,
Of the men that are slain like cattle
On African soil afar.
They sing of the gallant legions
A-bearm’ the battle’s brunt
Out in them torrid regions
A-fightin’ the foe in front.
They sing of Mauser and Maxim
And their doin’s across the foam,
But I hear none sing of the Fat Man
Who sits at his ease at home
Contrivin’ another measure
For scoopin’ a lump o’ tin,
New coffers to hoard the treasure
That his brothers’ blood sweeps in.
Chockfull o’ zeal for speedin’
The sword of his Queen’s behest,
But other men’s legs to bear it
Is the notion that suits him best.
Nothin’ he knows of fightin’,
He never was built that way;
But the game of War is excitin’
When the stake’s worth more than the play.
An’ a fat little time is cornin’,
When the turmoil has settled down,
An’ the Dogs of War are silent,
And the veldt is bare an’ brown ;
When the sun has licked the blood up
An’ the brown earth hid the bones,
His miners will go out seekin’
For gold and precious stones.
Like a ghoul from the reekin’ shambles
He grubs out his filthy pelf,
Reapin’ a cussed harvest
Where he dursn’t have sown himself.
Now, this is one man-’s opinion,
An’ I think it is fair an’ right:
If he wants the land of the Dutchman
Let him go like a man an’ fight.
If the African mines have treasure,
An’ the Fat Man wants a bone,
Let him go by himself an’ find it,
Let him trek for the Front alone.

English have murthered, and now, God
save and bless ’em, they’re goin’ to fight tlie
battles for the Queen!” And tlie panting,
struggling, sweating soldiery shambled past with
<i shouted salutation to a face caug’ht in tho crowd
with the tail of the eye, and an occasional ejacula-
tion, “My Christ ! It’s hot ! ” with other trim-
mings. All the time the blare of brass, and inter-
minable “Say, Außevoir,” “ The Soldiers of tho
Queen,” “The Sons of the Sea,” “Tommy
Atkins,” and other strains of music-lialldom.
Strings of dirty bunting lined tlie route—the
wretchedest display imaginable. Bottles of Ixier
were consumed in the ranks, and fresh bottles,
flasks of spirits, and cigars were handed to the
“boys” by friends in the crowd’.
By the time the Post Office was reached the
procession had become a rout. Women joined the
men in the marching column. Some favored sons
of Mars had two, three, and four girls hanging
on their arms, and to the “D.’s” in their
shoulder-belts. Flowers were blooming from
rifle-muzzles, and were stuck into helmets. Wives
and sweethearts and babies were escorted along by
the departing warriors—also, fourpenny Lizzies,
with last year’s beer stains discoloring their
bodices. Civilians tramped cheek by jowl with
the 1 lops—some supporting the already overcome
heroes, some plying their mates with hop beer,
some carrying the men’s rifles. Altogether a
scene of Pandemonic confusion.
The Queensland troops were great favorites.
When the bold Bananalanders had readied tlie
Bacchanal stage of the procession, they began
seizing and kissing everv pretty girl near enough
to clutch. And so the throng rushed down to the
place of embarkation. The march began decently,
but in its latter moments it wits just as orderly as
the downhill rush of the Gadarene swine, and the
evil spirit of Hot Beer was in it, and it knew not
where it went, or why.
