Town Hall. Maud MaeCarthy Ooncert(Jan. 30)
The nice girl-soldiers at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) still wage unsanguinary war against the
Volf-Boers in the bright yellow fortress; tho
tallest and handsomest of the nice girls being put
in the New Soutli Wales Lancers—possibly to
indicate that the Cornstalk country is holding up
the biggest end of the campaign. Every theatrical
manager has an impression that this groat country
will stand any amount of cracking up, which is a
mistake —it will stand only a certain amount.
The Australian knows quite well that he is twice
as good as a Britisher, but when he is told that he
is three times as good lie perceives it is an
exaggeration. The coin which spins round the
pale slippers of Dorothy Vane comes down in a
rather unenthusiastic torrent now as if it knew it
had to come, but didn’t want any fuss made about
it. A modern Pantomime is one of the most
mentally confusing sights in creation ; it ought to
be done in private like an execution. It tries to
be a great many things, and among them all it
succeeds in being nothing at all except a very large
picture. It has strayed far from the right path,
but some day it will return like the Prodigal Son,
clothed and in its right mind (if it wasn’t the
Prodigal Son who was referred to as clothed it is of
no importance). Tlie heavy work done by quite
small children and the abolishment of the clown
and pantaloon are features of the modern Panto-
mime. Once the Pantomime was a place where
children went to be amused by grown people ; now
it is a place where grown people go to be enter-
tained by children.
** * *
“Tess of the D’Urbervilles ” is described
by the local authorities as not so grimy as was
expected. This scribe found it grimier, because
she had not expected it to lie as grimy as she
expected it to be. Which is to say, that when a
novel like “Tess” is put on a nice, clean stage,
all the alluviai deposit is generally washed off it,
and it is made utterly false and unreal ; whereas,
in this ease, only half the moral loam is so dis-
posed of, and realism isn't wholly sacrificed. The
early century agricultural laborers who slouch on
to the hay-strewn boards are not as dirty and
disreputable as they were in real life, because the
shock would be too much. Similarly the dialect
isn’t as much so as in real life, because, if it were,
it might as well be gibberish or monkey. But
what realism has beon preserved gives tone to the
play. It is Art, and Art isn’t necessarily the same
thing as soap. The unbathod and unbuttoned
look of Linda Raymond, and her slab of black hair,
and draggling skirts, and dreadful I louts are as re-
freshing to the tired theatre-goer as the same thing
in real life is dispiriting ; and the only objection
to her is her weird habit of salaaming with
outspread fingers when she kneels before
Tess in the last act. Tess herself continues
to bo the same spotless heroine as be-
fore. There is too little of the peasant about
lier. She doesn’t impress you as ever having been
the little girl who went about with “ her hair in
pothooks and ladderlike holes at the knees.” If
the result of having a past is f o make a girl talk
and think hotter (and doesn’t Hardy hint as
much ?) then it is a dangerous moral to put before
any country. The tragedy of Teas consists in the
fact that Angel Clare in critical moments of her
life always manages to be too late. In the first
instance he is just three years too late in becoming
acquainted with Tess at all. Haring decided to
forgive her for liis own want of punctuality, he
contrives to delay said forgiveness just a month
or so too long, and Tess lias gone off the
straight once more. He is so impressed by
this that lie resolves to be only a little late next
time; consequently but a few minutes elapse
between his departure and re-appearance. Never-
theless which is just his luck—Tess has contrived
to put an effectual barrier between them even in
this timo, and Angel is too late again. It is a
solemn lesson on the evils of unpunctuality, and
the rising generation ought to be taken to “ Tess”
in shoals. There is a rumor that “Trilby” will
be revived after “Tess.” “ Trilby ”is a piny that
took the heart of this great country by storm some
years ago, partly because the chief lady wandered
alxnit in bare feet. A larger quantity of bare feet,
with some ankle thrown in, will be necessary this
time. Feet are like laudanum. As you get used
to them the dose must be increased.
** * *
“ The Bells ” have stopped ringing at
Sydney Criterion, and when next unpacked their
jangle will sound in the large serious ears of
Maoriland that blessed province where the
Female Franchise and tlie Early Closing Bill and
other legislative trifles at present agitating Aus-
tralia are now ancient history. “Tlie Bolls” is a
clever play, and the nightmare is a good sort of
nightmare, but the days of the drama with the
horrid dream in it are generally short nowadays.
Life is pretty nearly all a horrid dream just now,
for various reasons.
** * *
“The Power and the Glory” still holds
out at tlie Lyceum, and the blind child continues
to direct love-scenes from her deathbed within a
minute or so of her ascent among the angels. This
youthful actress is just at the awkward age when
she is too old to win commendation for the mere
fact that she is able to toddle on the stage and say
something, and yet much too young to be judged
as an adult. Nevertheless, she contrives to invest
the wooden character of the stage blind child with
a little saving humor, and deserves a better fate
than to be afterwards found tottering on a cloud
late at night.
** * *
On Saturday night a large crowd surged
into the Criterion to greet the Kickards co. on its
opening. When the purple curtain vanished aloft
(the advertisement horror was not in evidence),
the packed house saw a far-off range of hills
covered with dull blue shadows, their bases draped
in twilight. In the valley beneath, terraces of sky-
scraping houses with long, sharp gables and gleam-
ing windows. Upwards from them the landscape
swells to a little leafy clearing on the ridge. Alto-
gether, it is a pretty scene. Unfortunately the
freshness is somewhat marred by Irving Sayles
introducing a very stale joke of his, which he has
carried round so long that some night it will break
with a loud hiss. When little Fanny Powers
has finished her turn the dark “Komic King”
rises to his feet and makes a horrid row
to the effect that he wants the infant called back
to present her with a pound. And Mr. Lobengula
Pope is understood to allege that he hasn’t the
price of a pound of chops. On Saturday night,
Miss Noni Kickards made her first appearance on
the vaudeville stage, and sang softly of the youth
who went away to shoot Boers because “ Some-
body has to go to the Front.” And when she sang
of how his spirit stayed there to keep ghostly
watch over tlie land he helped to prig, the
Tivolians took it kindly, and sundry baskets of
flowers were passed up, and Miss Noni came out
several times and sang some more. Harry
Rickards treated himself to a reappearance with
his hair plastered down on each side of liis face
nice and shiny. When he smiled, it looked
like the spirit of Johannesburg perishing
amidst a desert of white shirt-front. He
sang about things that happen in the
pale moonlight. Then he reappeared and repaid
liis gorgeous welcome by inflicting on the audience
a wearisome old chestnut about An Absent-
minded Beg-GAR. The little Smith Sisters and
Baby Powers gathered the collection into Harry’s
straw cady. The cady is large and deep, and it
was full of filthy lucre, which Rickards is
giving to the Bushmen’s Contingent - fund.
A weird - looking figure billed as Tod Callo-
way got some recalls. The Newsky family
seems to be pulling the public leg. No matter
whether the 'Newskys appear as Russian pea-
sants, Cossack notables, or Moscow dignitaries
they always wear the same red boots, and tlie
Czar of the co. has his covered with gilt filigree.
And though red boots may be worn exclusively and
indiscriminately in Russia, it would not be a bad
idea to introduce some blue ones with silver fili-
gree work : variety is always charming. Still, the
Newsky song about -ski covers a multitude of
small sins like that.
** * #
A melancholy addition to the brief list of
Melbourne shows was made by the Vic. Artists’
Society last week, at its white elephantine gallery
in Albert-street. The present annual exhibition
includes 130 paintings in oil and water colors,
some of the works being ancient acquaintances
which can offer no particular excuse for their reap-
pearance. They came, and were seen of yore, and
they didn’t conquer* and now they are found
hanging around the same old place merely to keep
the walls warm. Next year the proportion of
young untried pictures will probably be much
smaller, and then it may be advisable to bill the
show as the V. A.S. Exhibition of Hardy Annuals.
The members of the Council have done no good
thus far by taking their Society seriously, so they
might as well try the effect of this poor little joke,
in conjunction with a brass band and a Punch-and-
Judy. There are more ways than one of attracting
the public to an art gallery.
* * » *
The striking pictures (among those now
exhibited for the first, or nearly the first, time)
are too few to be worth detailing. Arthur Boyd,
A. H. Fullwood, Walter Withers, and two or
three other honest workers, are the names signed
to the best of them. About 50 percent, of tho
balance were sent in by ladies, whose productions
range from clever to shocking, but inasmuch us
the shockingost lady artist is quite likely to bo the
nicest girl for domestic purposes, all their sins are
herewith lumped together and forgiven. Talking
of sins, the V.A.S.’s annual display introduces a
Persian Prayer Carpet, which came from the
East, via a Melbourne drapery shop, to lie rattled
for at one guinea per ticket. Having gone the
round of the Exhibition, one is at liberty to kneel
upon the prayer carpet and say Grace, or some-
thing.
#* * *
Melbourne’s two surviving theatres con-
tinue to divide the spoil. “Little Christopher,”
at the Koval, is now m her fifth week, and the end
of Silas and Mi’s. Block, and O‘Hooligan, and
Miss Guinevere, likewise the various male imper-
sonators who sometimes disguise themselves in
their true sex, and vice versa, seems to be afar off.
The performance could be improved by a ten
minutes curtailment of the first act, and a new
joke or two in the second act; but, seeing that
the house gets pretty well crowded every night,
this suggestion is probably uncalled for. The
“Little Christopher” matinee trill be given on
Friday this week.
#* * *
At the Bijou the departure of three plump
Maearte Sisters is mourned in moderation. Tom
Costello tells how he met his own original doom
before the altar of Trinity Church ; also, he fights
for “Motherland,” but the patriotism of Thomas
seems less real than the sorrow of the doom-
meeter. Spry and Austin frivol as before, and
the cardboard cow with a crumpled horn is milked
from a syphon every evening. And the gloomy
ballad of the Absent Minded Beggar goes rolling
on, encouraged by the nimble shilling, the
cautious sixpence and the threepenny which was
intended by Providence for a church-collection
next Sunday. About £7O altogether had been
thrown upon the Bijou stage up to time of
writing, mostly for the sake of Tommy Atkins
and partly for the sake of throwing it. In not a
few cases the Beggar’s Fund has benefited by the
violent donation of some young blackguard or
other who was simply aiming his bits of silver at
the Itickards Company with a possible view to
blackening its eye.
* at * *
On Thursday (30th), a farewell concert to
Maud MaeCarthy trill happen at Melb. Town
Hall, and on this occasion the city trill be ex-
pected to make amends for its previous coldness.
Maud MaeCarthy is already a wonderful violinist,
in all ways but one. People who lost their heads
over a mediocre performer like Kruse, and were
“carried away” by the comparatively weak
fiddling of Jakoff Hambourg, ought not to find
any difficulty in raising a profound admiration for
the young Australian Neruda. Meanwhile, they
must make up their minds to hear Miss Maud
before she goes where glory waits her.

The Melb. Alexandra opens on Friday for
a season of two consecutive nights, beginning and
ending with “Shamus O’Brien” on a scale of
fleeting splendor.
Captain Heinsworth, a British ex-cavalry
officer who has seen much active service, is, The
Bulletin is informed, at present slowly dying of
cancer which developed in an old sabre-cut; and,
as the unfortunate gentleman is also penniless
and helpless, something of the present “patriotic”
fervor might well be diverted towards the relieving
of the old soldier’s necessities. The address is c/o
Mrs. Josephs, 45 Argyle-street, city.
The late A. G. Taylor, of Mudgee
(N.S.W.), in addition to possessing many tine per-
sonal qualities, was a clever journalist, and a fine
orator, and a political Ishmaelite. As a destruc-
tive critic he had few rivals, but in a constructive
sense he was wholly wanting. Possibly he would
have developed the constructive faculty in course
of time, but his career was unfortunately cut short.
It is so much easier to be brilliant in showing that
some old institution requires to bo destroyed with
an axe, root and branch, than in patiently ex-
plaining the details of the new institution which
should be built up in itsstead, that destructionists
are to constructive men as ten to one. Yet the
patient, dull, and often bald-headed and uninter-
esting little man who can explain what really
ought to be done is worth a thousand long-haired
geniuses who can tell in burning language that
something ought to be hit on the head and utterly
demolished, and somebody should put something
in its place.
Hysterical N.S.W. War - Minister See,
apropos “disloyal” civil servants: “Every man
who lives on Queen Victoria’s money ought to be
a loyal subject of Queen Victoria.” But Queen
Victoria never had any money not raised from the
British taxpayers. N.S.W. civil servants don’t
live on Queen Victoria’s money ; they live on Aus-
tralia’s money—money raised from N.S.W. tax-
payers, or money borrowed on N.S.W. taxpayers’
credit. Queen Victoria doesn’t pay one of them a
single copper; she never did. Every man who
lives on Australia’s money ought to be a loyal
subject of Australia, and oppose the squandering
of Australian resources in a disgraceful foreign
war. The public servant who lives on Queen
Victoria’s money certainly should be loyal to
Queen Victoria, and the public servant who lives
on England’s money should be loyal to England.
But as there is no such man in the N.S.W. Public
Service—well, what kind of an exhibition is
Minister See making of himself? And isn’t
“loyalty” rather undermining the intelligence of
the Cow-Shaking Ministry ?

COLUMN
Westralia’s Loan Bill this year is for a compara-
tive trifle of £7SO,(XX). Comparing this with the fearsome
I.oan JJill just passed by Queensland, it looks as if the
latter province was goinj; u> take the place so lon»- occu-
pied by Westrnlia as the boss loan spendthrift of Aus-
tralasia. It must be admitted, too, that in one respect
John Forrest’s Government, despite its enormously lavish
scattering of borrowed money, and its bad use thereof
has been a trifle more respectable than some. For in-
stance, in the seven years 1891-97 it spent £5,268 307 of
borrowed funds, but of this £4,050,356 was for"railways
ami telegraphs—both distinctly reproductive works The
fact that nearly one-fourth of the money was spent on
non-productive works which should have been charged to
revenue is awful enough. Still, in N.S.W. and Tasmania
things are worse than that.
* » * *
Plainly the Victorian Government trill shortly
have to find some new way of raising funds for its Slate
Land Bank to lend out on mortgage. In 1599 the Hank
borrowed £235,450, of which llie public subscribed only
£22,150, and the Savings Bank supplied the oilier
£213,300. Up to now the State Land Bank has sold
£918,925 of bonds, the puhlic taking £164,200, and the
Savings Bank buying £754,725. The Savings Bank can't
fill itself up with these not-immediately-realisable securi-
ties indefinitely. The explanation seems to be that 3 per
cent, is too little interest. At 3j per cent, the scheme
would probably work well enough. It is the old trouble.
Barring one infinitesimally Small 3 per cent, loan of
£50,000 which sold at par, Victoria has never got an
English loan at less than £3 7s. 4d. per cent., with
management expenses and cost of remitting interest
added ; yet when it floats a loan locally it demands that
the Australian investor, being an inferior animal, shall
be content with 3 per cent. And he isn’t. Why should
he be ?
* * * *
There is a common delusion that all loan ex-
penditure on railways is reproductive, also that only new
rolling-stock is charged to loans and when it wears out it
is replaced out of earnings. Those who imagine this
don’t know the full glories of the great loan fraud. Vic-
toria has spent in all £1,956,212 of borrowed money on the
purchase of railway locomotives, and all the locomotives
it now possesses are only worth £1,322,900.
* * * *
Waddell, M.L.A., lately appointed a trustee of
N.S.W. Savings Bank (without salary), is working hard to
extend the scope of the hank’s operations with a view to
lending money to fanners and'graziers at a lower interest
than private banks charge. But what is the matter with
the long-deferred State Bank '? That would do, under
safe conditions and expert advice, what Waddell wants to
do under certain disadvantages.
• • » »
Vic. news agents licensed to sell stamps are
appealing ,to Postmaster-General Watt for a reasonable
commission on the business. The com. at present allowed
is only 4d. in the £, and the vendor at this rate may have
to serve two or three or four hundred customers in the
course of earning eighteen-pence. Inasmuch as the
average purchaser of a halfpenny stamp is very apt to
want change for a shilling and seldom wants anything
else from stock, the demand fox a relaxation of the late
Government’s retrenchment policy in this matter seems
fair enough. All the other Australian Governments are
kinder than 4d. in the £ to their stamp agents.
* * * *
Even now, it would seem Mount Deddick isn’t
so dead as it ought to be. A syndicate, it is alleged,
wants permission to build a railway from Cunninghams,
the windy little town on the Gippsiand Lakes, to Beddick
at a cost of £375,009, and asks for a grant of 500 acres of
supposedly mineral land at the Mount. But Victoria has
no privately-owned railways now, and it would lie a pity
to introduce the lamentable system. Moreover, the
general recent experience is that the privateshindykil be-
gins its railway without money, and breaks down about
10 miles from its starting place, and howls for State aid.
And if lonesome Tamlio and Croajingolong and Ite-
nambra. and the men up the Snowy lliver had come to re-
gard the line as a certainty, and then saw it slipping
away, they would doubtless back up the co.'s demand for
aid, and the Government, rather than lose the vote of
East Gippsiand, would probably give it, and, quite possi-
bly, in the end the State would have an unpayable Beddick
line on its hands at a higher price than if it hod built the
thing itself outright.

Oliver Cambridge, the young, long-haired
and purely local Australian, whose fearsome tales
of travel, and tendency (on paper) to be knouted
in Russian dungeons and to seize the raging lion
by the bail in Africa, earned him a passing noto-
riety, was last heard of as having a bullet m him
at dtahuhu (Maoriland). Bainbridge’s statement
is that he was mooning along the road in that land
of profound peace, looking at the scenery with a
loaded revolver in his hand —which is eminently
probable, for Bainbridge always seemed the sort of
youth who would arm himself with a Winchester
rifle before tackling the bloodthirsty and revengeful
porridge at breakfast. Then he fell over some-
thing, and the revolver went off.
C. J. Moran, M.L.A. for East Coolgardie
(Westralia), is not intensely popular with his con-
stituents, and quite a number of them recentlj
signed a requisition to him -in the following
terms: —
We, the undersigned, electors of the East Coolgardiegold-
fields, in view of the generous offer made by Mr. Conolly,
M.L.A., to join the Australian contingent in the Trans-
vaal, hereby respectfully request that you, as our repre-
sentative, will do likewise. We firmly believe that a
knowledge of your valor and our sincere wish would war-
rant English officers sending you early to the front of
battle; and assure you that during your absence from
legislative duties our interests will in nowise be adversely
affected. The favor herein solicited is the only one we,
your constituents, are likely ever to ask, and should you
grant the boon your return will be watched with un-
speakable anxiety.—Yours hopefully.
“ Yours hopefully ” has a cheerless and invidious,
sound under the circumstances.
Daily Telegraph item re the Cow-Shak-
ing Government of N.S.W. :
The Minister for Public Instruction does not intend to
allow public school-teachers to indulge publicly in disloyal
rhetoric. He has had reports of several school-teachers
who, not satisfied with receiving Government pay and
having a secure Government billet, have openly ex-
pressed sentiments disloyal to the Power that provides
their incomes. This sort of thing, says Mr. Perry, can’t
be tolerated. Only one case, however, is clear enough for
investigation. He has ordered an iniquiry to be made.
Now if Perry refers to Britain as the Power which
provides the teachers’ incomes, that foolish little
man ought to be reminded that the said Power
doesn’t provide a single copper of the money—it
all comes from the N.S.W. taxpayer. And if he
is talking of N.S.W. as a Power, he doesn’t know
a Power when he sees one.

The Commercial of Sydney holds £1,333,731 in State

Earnings are at the rate of £4 ss. sd. per cent, per annum
(on shareholders’ capital, £85,000, and reserve fund,

of profit and loss. There is a very good increase
in deposits, and cash and liquid securities (£77.747, equal
to 35 per cent, of all liabilities to the public) have increased
during the half-year by over £lO,OOO. The great strength
of the little Ballarat Bank lies in the fact that the share-
holders’ funds are enormous in proportion to the size of
the business. If its advances depreciated 50 per cent, it
would still he a perfectly solvent concern, so far as
depositors were concerned, while, in the case of the biggest
and most esteemed banks in Australasia, a decline of 25
per cent, in the value of advances would mean practical
insolvency. From this standpoint, some of the smallest
banks are also the soundest.

The Commercial Bank of Australia publishes a
balance-sheet for the half-year to 31st December, 1899,
which, like most balance-sheets of this institution, tells
nothing definite. The profits from the visible section of
the hank show a great improvement; the profits or losses
of the half of the bank whose affairs consistently remain
dark may be better or worse—there is no information on
the subject. Visible profits compare as follows with
those of previous half-years since the second reconstruc-
tion :
Decemher, 1896.. £41,636 Decemher, 189 S .. £52,50l
June, 1897 .. 52,529 June, 1899 .. 52.247
Decemher, 1897 .. 53,222 December, 1899 .. 65,351
June, 1898 .. 52,363
There is here a very remarkable improvement so far as
the Commercial Bank of Australia is concerned. The profits
shown above are equal to £4 Is. 7d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds (capital, £3,163,938 ; reserve
fund, nil ) —a very great advance on last half-year’s earn-
ings of £3 7s. Id. per cent. A dividend of 3 per cent, per
annum on preference shares absorbs £31,756. (By the
terms of the last reconstruction preference shareholders
were guaranteed a 2 per cent, per annum dividend—
£2l,l7l per half-year—until 30th June, ’99, and thereafter
a fixed dividend of 3 per cent, per annum, and it is a very
remarkable coincidence that in the first half-year when
the extra £10,585 had to he paid the profits increased
about £13,000 and covered the extra liability very com-
fortably). Another £30,000 is transferred to the Special
Assets Trust; and the remaining £3595. added to previous
balance, leaves £13,843 to he carried forward.
* * * *
The Special Assets Trust remains a mystery, as
usual. Something like £4,200,000 (nominal value, any-
how) of the Commercial Bank of Australia’s assets are
transferred to the Trust, and these were always under-
stood to be the least profitable and the least realisable
assets. About £3,400,000 of the hank’s liabilities (de-
posits) were also transferred to the Trust. But these
assets are as much the bank’s assets, and these liabilities
are as much the bank’s liabilities, as if they had never
been transferred. The Special Assets Trust is, to all
intents and purposes, as much a part of the Commercial
Bank of Australia as the Bank itself. If the Trust’s assets
don’t earn enough to pay interest on its liabilities the
Bank has to make up the shortage in the end. If the
assets don’t sell for enough to pay off the liabilities the
Bank has to pay' the difference. If there is a surplus in
the end the Bank takes the surplus. Therefore, unless
the Trust earns just enough to a penny to cover its out-
goings, the profits shown are not. the real profits, and they
don't show the real position of affairs, and the share-
holders are being kept in the dark as to the state of their
property, for the balance-sheet says not one word about
the Trust’s earnings. If the Trust earned £40,000 less
than working expenses and interest (supposing such a
thing were possible) then the Commercial Bank of Aus-
tralia, after paying its preference dividend, made a loss of
some £6400 ; if the Trust had a £20,000 shortage, then
the profit, after paying preference dividend, was only
about £13,600. These are interesting speculations.

Some 'time ago the directors of the Commercial Bank of
Australia professed to explain the reasons why they
supplied no information about the income, expenditure,
profits or losses of what is practically about one-third of
the Bank. They said that they, having only assumed
office since the reconstruction, were not responsible for
the old business taken over by the Trust (Tun
Bulletin didn’t say they were responsible ; it only said
the shareholders were entitled to know how that old busi-
ness—their own old business —was getting on). They
also said they couldn’t take the responsibility of valuing
these old assets. (The Bulletin never asked them to
value them. It only asked them to state—what they
surely must know —what income they produced and how
far it went towards covering outgoings.) They also said
that information about the Trust was available in the
Trust’s printed statement, but that statement didn’t
show either income or expenditure, so it revealed nothing
about how the Trust was, or was not, paying its way.
This reticence creates an impression that the Trust isn’t
doing very well. It isn’t even known how much of the
£30,000 transferred from the Bank to the Trust is re-
quired to pay interest on deposits, or if it is all so re-
quired, or whether more is wanted ; or whether there is
a balance to go towards writing down assets. The
Trust’s difficulties make no difference to the de-
positors in the Commercial Bank, but they interest
the shareholders very largely. They will have to
make up the ultimate shortage, if any, whether
it is £5 or a million. And if the new and valuable and
beautifully-painted Vic. hanking law, which was to make
all hank balance-sheets plain and above-board, allows the
income and .expenditure as regards over £3,000,000 of
assets and liabilities to he wholly kept dark merely by cal-
ling them a Special Trust Co., then the new Vic. banking
law isn’t so good as it looked.
Visible points are these : During the past half-
year the Commercial Bank deposits have increased about
£6ooo—this despite the repayment last September of
£340,000 of reconstructed moneys. Private current
accounts, in particular, show a splendid increase of about
£435,000. Note circulation has increased £lO,OOO. Cash
and liquid securities have increased by nearly £370,000,
and now stand at £2,132,660—equal to the enormously
strong proportion of 50 per cent, of all the bank’s lia-
bilities to the public. And against this there is the solemn
fact that the deposits of the Assets Trust are being paid
off at the following very slow rate :
}-yearto June, 1897 £27,907 }-year to Dec., 1898 £56,591
„ Dec., 1897 41,949 „ June, 1899 26,525
„ June, IS9B 45,474 „ Dec., 1899 20,682
This means that £219,P28 of the Trust co.’s reconstructed
deposits have been paid off in three years, reducing the
total from £3,642,001 to £3,422,873. At this rate of pro-
gress the liquidation will be finished in about 48 years.
In the same period £183,000 has been paid to the Assets
Trust out of the earnings of the Commercial Bank. There
is very little direct evidence in these facts, still they
suggest that the Trust’s assets are very slow of realisa-
tion, and that it is able to absorb a very handsome amount
of the hank’s earnings.

Out of the £31,153 which remains after payment of the 10
per cent, dividend, £lO,OOO is added to reserve fund
making it £100,000; and £lO,OOO to reinsurance fund
making it £120,000. The remainder raises the profit balance
from £2193 to £13,346. The New Zealand Insurance
Co.’s reserves are altogether £236,868. A good deal has
been done this year to repair the slumpof 1898, when the co.
went £35,541 to the bad, reckoning loss and dividend, and
t00k£35,000 from its reserve fund. Net premiums (£315,284)

largest fire and marine premium income in Australasia.
Interest and rents are £l6,992—about £3OO less than last
year. Still they average about 4 per cent, on investments,
which looks as if the assets were quite sound. The New
Zealand Insurance Co. has not only the largest premium
income, hut also the largest reserves among Australasian
co.’s. Even the great South British Co. which is an ex-
ceedingly good second, only shows £197,082 of reserves ;
and the rest are a long way behind.

At December, 1898, the paid-up capital was £73,376, and
at June 1899 it was £89,702, hut on both these occasions
the dividend was paid on £60,000 only. Capital paid-up
is now £90,000, and for the first time the dividend at 5 per
cent, per annum is paid on the full amount. After pay-
ing it there is a balance of £443, which, added to amount
brought forward, makes an accumulated profit of £3696.
The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. some
time back wrote off all its reserve fund to cover the ex-
penses of an unfortunate lawsuit and to write down
depreciation of certain unfortunate investments in the
Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank. These invest-
ments now stand in the balance-sheet as follows :

To keep strictly to market values the co. ought to write
another £2580 off these securities, but as it is becoming
practically certain that the A. D. and M. Bank’s deposits
will yet be worth the full 20s. in the £, the point is of less
importance than it was. If the other assets of the
Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. have been written
down strictly to present values, the co., with its improving
income, may be reckoned as showing reasonable signs of
solidity. Its chief troubles are that its leasehold property,
37 Queen-street, Melbourne, yielded last half-year £350
less than the amount paid on it in rent, and the £20,000
mortgage on the co.’s freehold cost in interest last half-
year £250 more than the co. received as interest on the
£20,000 of its own funds, which are invested for the
purpose of paying off mortgage at maturity. Still, these
things will probably come right in time. The great
defect is the want of any reserve fund, and one ought to
be started immediately out of the profit balance of £3696.
Also, the co. should take a resolve not to increase the
dividend beyond the present 5 per cent, till that reserve
reaches something like £50,000.

• * * •
Compare the returns made by some Westralian
co’s to the Govt, with the same co’s’ reports furnished to
shareholders—and then make a note of it. Lake View
Consols, for instance, told the Govt, (statutory declara-
tion accompanying) that in September 15,5310 z. were
obtained ; report to shareholders made it 32,0800 z. But
in October the figures supplied to Govt, were 55,1510 z.,
while those exported for shareholders’ (and perhaps
intending shareholders’) consumption were 30,0720 z. On
the year’s operations the totals are much the same; but
the comparison shows just how little “ averaged ” returns
are worth—and they all do it, or nearly all.
* • * *
When the figures are all made up it will be
found that Coolgardie district produced about 127,3500 z.
gold during ’99—a serviceable spring from the 93,6730 z.
of ’9B. Returns latterlv average just about an ounce a
ton ; this includes gold from tailings cyanided. The
district has left alluvial-mining behind it; the yield from
that source is not now 2 per cent, on the whole. Bayley’s
is the best producer, with Burbank’s close up.
* * • *
Spite of satisfactory.reports, tbe old hand will
reckon that all is not as well as it might be with Green-
bushes tinlield (W.A.) when “most of the leases have
taken advantage of the Christmas exemption, and the
field is practically deserted.” *
There was not a single Westralian goldfield (and
officially it has just a score of them) that had not by the
end of Nov. far outstripped, for’99, its total ’9B output.
Murchison had to hustle most to do it—at least, it didn’t
hustle and only just did it.

plant so far are satisfactory, and anything from 1090 to

probably 500 oz. Lying round are about 00,000 tons
tailings. But the mines badly want a fillip underground
as well.

» * * *
W.A. Associated Mines yield for December —
22280 z. from 3950 tons, or much less than one-half the
returns of some previous months. And this depressing
report from the manager : “ Quite impossible to get ore
from Yetley shaft last month. . . Total amount crushed
from lack of perfection of details is unsatisfactory.” Has
the sulphide plant proved a failure? If so, this will
account for the drop in price, though that cannot affect
the reduced tonnage. Apparently dividends will be
fewer and smaller this year than last.
* * * *
Wire from a Kalgoorlie correspondent: Returns
for the next three or four months will probably show a
decrease all round. The Associated’s January output
may be larger than that for December, but the average
yield per ton is almost certain to be much less. Lake
View Consols is in much the same situation. If the cost
per ton of sulphide treatment were made public it would
be calculated to give the shareholders paralysis, but particu-
lars are carefully concealed here. Information of this kind
goes to London, and drifts hack in a gradual way by
mail when London people are quite done with it. The
Great Boulder is putting through its richest tailings, and
the returns from them compensate for the falling-off
in the supply of oxidised ore —for there is a very great
falling-off. It is an open secret that the London people
are very anxious over something or other with regard to
both the Associated and Lake View Consols, and the
stoppage of shipments to the smelters has caused a
startling diminution in the output. There has been some
movement in Hannan’s Consols, an unpromising show
adjacent to Hannan’s Reward ; also in Hannan’s Central,
which adjoins Block 45, and resembles it in patchiness.

The Lady Shenton Mine (Westralia) for four
weeks ending 6th January obtained 12400 z. 7dwt«. of
smelted gold from 1244 tons ; and 490 z. 14dwts. from 115
tons of “ trial ore,” whatever that may be. This makes
rather less than loz. per ton. For the year 1899 the out-
put from the mine was 13,779 tons, yielding 22,2450 z.
17dwts. of smelted gold, and in addition 5720 z. 4dwta.
were obtained from 203 tons of concentrates. The last
clean-up, therefore, shows a much smaller yield per ton
than the year’s average. The regular gag is that “ the
stone will get richer as it goes down,” but it much oftener
gets the other thing.

“ M. 0.” : On the barren acres of tbe Dora mine,
a concern which an enterprising push of West Coast (Tas-
mania) promoters shot into a speculative public, there is
not a soul to be found now, and the lonely crow flaps its
wings disconsolately over a telephone-line extracted out
of the Government by the same crowd. The Red Hills
Mountain of Copper (?) so consistently boomed in many
quarters, seems also pretty dead, and unless it sells to a
new co. which has better success than the old one
the Tas. Central Board of Health should order au early
burial.
• * * *
J. A. Wallace’s introduction of electricity
instead of steam, as a motive power, on some of his
Yackandandah dredges, is one of the most important
changes so far made in Australian dredging ; though, of
course, there are comparatively few places where sufficient
force of water can be got at a handy distance. The
biggest gain from the public point of view is that, with
the fuel bill reduced, the dredges will not have to pick
out the eyes of the alluvials flats, as they are doing now.

“Melb.”: A curiosity is the House-Knacker
town, in North Fitzroy, on the plain near the gas-works.
Here are gathered the wooden houses which have been
rooted up from all quarters to escape the heavy sewage
tax. They await transference to other places, according
as they may be sold, and £lO will buy a residence on
which a thrifty paterfamilias has paid his £l5O to the
building society and lost it after all.
» * * *
For 17 years S.A. State Forest Department has
been distributing trees free ; in that time 4,582,133 plants
have been sent out. And during the eight years that
vines have been given away, 1,258,327 have been dis-
tributed among 6148 persons. The Department is now
specially fostering the date-palm plantations in the far
north; at latest 3013 plants were alive and flourishing.
The overflow from several Oentralian artesian bores is
bordered with these palms.

“ Y.”: The profits of fruit-growing: I sent four
gin-cases of excellent peaches to market last week by a
dealer. After paying charges, middlemen, and others, I
got 9d. per case for my fruit! Come off the land, young
man !

The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
Ltd., South Melbourne, to build a pair of compound con-
densing engines of 700 h.p., to drive their extensive mil-
ling plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proell valve gear. The flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons. The
total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines are
to be completed by the end of the year and will be the
finest and largest plant on the field. Three engines under
construction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie Gold-
fields have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*
Cyanide, caustic soda, chloride of lime, and all
mining and assay chemicals and apparatus, can he ob-
tained of Felton, Grimwade and Co., Melbourne, who
have issued a very handsome illustrated catalogue of all
mining requisites, price Is. 3d. post free.

The United Brothers gold mine, situated at
Glen Wills, about 25 miles from Omeo, Gippsland, has an
improved appearance, and even better things are looked
for as deeper ground is reached. The mine possesses an
up-to-date mill. The stone is conveyed automatically to
hopper, thence to Gate’s Crusher and on to 20 head of
stamps of the A.T.B. pattern. A small but complete
plant of Luhrig Yanners and Classifiers treats the battery
tailings and continues to win a large percentage of valu-
able concentrates, which are a satisfactory source of
revenue to the co. This Luhrig plant was supplied and
erected under contract by Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere,
506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne.*
Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British ” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; also
for the Taylor Ilorsfield Improved National Rock Drills,
Air Compressors, lie., &c.*

Related that a little gang of Bendigo “amal-
gam-touchers ” used to plant their loot in a heap of tail-
ings and sometimes had to wait a month for a chance to
get the stuff away. But alas! one of the gang formed
an unholy alliance with a local Fatman, and when there
was a specially large plant of spoil, Fatman bought the
tailings from the co. and had the heap watched day and
night until it was carted off, the deal resulting in a profit
to him of close on Jt'looo.
• • * *
A Sydney landlord in a small way lately bounced
an elderly female tenant for rent-arrears, and seized both
sewing-machine and mangle. “ Drop them,” said the old
lady, “ and get! ” Presently the landlord found that a
recent Act had exempted from distraint typewriters,
mangles and sewing-machines. Landlords’ special privi-
leges in N.S.W. are, without the community being fully
aware of it, being gradually eaten away. For instance,
in addition to the above exemptions, any tenant may hold
against his creditor-landlord wearing apparel, tools of
trade and bedding to the value of CIO. Further, should
the landlord set about shunting the tenant, this is the
process : First, he must give the tenant seven days’ notice
to quit; second, at the end of that time he must apply for
an ejectment-order, for which he pays ss. It will take
between five and eight days for the application to come
before the court, and then the magistrate may decide
that the order shall take effect in a week, 14 days or a
month. Next, the landlord presents himself anew to take
out the ejectment-order, for which he pays another half-
crown. Finally, armed with the order, he may call on a
policeman to put the tenant out. No member of the
community was so buttressed with special rights and
lirivileges as the landlord once was, hut now he doesn’t
oom up and obliterate the heavens as he used to do.
* * * *
“Scissors”: The newspaper-law needs amend-
ing so as to prevent “ enterprising proprietors” forcing
their papers on the public and then demanding payment.
Why should a newspaper-man, any more than any other
trailer, have the right by law to do this? Business firms
frequently receive gratuitous copies for the sake of their
advts., and with the number of free periodicals of all
kinds arriving almost daily, it is impossible to tell the
“complimentary” copy from the blackmail, and conse-
quently one is in a quandary about returning them to the
P.O.
• * • *
N.S. Wales was, from its earliest days, the Tom
Tiddler’s ground of the medical quack. Every convict-
ship carried men who professed to he able to heal all
diseases and who sold their ignorance for whatever
it would fetch. Rum was a good currency, hut
victuals or clothing were gracefully accepted in quittance.
The public of ttie day seemed to prefer these practitioners
to those accredited by Govt. Gov. King, who had a
liberal sense of duty in this respect, ordered all the
quacks of the territory to appear before him on a certain
day, and when the muster was complete he interpellated
the gang severally as to antecedents and medical
knowledge. He picked out a few and licensed them, and
the rest he prohibited to practise under pain of exile.
But after King the industry revived, each convict-ship
setting loose in Sydney one or more rascals whose sole
medical knowledge was acquired in scrubbing the wards
of gaol hospitals in England.

Bertram Mackennal’s statue 'of Her
Gracious sails for Melbourne this month, but will
probably not be unveiled -at Ballarat before May
24th. The idea is to reserve it for a special burst
of Birthday “loyalty.” The Queen, as sculped by
Mackennal, is a noble work of art, and a master-
piece of deception, judging from criticisms to
hand. The “squatness” of the aged figure is
dissembled by its royal robing, and the fact that it
WHY THEY WERE GOING.

PARTY (unanimously): “ Oh, we're taking little Johnny to the pantomime. Children are so fond of the pantomime.”
stands ten feet high will go a long way towards
convincing Ballarat that a limited monarchy is a
very fine form of government.
* * * *
The cruel story is told in the West that a
“volunteer for service,” who owed the examining
surgeon a considerable sum for attendance, was
Sromptly turned back with the remark that the
octor thought this the best climate for his ex-
patient until —well, until all the traces of the late
illness had been removed. As the only remaining
trace of the late illness is the unpaid bill the re-
mark might be construed as a hint.
In Melbourne the other day a wild-eyed
British drunk went into the shop of a German
tobacconist and demanded tobacco gratis. Appar-
ently he was a good jingo, who thought the
degraded foreigner ought to keep a noble citizen
in free tobacco. He afterwards broke a plate-glass
window with his billy and was fined £lO. The
loial papers described it as “assault by a pro-
Boer.” Melbourne is so greasily “loyal” just
now, that a man can’t even throw road-metal at
a dog without being called a pro-Boer.
• * ¥ m
A romantic Maoriland damsel, signing
herself “ Polly Atkins,” has suggested, in a letter
to the Wellington Evening Post, that, as a
barber is kept busily employed at the encamp-
ment, the clippings and shearings might be artisti-
cally made up into locks, with Contingent colors,
and be sold to swell some of the numerous funds
now being provided. She is sure that, at Is. a
piece, the supply would be unequal to the demand;
but, at the same time, she says that precautions
should be taken that “we shall not be supplied
with any hair but that shorn from the dear boys
who are going across the water.” But, alas !
everything in this world is adulterated, and even
the dear boys’ hair would probably get mixed.
And then a worm would gnaw at Polly’s soul as
she wondered in after years if she had really got
the right hair or not.
• • * *
These jingo Australians should really be
more careful of their history. Captain Freehill,
at the farewell to N.S.W. Irish Rifles, off to shoot
Boers, magniloquently reminded his hearers that
the Irish
would display the bravery of their forefathers, which was
shown at Fontenoy, at Waterloo, and wherever the
honor of the British flag had been upheld.
Now, it was precisely at Fontenoy that the honor
of the British flag was not upheld ; the British and
their allies were beaten, thanks, in great measure,
to the charge of the brigade of Irish exiles which
fought with the French.
» * * *
Lollie Kaser Singh was duly hanged at
Adelaide, the other morning, for the destruction
of a brother Indian-hawker with an axe. Lollie
may be said to have died for the glory of his
ancient race, whose courage had been under a
cloud of suspicion since the time when one Fatta
Chand cut a gruesomely humorous figure at
Ballarat gaol. Fatta Chand showed the strongest
possible objection to death by violence. After
being placed on the drop he wriggled his arms
out of bondage and caught hold of the beam, so
that it became necessary for the hangman to un-
loosen Fatta’s grip of this world and assist the
brown malefactor into eternity. The late Lollie
effected a very different exit. He bowed politely
to the hangman, and the warders, and the press-
reporters, then smiled a pleasant smile and
straightway dropped through the iron floor, like a
hawker and a gentleman.
The “At Home,” and the “afternoon
tea,” are almost silent feminine excesses compared
with “Ladies’ day” at the baths.
When a swarm of females from
five up to 50 invade the
baths they all shriek
and scream and splash
and shout and laugh
and cry and call and
clamour at once. It is
an ear-splitting confu-
sion of throats alto-
gether eclipsing the
corroboree at the
tower of Babel.
Every swimmer and
paddler wants to be
seen or heard or ad-
mired. She wants
to attract attention
although she hasn’t
a feather or a fashion
on. Her figure may be \
as angular as a cathedral
gable but she wants to be seen.
Yet nobody looks at her; every-
body is too busy hollering to
look at anybody. She may have
a voice like an angel; nobody
cares. Should any inexperienced nymph get beyond
her depth and cry for help she runs a big risk of a
watery end, for no female body heeds her. An
unemotional man is generally on the look-out and
his plunge into the water is the first intimation
to the bathers that somebody is sinking. The
female bather rarely learns to swim. She never
gives herself a moment for reflection; she merely
wears very little and hollers.
* • » *
In Western Queensland blessed with an
artesian well you occasionally meet with strange
experiences. You come in, hot, thirsty. You
naturally feel that a bath is just the thing you re-
quire and you adjourn for the purpose of getting
one. You pull the string or turn the tap (as the
case may be) and find yourself boiled in ten
seconds. Then you say tilings that would look bad
in print. You ascertain, shortly after, that the
tank has only just been filled from the bore, and
that the temperature is only 180 degrees Fahren-
heit. Then you say more things.

There are not want-
ing signs that the Con-
tingent mania will yet
do a good deal towards
breaking the already
weak financial back of
N.S. Wales. It is
gravely stated in S.M.
Herald (IG/l/’OO) that,
“under a mistaken
notion of what the
British army authorities
would do, the full rate
of 4s. Cd. was at first
paid out to them ” (the
families of the absent
Contingenters). “As
soon as it was ascer-
tained that the men in
the field would be paid
the Imperial rate per
diem, that amount was
deducted from the 4s.
6d. per day.” Now,
though 4s. 6d. a day is
probably the biggest
soldiers’ pay in the
world, it is a miserably
small sum when divided
up. The War Office
must have its ration
stoppages paid, and the
soldiers require tobacco,
boots, socks, shirts (such
things don’t last long
on active service); so,
in the end, the wife and
family of each Austra-
lian Absent - minded
Beggar will have to
crowd through on about
2s. 3d. a day (15s. 9d. a
week). If there are four
or five of them, and the
rent of the weather-
board cottage with the
hot iron roof, is 10s. a
week, that leaves ss. 9d.
a week for food and
clothes for the crowd.
So, unless the Austra-
lian warrior’s depend*
ants are to starve in
their absence, and be a
very bad advertisement for the country, it will
presently be necessary to do something pretty
large and expensive for them. And the financial
back of the country is already sagging down in
the middle.
* * * •
The small boy’s presence of mind is a
wonderful circumstance at times. A Hamilton
(Maoriland) smail boy lately discovered a man
who had just hanged himself, and with a dim
recollection of something about the means to be
taken for the resuscitation of the apparently
drowned, he rushed at the would-be suicide and
hauled desperately at his feet. He might have
hastened strangulation, or ho might have broken
the suspendee's neck, but as the luck happened
his extra weight broke the rope instead, and the
man fell on the small boy and was saved. Then
the boy, bruised but proud, explained the circum-
stances to the police.

beastly locality.” Probably meant
for “healthy locality.”

Officer (unwittingly describing the situation):
“ ’Tension!”
The dreadful mistake of the Vic. Vice-re-
gal and contingent farewell ceremonial was made
at Queenscliff, where the battery exploded mighl "y
in honour of a no-account vessel loaded with hides
and sundries, and allowed Brassey and his Sunbeam
to pass out later in ominous silence. The pathos
of it lay in the explanation of the officers that they
had only ammunition enough for one salute, and
having wasted it on the wrong vessel, they were
helpless.
* * * *
On the night of the passing of Melb.’s
2nd Contingent of Boer-prodders the warriors who
remained to hold up this end of the Empire (and
drink beer) became rather pugnacious. Outside
Young and Jackson’s hotel, at 11.30 p.m., two
Artillery men treated the public to a good, willing
fight, while a little higher up the street some blue-
jackets bailed up a Highlander, and pulled down
his socks, and compelled him at the point of the
bayonet to sing “Sons of the Sea” in a thick
Scotch voice with variations.

“N.”: What a lot of trash the so-called
National Gallery of Portraits, at the Melbourne
Public Library, is made up of. It is selected, or
pitchforked, on the principle of massing Shake-
speare, Milton, Smith, Brown, Jones, and Robinson.
Neither George Turner nor George Reid appears
there, although the living do flaunt in oil portraits
of smirky Dunky Gillies and a certain B. Cowde-
roy, Secretary of the Melb. Chamber of Commerce.
The only “Australian women” included are—Lady
Hbpetoun and Lady Carrington ! Even Higin-
botham is not there. What does such a rotten
National Gallery mean ? But stay, it includes all
the Governors of all the beastly kawlines. This
is what posterity will thirst after—Gawler,
Snooks, Jooks, and Lord Nozoo.

Chief Justice Madden, of Victoria : “If
ever there was a just quarrel this (the Transvaal)
war is one.” A return just laid before Vic.
Assembly sets forth that in 35 appeals from
Madden’s judgments his judgment was held to be
wrong in 20 cases—in other words he was wrong
four times out of seven. The above makes five
times.
BRIDE NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Into the World came the starlit Night,
With kisses cool and chaste
She held me close to her breast, moon-white.
And all my cares effaced.
She swept the dust from my weary eyes
With Angers deft and fair
And with the breath of her fragrant mouth
She tenderly fanned my hair.
At the hour of twilight, when passionate day
In a death-bed of glory dies
I hear the sweep of her regal robes
And feel the light of her eyes.
Ah dear Bride Night with calm soft arms
And hair in cloudy streams,
Come and kiss me out of the world
Into the land of dreams.
LOUIE ERNEST.

When the plague outbreak was reported
from Adelaide quite a number of Melb.’s represen-
tative citizens, who had booked sleeping-berths by
the Adelaide express, suddenly discovered that
they had urgent business which would detain them
for a week or so. When they notified the circum-
stance at the booking-office with a view to a re-
fund of the deposit, the clerk laconically and quite
innocently observed that “he had heard the
plague had appeared over there.” “O dear, no!
Nothing of that sort would keep me, I assure you 1
Pressing business matters ! ” One individual was
more candid. He wasn’t afraid of the plague
himself, but his wife and her mother were also
foing over, and he was alarmed for the old lady.
Ipidemics of that sort, he said, were usually so
fatal to old people.
* * * *
There is much of mystery surrounding the
alleged bubonic plague cases at Adelaide Hospital.
Eppstein, the seaman who was first attacked,
had been on board ship on his way from New York
for many weeks, touching at no infected port en
route, and after arriving in Adelaide had been
ashore for seven weeks before going to the hospi-
tal. The doctor who sent him there did not regard
him as a case of plague. He was sent to the hos-
pital on totally different grounds. The second
patient was a boy from a country township, and
the only reasonable hypothesis is that he caught
infection at the hospital. The authorities have
taken no notice of the charge freely made that the
trouble has arisen through someone failing to take
E roper care in the matter of plague “specimens
ept on the premises, and no outside experts were
consulted.
* * * *
The legend goes that during dear departed
Lady i.Brassey’s travels in Victoria, she took a
fancy on one occasion to have an escort of sailors
from the Cerberus, and they were speedily
mounted on a spare lot of police horses, a,nd
formed into a bodyguard. The son of the heaving
Yarra, as a rule, managed to stick to his craft
pretty fairly, but his clothes were unsuited to the
emergency. Grievous was the shock her Ladyship
and party received at beholding the pants of the
escort gradually working up to the knees as they
rode along, and exposing a vista of hairy legs before
the Vice-regal vision. The contingent was fre-
quently halted to have its disgraceful slacks
pulled down, but all was of no avail until the
nearest township was reached, and the local
saddler called in to make fast the unruly garments.
The officer in charge of the Naval Mounted Horse
was severely reprimanded for allowing such a
sight, and ordered to be placed at the bottom of
the list, which didn’t affect him much, as he was
the only one of the grade. This tale is published
in a frivolous spirit as a matter that may be true,
and if it isn’t it ought to be, and if it ought not to
be it doesn’t matter much.

A bright Christmas incident is reported
from Nagambie (Vic.) A local mother had for-
bidden her boy, Johnnie, aged'
12, to go swimming in the heat
of the day, and, naturally,
Johnnie went at the earliest
opportunity. Hearing of
the mutiny, his parent armed
herself with the neces-
sary weapon, and sailed
down to the river,
where Johnnie was
discovered disporting
in midstream. She
called Johnnie ashore,
but Johnnie knew
enough to remain out
in the wet, and dis-
creetly disobeyed ;
whereupon his mother
charged out upon a log
to improve the oc-
casion. The log canted,
and the matron was
swarming in the river,
making desperate and
unwise attempts to
swallow the whole
stream at a gulp.
This was J ohnnie’s
3ortunity, and right
ly he responded.
He went straight to
the rescue, and, after
a gallant fight, suc-
ceeded in towing the
heavy parent ashore.
For a few minutes
she lay upon the bank
with tTohnnie in one
hand and the strap
in the other. Pre-
sently, however, she re-
covered, and then, rising
to her feet, she turned
to her noble son—and
whipped the nude youth till he
was pink and purple from post
to finish. Shows how sharper than
a serpent’s tooth it is to have a thank-
less mother !
When there are no warships in, and no
contingents to send out, ’Obart has to depend on
its own little tragedies to keep itself awake. At
a recent very swaggish “dahnce,” a diminutive
ex-Judge created intense excitement by cavorting
around with a lady’s hair-comb dangling at the
end of his whiskers. And the strangest part of
the business was that the owner claimed it without
a tinge of remorse. But this is only an episode
in ’Obart.
* * * *
The unclean city of Footscray (Victoria),
the bone-crushingest and soap-boilingest place of
its size in Australia, almost got cleaned a little the
other day. In view of the bubonic plague out-
break at Adelaide, a councillor suggested that one
extra man be put on to remove the things that are
better not specified from the open gutters and the
mud-flats, but his suggestion lapsed for want of a
seconder.
The small ironies of life. Oyer the prison-
ers’ entrance at Prahran (Vic.) police court hangs
a horse-shoe—presumably for luck,
though whether it is for the
luck of the copper or the
copped nt one knows.
“ MUSICAL MELBOURNE. "—SOME “ROAR” MATERIAL.
The outbreak of bubonic plague in a S.A.
country town has led a doctor with foreign
experience of the disease to refer to the serious
Sart rabbits may play in its perpetuation and
istribution among the Australian inlands. The
rabbit is a great germ-carrier, and the danger is
the greater because, in drought time, the rabbits
flock into the neighborhood of the townships,
where they can at least get a little garbage and
water, and a good many then die about the houses
or on the nearest water-catchments.

“ Cecil P.” : Sign, on humpy, in a little
New England hamlet, reads “ Tronduenmiphili-

Tasmanian papers (they have got the Jingo
fever very bad in Tasmania, and are coming out
in spots the size of a dinner-table) are deluged
with patriotic poetry, supposed to be original. It
isn’t all original, for one excited Tassie lately sent
in the old “Story of a Drum,” and the editor,
whose reading was apparently limited, printed it
in all good faith as a perfectly fresh local produc-
tion. But some of the outbreaks are obviously
made on the premises. One recently published in
Launceston Examiner has the refrain :
Rich and poor, high and low,
Are all invited to pay
For Justice, Honor, and Tommy,
With a hip-hip-hip-hurray 1
“ Justice, Honor and Tommy ! ” Why not “ For
God and John Smith ? ”
But, y’see, yer Waship, I’ve got a cooler at my
place, and, it being not, the gentleman brought
down the couple of bottles and left ’em with me
the day before.” However, there was the payment
to be accounted for; for money had been seen to
pass. “ Oh, yes, yer Waship ; y’see I’d washed a
pair of pants for the gentleman, and he was paying
me.” And yet they fined her £SO.
The honorary beak again. For assaulting
his wife with'a chair and breaking two of her ribs,
the Bendigo J.’sP. fined a man 405., and
ordered him to keep the peace,
and not break any more of
his wife, for six months !
“ T.D. ” So far the efforts of Australian
war correspondents are mostly dreary commonplace
beside the intense descriptive work of the big
English specials. The man from Australia is
handicapped by having to chase round after the
little penny contingents instead of seeing the stnte
where it is hottest. The big point about lately-
deceased Steevens, for example, was that he had a
practised eye for the thing as a whole; had he
been put on to follow the IST. S.W. handful of
Lancers, he also would possibly have ground out
columns of stale and unprofitable small-beer.

As-The Bulletin anticipated, the support
of the so-called N.S.W. “ Bushmen’s Contingent”
has already been to some extent shoved on to the
NSW. taxpayer. The Govt, is feeding the pen
camped at Sydney, ah<T the Bushmen’s Committee
haughtily announces that it will only start paying
when it has picked and approved its men. Sundry
city men in tall hats, who wouldn t know a gum-
tree if they saw it, are doing the selection. It
would be interesting, by the way, to know how
much of the £22,000 subscribed is held p cash and
how much in enthusiastic promises. The most en-
thusiastically promising thing in nature is an eel.
“ Yarrum ” : At a certain N.S.W. con-
vent school, 75 per cent, of the young lady boarders
are daughters of publicans. How’s that for beer ?

The departure of the second Vic. contin-
gent on Saturday was remarkable principally for
the immense enthusiasm of the crowd and the.
utter extinction of Gov. Brassey as a public
institution. Five miles of man and woman
and dust and dog were in a state of intoxi-
cation, yet not one cheer was raised for the
departing Uncle Tom all along the route.
The Governor’s party was about 20yds. in
advance of the leading contingent line, and
the difference of the applause in the 20yds.
was astounding. People rushed the rifle-
men and affably pounded their backs, and.
once out of the city on the Port Melb.
road, the absent-minded beggar’s casual
Sirl with her large red hat falling un-,
eeded m the road and her short skirts
flying in the wind charged as one girl
and carried the day. All the rest of
the way 250 warriors and 500 girls
were inextricably mixed, the formation
being badly burst up in tearful females
who hung round the necks of the con-
tingent and wept all over their khar-
kee. And Uncle Tom, mooning along
like a large, sleepy, kindly matured cat at
the top of the procession might have been a
Boer for all the enthusiasm he created.
The Australians in the
Transvaal seem to bear charmed
lives. Every day cables say.
they pass unharmed through
a perfect hail of bullets, miles
by hours in dimensions, their clothes are
perforated, their horses shot, the ground raked

THE DISLOYAL DOMINIE.
Mr. Perry, of the Education Department, was
sent into the country the other day to enquire into
the case of a public school teacher accused of dis-
loyalty. The charge was, in effect, that this
unpatriotic pedagogue had advised his pupils to
“pray for the Boers.” Mr. Perry “considered
the matter of sufficient importance to demand a
full and searching enquiry.”
Far out, or near—l cannot say—
But call the place Burrungaree—
There is a school where pupils pray
For Boers vile diurnally.
That this was wrong they did not know ;
Their wicked teacher taught them so.
And, since the war began, it seems
Those youngsters of Burrungaree
For Boers and their brutal schemes
Have been a-praying constantly—
They did not know the Boers were
The bosom friends of Lucifer.
The efficacy of such prayer
No mortal man can ever tell !
P’r’aps Gatacre was caught in snare,
And poor Penn Symons fighting fell,
All through the prayers sent up so free
By juvenile Burrungaree.
The Shocking Traitor, steeped in crime,
Who did his country thus betray—
What made him so his pupils prime ?
Was he, perchance, in Kruger’s pay 7
The case against him looks as black
As if he’d pawned the Union Jack.
If he had even stood up, and
Blasphemed like Holman, M.L.A.
Against the Dear Old Motherland,
’Twould not have been so bad, I say,
As thus to use, for purpose vile,
The prayers of the juvenile.
And, horrible to contemplate !
He might have still been doing so ;
And hampering the march of Fate ;
And bribing Heav’n to work us woe;
Had not some patriot, out back,
Put Mr. Perry on his track.
Then Mr. Perry, in hot haste,
Swooped down upon Burrungaree,
And under cross-exam, he placed
That rank, disloyal dominie.
Result: “He tells his pupils they
Should always for the British pray.
There was a Personage Divine-
Long, long ago, in Galilee — .
Who taught—and tried—the creed benign
Of praying for the enemy.
He might redeem mankind—but He

The number of smiling and happy faces
lining the Melb. streets on the day when Lord
Brassey and the contingent went away did not
denote universal joy at the loss of that fro’i-Dome
potentate. It testified to the universal pop Parity
ot the P.D. corset. The consciousness that she is
encased m a P.D. will preserve lovely woman’s
equanimity even under the harrowing circum-
stances of a contingent’s departure. *
It was encouraging to notice in a recent number
of Pearson s Weekly that the public in England were
appreciating the South Australian invention, the “ Alba-
tross. Lamp Wick.” They endorse all that is claimed for
VC, viz., that it requires no trimming,” saves oil, gives
better light, and is far safer.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
My Dear Myee,- Sydney ’ January 23 ’ 1900 -
dS the J h | ey march ® d *?»y down the
village street, their banners floating gay <
bringing the history book up to the date of that
warm Australian Wednesday; breaking through
our one little century’s shelteredness, and commg
face to face with a great remote, beautiful
full of guile and horror, gilded till it fascinates’
that was what Sydney was doing last Wednesday
when the second contingent went away. ' y
I went over the Moravian, and saw the nurses’
cabins. All m the steerage, except Miss Gould’s »
Certainly they were to dine saloon, but I shall not
forget in a hurry that eight-berthed stuffy hole
wher«te nd l h l 10rses ’ bey ° nd the kitchens,
where those plucky women were going to camn
en ™y*ge- It was remarked that women must get
used to roughing it when they are going out to
battle, but why not apply that remark to the men
also . Trust me, a hospital nurse doesn’t want any
longer apprenticeship to hardships than a man. '
Silhouettes silhouettes. Black shadows thrown
by the golden sunlight. A weed of a boy with six
women round him, mother grey-haired, with wild
eyes betraying the desperate mind, revolving re-
volving, from his first tooth and his first prize up
to this terrible day ; “ his girl ” sobbing, but sup-
ported by his arm and the knowledge that she is
his girl; two friends who have come to help her
bear it, broken down into their handkerchiefs
crying worse than anyone ; little sister with rims
of scarlet to her big black eyes; married sister
come from the country to say good-bye to “our
Jim. Only a weed of a boy, made of a pattern of
thousands of others. But six women, In mortal
anguish even now, when he stands there thin and
weed-like and living beside them.
His Ex. paid a little visit to the Moravian
before it started. He was gotten up in the green
vestments of our own Australian Horse. I had to
assure myself of that several times, else I must
have believed he was masquerading as Rosalind
In the words of somebody else (I feel sure that
these are not my very own) “a large green hat
flopped sideways over the soft young face, the
wind drifted through his black plumes, and he
dipped like a wilted lily against the soft dark-
green of Ins collar.”
Anyone with a dry eye must have smiled at the
anxious motherliness with which Chief-Justice
Darley shepherded Rosalind through the crowd.
‘On his own ” the Chief is a hardened diplomat
that nothing can fluster. But when taking Little
Billy under his wing, he looks as worried as if he
was suffering from congestion of the conscience
unscientifically known as Moralysis. Certainly
His Ex. looks exquisitely helpless on all State
occasions, and the dear old Chief has had to guide
his foot-steps, as it were, more than once.
. Bill-Jim, assembling to see Tommy off, crowded
m large quantities on to the roofs near the gate-
ways of Woolloomooloo wharf. On their off-
moments, when not engaged in guiding bits of the
procession between the gate-posts and in picking
off other bits that had already stuck, the Roberts
made systematic sorties on to the roofs by way of
ladders, and heaved Bill-Jim off in large and angry
fragments. When, during one of these raids, the
ladder fell and a lonely constable was left stranded
on the enemy’s territory, the cheer that rose from a
thousand British throats was worth even attend-
ing an embarkation to hear. Just then the Gover-
nor in his tight waist strayed past, looking every
inch a soldier—of the opera-liouffe ; upon seeing
whom the old ladies lifted up their voices and
wept. Why do old ladies always weep when they
see Lord Beauchamp ? It is a psychological
problem.
N.S.W. War Office does not work prettily. The
dispatch of that section of the second contingent
that went by the s.s. Surrey last week was a
master-piece of confusion. The Moravian looked
nice and clean and seemed to be possessed of some
desire to look respectable. But the Surrey was
awful. The decks were thick with coal-dust.
From-end to end she was covered with t .vo rows
of flimsy-looking horse-boxes that reminded one of
hen-houses on a poultry farm. They were guiltless
of pamt, with great cracks through which could be
seen the gathering crowds in the Domain. Black-
smiths and carpenters were hard at work putting
up more of these structures which looked as if one
moderate sea would carry them all td the bottom
of the vasty deep. Some of the horses were aboard
and their tails hung through the cracks.
On the wharf it was confusion gone mad. There
were carts of meat, waggons of fodder and port-
manteaus and top coats, lorries of flour, piles of
boxes marked “compressed mutton,” barrels
marked “vinegar,” a couple of maxim gun car-
riages, and stray fragments of kits and loose horse
furniture lying all round.
The Moravian had a plentiful supply of com-
pressed fodder piled round the funnel. If a spark
gets into it it will be declared that the wicked Dr
Leyds has reached out the long secret hand of
incendiarism from his Paris villa to save his
adopted country from the terrible fury of the
Cornstalk.
Judging by the way some of the horses played -
up at embarking, a few of the mounted infantry
are in for a bad time when they reach Afric’s
sunny fonteins. One brute, who was dozing in a
small army-service corps’ cart, suddenly awoke
with an insane desire to kick, and he did kick,
fiercely and impartially. He kicked his harness
off, he kicked the ribs of a big draught horse in a
flour lorry, kicked the cart down with a bang, and
he kicked a portmanteau off the splashboard, and
barely missed an officer’s chest as he did so. The
soldiers stood round him in a ring and shouted
‘‘ Whoa ! ” and “ Halt! ” What would have hap-
pened is hard to say had not a butcher-boy rushed
in and seized him by the headstall, and kicked
inn m the chest, and backed him against a pile of
wood where he simply couldn’t kick.

of flour from which to watch the soldiers go by.

ber skirts round her and shrieked loudly An
