More kharki, more Austral Horse on foot, and
tneLancers stream past waving farewells A girl
with a tear-stained face burst through the crowd
and gazed hungrily up and down the passing
column. When the last man had passed she sank
down on a box and sobbed. Down where the
troops were marching to the Australian the police
fil 1 , Naval Bngade were stopping all female
fi lends. But bit by bit the line of police and
blue-jackets bulged and gave way, and next
moment scores of women were streaming down
the wharf. After that things took care of them-
selves, and order went to such small pieces that I
couldn t find a fragment of it anywhere.
Some odium is due to the men who offered
bottles of whiskey to the Contingent all along the
line of march on Saturday, because it inspired a
nameless theological crank with the idea of offer-
ing tracts done up like the whiskey bottles afore-
said. 1 lie dry warriors opened them eagerly, and
were comforted with “ The Hying Drummer Boy,”
and things to that effect. Whereupon they
naturally took more whiskey, and the procession
demoralised in a terrible way.
The marriage of Miss Slattery, only daughter of
Lawyer Slattery, with Mr. Frank Lyons, son of a
late Maryborough solicitor, was accompanied by a
nuptial Mass and Communion, the Cardinal in his
red gown, and some beautiful singing at St. Mary’s
Cathedral on Tuesday morning. The wedding
made a stir in Catholic circles, as the blonde bride is
the centre of a bevy of handsome Catholic convent
girls who seemed to be debuting all in a bunch last
season. One of the bridesmaids was pretty Miss
Oxenham, in white tucked Swiss muslin
fichu and a white chiffon hat. Her father,
the redoubtable Humphrey, donated a mag-
nificent wedding gift, a huge diamond
crescent. The bride’s frock was a creation of
Limerick lace, made into a peplin over chiffon
flounces mounted on white glace silk. Mrs. Slat-
tery, aunt and step-mother combined to the bride,
wore a black lace polonaise over white silk, and
black lace flounces. The wedding was strictly
guestless, but the Cathedral was crowded, all the
same. In the afternoon a reception went on at
the Australia, and the bride left for the Mountains
in a grey voile frock made up over lilao silk, and a
grey straw hat with lilac feathers and orchids.
You will be sorry to hear that Edith Crane has
been ill ever since “ Tess ” started. She suffered
badly from her throat all through the first night,
and has been unable to play ever since. Linda
Raymond took lead for a few days, and Florrie
Gleeson was put into Linda’s place as milk-maid.
Then Emily Hughes was imported to play Tess,
and Linda Raymond went back to her own part,
which missed her badly, and Miss Hughes’ pain-
fully affected tones are nightly reading out her
newly-married name as “Mrs. En-gel Clee-ah.”
Which, being interpreted, means Mrs. Angel
Clare.
I told you last week about the scent given away
at the Royal and Her Majesty’s. This week goes
one—a dozen I should say—better. Afternoon
teas and ices are to be handed round to the d.c.
and reserved stalls at matinees ! This is theatre-
going made comfortable, if you like. In fact, the
only thing now wanted is a gilt-edged invitation
from J. C. Williamson to everybody “requesting
the honor,” etc.
The Newsky Family’s rapid heel-and-toe work
in their Cossack dance, at the Criterion, is pro-
bably the ideal system of gyration for their region
of snow where it takes a lot of rapid motion to
keep one’s nose from becoming public property.
But in that case why do nine-tenths of the dancers
stand almost still with their hands gently clasped
over their black velvet front clothing while the
other tenth in his fiery boots scuttles round the
semi-circle looking like a lost soul seeking the
shadiest spot in the infernal regions ? The British
dancer’s idea is to get his heels as near heaven as
possible, but the Cossack’s is evidently to get his
head as close to the earth. The one is a high-
kicker and the other looks like the football that is
to be kicked.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ During the wait for the procession of Brassey,
&c., on Saturday, just when every inch of Collins-
street, and every window was full up of spectators,
a vision of female loveliness in the shape of an
• elegant young lady, calmly drifted out of a case-
ment at the top of an eight-storey building and
perched herself on the six incli window-sill out-
side composedly dangling her pretty ankles and
two expensive stockings over the ledge. Partly
on the ground that the lady might fall and kiil
herself, partly for the reason that she couldn’t
well fall without dropping on and killing some one
else, and partly through wild excitement arising
from the stockings, this vision was received with
dread mingled with loud applause. The girl kept
her seat for about an hour, and then gracefully re-
tired, the audience giving three cheers for each
symmetrical ankle as she disappeared, and one
cheer for the rest of her. She deserved it as
much as did Brassey, for on that memorable day
neither of them fell off.
“ Owing to the protest of the W.C.T.U., all
alcoholic liquors donated to the second Vic. con-
tingent were refused admission to the boat. The
donors, however, triumphed; they sent along the
cases branded ‘Bibles for our boys,’ ‘Tracts for
Tommy Atkins,’ ‘the Christian Soldier,’ and
other devices, adding ‘ with the compliments of the
Y.M.C.A. and the W.C.T.U.’ as a guarantee of
good faith. - And though about a ton of alleged
bibles were thus shipped, this superfluity of
apparent scripture seemingly aroused no suspicion.
“ The edict went forth that no women were to
be allowed on Port Melbourne pier to farewell the
second contingent. The barriers were carefully
guarded against every wearer of a petticoat—until
the vessel moved away. Nevertheless a couple of
hundred casual girls hugged the contingent at the
gangway, in defiance of all civil and military law.
The mystery is explained by the local boatmen,
who reaped a great harvest by rowing the girls
across from the other pier during the morning.
They were then smuggled away in the cabins of
the different ships lying at the pier by officers
who were unable to resist their tearful appeal,
and remained in concealment until the contingent
arrived.
“ Soldiers are all the rage at present. Judging
by the neglected aspect of recently-paraded
veterans, it was not ever thus ; nor probably will
it be thus for long. Some years back this scribe
met in a suburban burying-ground the sparsely-
attended funeral of old General JEiyall, formerly of
Lome, Bendigo, and Deniliquin, who had annexed
wounds and medals in the Indian Mutiny, and
many other places. Same week, young Clive
Clarke, of Cliveden, a maiden warrior with his
spurs to win, was buried in Melbourne Cemetery
with all the martial pomp and circumstance
usually connected with the obsequies of some illus-
trious military hero.
General Ryall, by the way, is still remem-
bsrecl hereabouts as a pleasant Irishman who wil-
lingly turned up his shirt-sleeves and tackled the
washing-up, etc., whenever his fragile womenkind
lacked domestic help. At one time, when he lived
at Deniliquin, a selector’s unsophisticated daughter
was pressed into the family service. This simple
maiden was once disappointed in the matter of a
visit home —her father couldn’t come for ‘ Beetie ’
by reason of many incurable diseases in the horse,
When the General informed ‘ Beetie ’ she bitterly
called him an ‘old dummy.’ Being reproved for
this, she repented, and resolved never to call him
old again—the idea of him objecting to the
‘dummy ’ never occurred to her. That evening
the penitent servitor drove the dining-room door
open by aiming her bare foot at the handle, and
entered with a candle in each hand but no candle-
sticks. Carefully dropping a blob of hot grease
on the table-cloth to fix them in, she said cheer-
fully, ‘ Them’s the candles, young man.’ It was a
touching attempt to hold out the olive-branch
with her naked foot, so to speak, and the General
took it kindly.
“Janet Lady Clarke’s second boy ‘Russell ’ will
be of age in a month, so it would have been no use
his mother withholding her consent to the young
law-student’s cabled request that he might join the
gentlemen yeomanry ’ leaving England for the
I ransvaal. As her elder son is already at the front
with his regiment, the sorrows of war encompass
the popular widow as closely as most. Recently
at a Town Hall meeting in connection with the
Patriotic Fund, she adorned the platform in one
of her prettiest frocks. Black silk-muslin, shim-
mering with jet and silver, veiled all the close-
fitting under-dress of white silk, except the yoke
tight slee\ es and skirt-border. A. relief of white
chiffon epauletted the shoulders and softened the
collar. The Mayoress, with a hard-blue vest
and the same color piled on her broad-brimmed
hat, seemed crude and utilitarian by comparison.
If money can buy it, however, Master M‘Eachani
and his two sisters should be rich in acquired Vere
de Verishness. These juveniles spend their child-
hood in cloudy England, parted from father and
mother, with a view to cleaving' effectually unto
the accent, deportment, &c., of John Bull.
“ Mr. Cameron, of Bairnsdale, who is interest-
ing himself in the corps of bush scouts, is the son
of a patriarehial Highlander, owner of Bealiba
station (Vic.) Cameron, senior, was married
more than once. One of his brides was a widow
with a family of her own, so there was a regular
gathering of the clans under the Bealiba roof-tree.
Ihey spoke Gaelic among themselves, and some
were Catholics, others staunch Presbyterians, but
they were all devoted to one another, and all re-
freshingly simple, honorable and brave.
“The folly of church-going was exemplified
at an inquest the other day, when the twelfth
juryman failed to appear because he had gone
to church on Christmas Day, and the clerical
feeling thus created made him think Xmas was
Sunday, so he fancied that Tuesday was Monday.
When church-going so upsets a man’s mentai
equilibrium, it seems to argue the necessity for
total abstinence from that brand of stimulant.
“Surgeon Fitzgerald's daughter is the only
Sassiety girl represented at this year’s exhibition
of the Melb. Artists. Two merlioere landscapes
are hung under the name of ‘ Ethel Fitzgerald ’—
one depicting ‘ A Rainy Day near Doncaster,’
which is a part of the country surrounding the
T itzgerald rural residence. John M‘lntyre opened
the Exhibition of Vic. Artists last Thursday with
the news that lie wasn’t an artist. He followed up
this great truth with several doubtful utterances
commencing with ‘lf I might say,’ ‘I may be
allowed to add, I venture the remark,’ and sun-
dry ifs and probablys.’. One satisfying thought
extracted from a long circumlocutory utterance
was his promise that he would endeavor to tap the
Government for a little better support than the
annual hundred pounds at present doled out to the
Artists Society.
lhe latest gem in the form of head-gear has a
tricky scarf dangling a fringed tail over the left
side of the nm. For a while, one only saw them
on picket-duty in the windows of ‘chic’ milliners •
but during the recent heat-wave trippers of the
Flock have flaunted them extensively. The over-
hanging silk fringe acts as a sort of winker to the
left eye. It is the quintessence of sauciness.
Saint Hilda has lost its queerist identity in
Barney Blair, the Queensland aboriginal boy,
whom Mrs. Dr. Blair adopted long ago. Every-
body knew Barney by sight, if not by name—a
short, curly-haired, flat-nosed and very black
dandy, taking his walk abroad in patent-leather
boots, and supported by a white gal on either side,
9 s a , ru , lhe summit of a good many young
females ambition was attained when they had
been seen strolling down the Esplanade on Sunday
with Barney, for he was notorious as well as odd-
looking. lhe story went that eccentric Mrs.
Elan adopted the grinning blackling (during her
husband s lifetime) for spiritualistic reasons. She
took the youngster for the re-embodied soul
of a deceased nephew or something, and treated
him accordingly— sent him to a grammar school,
and made a ‘little gentleman’ of him. Barney
was said to be almost out of his teens when his
aimles i career came to a full stop. Probably he
died of the conviction that he was out of place in
tips world, and would be better off elsewhere.
Tlis school-mates gave him an excellent character
tor amiability and generosity, and so forth, but
they were all agreed that poor little Barney hadn’t
enough heart to keep him going.’ Difficult for a
woman to get at the exact meaning of ‘ heart ’ in
men. If it means the quality that keeps them
going, one could wish that many of the creatures
were more heartless than at present.
“Bondon Trvrrcs correspondent George Morri-
son, while staying with his relations, the Higgins
family, in Victoria, related that there is a bronze
statue of the Queen in Burmah. He heard a
native criticising it, and expressing his astonish-
ment to find that the Queen was a black woman !
Re Bittle Kit Columbus’; there are scores of
smart, 5 dressy, showy matrons exactly like ‘Mrs.
Block. Good-hearted women as a class, though a
hard, knowing facial expression is characteristic of
the type. Sheridan’s antics with the train of his
yellow evening-gown are realistic. Certain human
birds of paradise must always flaunt their volumes
m observer s very face.
Suburban’s high-flyers, blessed with smart
petticoats, are not content to advertise their ad-
vantages by silken rustle. In railway compart-
ments homeward-bound from the theatre o’nights,
municipal marms turn up their dress-skirts to the
waist, and smooth their satin slips over their
knees with a smug complacency calculated to
worry she-onlookers. Also, their massive ankles,
neat as new pins in silken hose, &c., are spread
forth for inspection.
Sheridan s trick of hitching his rear draperies
recalls the contortions of women some months ago
when pockets had gone sheer to the back pre-
paratory to abdicating in favor of handbags. A
local tram-conductor, I’m told, lost his temper
one day because a lady kept him waiting ever so
Jong while she stood up anti burrowed and grabbed
d °-T\ back for her P} ,rse - At last the minion
said, When you’ve quite done scratching your-
self, mum, I’d like your fare.’

HALF-SICK
PEOPLE.
Just sick enough to feel heavy-headed,
lazy, and shiftless; to have no appetite;
to sleep badly ; to have what you eat feel like lead in
your stomach. Not sick enough to take to bed, or
to call a doctor, but sick enough to not know what to do.

The Bulletin has received the prospectus
of the Linnean Memory Lessons from the author
and teacher, Mr. R. Brown, of Sydney School of
Arts. Mr. Brown does not claim to do any
miracles. The memory, like the biceps or the
muscles of the hind legs, can be improved and
made much stronger by proper exercise. And
Mr. Brown claims to have the kind of exercise
that will do this. The Bulletin has not tried the
system, and what it is constantly on the lookout
for is a good system of forgetting things—mostly
the things it sees in the Daily Telegraph—but as
far as can be gathered by a glance through the
prospectus there is heaps of exercise in it.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A Gosford (N.S.W.) family named Legge
is alleged to have sent five sons to the war. A
genuine case of giving the cause a leg-up.
A beautifully-dressed male who recently i
arrived in Tas. from Melbourne, wore, on the
breast of his coat, the Union Jack, Stars and
Stripes, and a photo of General Buffer.
Rev. D. S. M’Eacharn, of Melbourne, re-
membered for his awful zeal as a heretic-hunter in ,
the Charles Strong case sixteen years ago, celebrated
his holy jubilee the other day. M’Eacharn has
been ordained Presbyterian for half a century, and
is stiff on the look out for heretics.
Miss Agnes Macready, the first Australian
nurse to go to the front in S. Africa, is daughter of
the Rev. Henry Macready, one time Moderator
of the Presbyterian Assembly. She studied
surgical nursery under Dr. Fitzgerald, of Mel-
bourne, and was lately matron of Bowral (N.S.W.)
hospital.
Major-General C. H. Smith, who is re-
ported from London as commanding the Austra-
lian forces in the front in S. Africa, fought against
the Boers in 1881. He was second in command
at Laing’s Nek and elsewhere. He led an
Egyptian expedition in 1891, and altogether did a
lot of heavy work.
Dr. Lalor, of Orange, N.S.W., now wait-
ing his trial, at Ballarat, Vic., on a charge of trying
to shoot an auctioneer in his rostrum, is the only
surviving son of Peter Lalor, of the Eureka
Stockade riots, in ’54. Peter had a daughter, but
she died before her father. Those two were all
his children.
The warrior’s return is sometimes a sad
spectacle. One N.S.W. town supplied a solitary
hero for the war ; he was presented with a medal
by a local member before leaving, and farewelled
at a big public meeting, and hundreds of people
and a band saw him off at the railway station.
Then a few weeks later he came sorrowfully back,
sacked on account of beer.
Thomas Hodby, who died in Adelaide this
month, was one of the very few soldiers who
survived the wreck of the troopship Birkenhead,
off the South African coast. Hodby, then 19
years old, got into the water at the final plunge,
and never knew how he got ashore. To show his
thankfulness he straightway took up the white
man’s burden and went shooting Kaffirs.
Of the late N.S.W. Speaker Terence
Murray’s two sons, the English-settled one is
among the Boers’ warmest champions while the
Australian one has gone to lead the N.S.W.
“ Irish regiment ” to the slaughter of the South
African patriots. True, the one was always
noted for brains and the other for muscle. It is
the one who is noted for muscle who is off to the
war.

“Hairpin”: A story of Abb£ Schurr,
referred to in a recent Bulletin. Some years ago
a young Italian priest, ill-acquainted with En-
glish, was sent up as junior to the Abbe, and at
the end of the month Schurr handed the young
man a cheque for his salary, quite forgetting that
he himself had, the day before, drawn at the bank
a considerable amount of money to pay his ex-
penses to Sydney. The Abbe left for Sydney
early on the day the Italian presented his cheque
at the bank. It was not paid, and for the next
few days the priest wandered round the township
calling the forgetful old gentleman
‘ ‘ tief, ” “ robbare, ” “ burgulare, ”
“ scoundereel.” It was a miracle
that “Ned the Baker” —a rale
Italian lad from Oireland —did not
“stouch” the slanderer of the
beloved old priest, but the Italian
father’s accent was so complicated
that most of his language wasn’t
comprehended of the masses.

Apparently there are some real ringers
offering for the Bushmen’s Contingent. One last
week nearly fell down when asked if he was a
bushman. “Why, dog-scratch-me,” he said with
a shriek of anguish, “ I’m gidyah bred, straight
from the Culgoa and no scalawag neither, but a
right-down silvertail. Can I ride ? why I rode a
ZR filly last week until the hide came off with the
saddle, and as for track, I’d track a bug to an
aboriginal.”
“ Majah ” Gordon, the eye-glassed S.A.
commandant, goes to the gold war with the
local contingent as special service officer,
retaining his salary of £5OO a year. Gordon has
been absent from S.A. continuously for the last
three years, but had sufficient influence in high
quarters to keep his billet, although the work was
done by Major Stuart at half salary. Gordon
owed his appointment mainly to the good offices of
Lord Kintore.
Poor old Q. politician Groom has been in
a sad state of collapse since his shoddy god Drake
seized the £lOOO a year. The broken-up patriarch
has been in Queensland Parliament since the year
one, or thereabouts, and hasn’t managed to strike
a portfolio all the weary time. He isn’t only the
father of the House, but its grandfather and re-
mote ancestor, Noah, as well. If Drake had taken
office with Dawson during the late crisis, he might
also have got a place for his faithful follower, but
now that James has left his party, the Remnant
isn’t worth buying.
Dr. Alexander Morrison, of Melbourne
Scotch College, was a student at Aberdeen Uni-
versity with the late Governor R. Hamilton, of
Tasmania. Among the Victorian “boys ’’ who
have passed through his hands are Trea-
surer Shiels, late Chief Secretary R. Ramsay,
Justice Hood, Professor Andrew Harper, and W.
Bruce and R. M'Leod, the cricketers. The first
boy to enter the college when it was founded in
1851 was J. D. Law, now manager of the Bank of
Victoria.
“J.L.” reminisces on a small brown
politician:—
Ex-Premier Turner (Vic.) served his articles to John
Edwards the younger, who would slaughter a man who
wrote “ junr.” after his name. John’s father was an old
Tasmanian settler ’ who went over to Melbourne and
became managing clerk to his son. The practice of the
“Oollingwood Boy,” alias the “Native Companion,”
would have gone to the dogs at an early stage but for old
Jack, who did the fatherly bully in the office, and George
Turner, who was the good boy of the firm—reliable and
irreproachable. Turner was born middle-aged and dead
serious.
Dr. Fitchett of “ Bleeds that Won the
Empire ” fame, represents in his new book “ How
England saved Europe ” that Arthur Wellesley
(afterwards Duke of Wellington), was a brilliant
success in arms from the start. As a matter of
fact he lost his first battle in India, but his brother,
who luckily happened to be Governor-General of
India at the time, promoted him and gave him a
fresh start. When the defeated soldier was asked
by a friend what he would have done had he lost
his second battle, he replied: “I should have
hung myself on the nearest tree.” Probably he
never said it, but it was said for him anyhow.
Sam Mauger, commonly, but wrongfully,
called “Mogger,”the new member forFootscray
(Vic.), is a hatter, and is the second of the trade
that has been in the Victorian Assembly of late
years. The other was T. Smith, 'member for
South Melbourne. There will now be a hatter
and a shoemaker (Billy Trenwith) in the Labor
corner, and if tailor John M ‘Mahon at last gets m
for Fitzroy, the comer will be able to rig-out the
whole House, if it only gets the job. Also, the
House will feel very uncomfortable when it is
rigged-out, for Trenwith’s boots are said to be
lamentable.
Indignant loyalists in Melbourne are
writing to the papers to say that something hard
should be done to Jack Murray, M.L. A. foi
Warmambool, whose mild speech in opposition to
the second Contingent is felt to be a great sin
especially just now when there is a demand that
freedom of speech shall be suspended. But nobody
does anything to the big man from Warrnambool,
and nobody will—if he knows what is good for
him. About a year ago in the precincts of Parlia-
ment House somebody did try to remonstrate with
Murray. He got home suddenly on the member s
nose and made it bleed. Murray simply fell on
him, spread him out on the floor, and holding him
on his back, allowed his bleeding nose to drip upon
him from head to foot until the assailant looked
like a pantomime demon. Nobody has seriously
remonstrated with Big Murray since then.

Last week Melb. Argus announced the
death of R. W. Shadforth, ex-P.M. at Wanga-
ratta, and said he came to Victoria in the forties
as Associate to “Justice Burton,” when Justice
Willis was “transferred.” Burton was never in
Port Phillip or Victoria. He sat in Sydney, and
nowhere else. Shadforth came with Judge
-Jeffcott, who was the man that succeeded Willis.
And Willis wasn’t “ transferred ”at all. He was
removed from the Bench finally, for misconduct.
.The par. mentions in an offhand way that Mr.
Shadforth married an admiral’s daughter “many
years ago.” It was a trifle of 55 years ago—in the
year 1844.

Occupying a respectable position in N.S.
Wales at present is a man, under 30 years, who
spent six years as a leper in N.S. W. lazaret. The
disease germs died out while he was in the lazaret,
but he carried back to the world and still exhibits
evidence of the mutilations the leprosy—' was it
leprosy ’—caused. The case is encouraging, inas-
much as lepers and their families are so generally
afflicted with a sense of the hopelessness of re-
covery.
Reitz, Transvaal Secretary of State, is of
German descent, and years ago, while holding an
official position in the Orange Free State, he
founded Ithe Afrikander Bond, which afterwards
swallowed, or was swallowed by, Hofmcyer’s
Farmers’ Protection Society. Later on he became
President of the Orange Republic, and while he
occupied thav position he took a trip to Europe.
In Holland, Belgium, and France he was received
as the head of a sovereign State, which he un-
doubtedly was. Then he crossed to London,
where a bull - headed Government absolutely
ignored him, and refused him the recognition it
extends to German princelings who are not even
sovereign, and who hang on to territories not a
quarter the importance of the little Orange Re-
public. This studied insult made for Britain a
most valuable enemy, and was an item in a train
of circumstances which will cost many millions.

A VOICE FROM THE BUSH.
m
'JAmctW
Whenever I read The Bulletin, with its dis-
loyal gutter sewerage, 1 spit on it, trample it under
foot, and then burn it. There is only one thing
poor Paul Pry is proud of, and that is his country
—and when he hears that country called a nation
of cut-throats, murderers, robberi, and sweaters, it
riles him, The Bulletin doesn’t believe in a God,
and thinks the world was created by the devil. It
is “agen ” everything. It calls its minimi informa-
tion “ The Wild Gat Column,” and assumes that
every mine is a wild cat and the directors a set of
scoundrels. It never spoke of a good action of any
man in its life without assuming a corrupt motive.
There is one exception to its universal ‘ ‘ slinging
off” and that ivas the Suffield case. Suffield was
convicted of a crime, and so The Bulletin had a
sneaking regard for him. The case gave The Bul-
letin an opening to libel the police, the law authori-
ties, the Government, and the female sex as lasci-
vious perjurers. I would not like to know very
many of the ladies with whom The Bulletin
associates, and on whom it founds its notion of the
female character. Its right place is in Russia, as
the official organ of the Nihilists. There it could
have its Uing. Issued in some lonely cave, we can
imagine it being read by groups of desperate men,
who had sworn to assassinate the Czar and bring
about the reign of chaos. But I fancy the Nihilists
wouldn’t have it; they’d be afraid their organ would
turn against them. Perhaps The Bulletin might
shift its plant and staff to Hell. 'There it would
find a world such as it imagines this to be. But
there is one objection to th is. Satan is beginning to
be very jealous of The Bulletin, and two such
great men would not work well together. Then The
Bulletin, if it got to Pandemonium, would com-
mence to boss round, say how things ought to be
done, and call Satan, Belial, Moloch and Cos. a lot
of “ old fossils.” — GIPPSLAND MINERS’ STANDARD.
Bill’s brother had died, and a faithful mate
had nursed him tenderly. Said Bill, between
sobs and sighs, to express his gratitude to that
faithful mate : “By Gord, Jim ! I hope your
brother will die soon, and I’ll sit up and nurse
him.”

Dearß.—The trees in the jungleshere have
a peculiar fancy for having their roots half out of
the ground. The trunks at and near the ground
sometimes form themselves buttresses of the most
fantastic shape which wind sinuously among the
undergrowth, apparently roots, but quite indiffer-
ent about getting underground. Walking along a
timber-track through the bush the other night I
trod on a huge snake. Jumped wildly into space.
Struck a match —although bright moonlight in the
clearings it was dark in the jungle—and cautiously
crept back to find the snake was a tree-root on top
of the ground as usual. Half-an-hour afterwards
trod on another “ sarpint.” No mistake this time
felt the brute move. Another match—another
root . ! Just before reaching the clearing trod on
a third, purposely this time, as I could dimly see
w ia*- was Just about full of this root business.
Holy Moses ! a carpet snake about 10 feet long !
He made a “ dinny-aiser ” at my leg, but fortu-
nately did not allow sufficiently for the extraordi-
nary rapidity of my exit into the clearing.— Tweed
-tvIVER.

“ W.M.F.” : Re black- trackers. What is
probably one of the finest bits of tracking ever
seen in Australia, if not in the world, was done
near Brewarrina, two years ago. The weather was
dry. Two swagmen looted a hut 60 miles from
town. Three days later the trackers—two pro-
fessionals—were on the spot, after 38 points of
rain had fallen. No white men, not even the
best bushmen, could see the sign of a footmark.
The darkies started in the morning ; next evening,
they had discovered the stuff up in four different
hollow trees, about a mile and a-half from Bre-
warrina.
* * * *
“ X ” : Last night my office companion, a
huge tarantula, was mute and motionless on the
wall as usual. Below, a red cockroach, over an
inch long, was slowly moving upwards, his long
feelers playing first one side then the other. Won-
dering how they would greet each other, I gently
touched the cockroach’s near-side feeler with a twig
and luckily gee-d him over straight for the spider.
Gradually the distance lessened until the long 3-
inch feelers swept gently over the tarantula’s out-
spread foreleg. The next moment they were on
the floor in a death embrace. Watched the tar-
antula bearing the apparently stupefied cockroach
home behind the lining of the wall. Have often
seen him catch flies, but did not think he would
tackle cockroaches.

Dear Bulletin. —An incident as to clear-
ing-out of stores from a flour-mill at Molteno,
South Africa, by General Gatacre’s troops, was
reported in the London Daily Mail, of sth
December last, in the following form, viz.:—
Sterkstroom, 29th November, 6 p.m.—Our patrols are
in touch with the enemy. There has been no forward
movement on the part of the Boers, a number of whom
have been seen to the north of Molteno. A very large
quantity of flour and wheat at the mills at Molteno, in
danger of being commandeered by the Boers, was safely
taken out to-day by General Gatacre’s troops. The Irish
Rifles and the Berkshires went out early, and ran the
stock in five trains very smartly into camp.
About six weeks later (January 12th), the same
incident appears to be reported in the Melbourne
Argus, and other “leading dailies” here, in the
form of a cablegram just received from London :
London, January' ll.—Reports received from Sterks-
troom, the headquarters of Sir W. F. Gatacre, show that
constant touch is kept with the enern.y, and that the
General continues aotively engaged in reconnoitring
the Boer positions.
Information was brought to Sir W. F. Gatacre that the
Boers intended to raid Molteno, 22 miles north of
Sterkstroom, and that their object was to capture a large
quantity of flour stored in Molteno Flour Mills. Sir
Vv. F. Gatacre determined to be beforehand with the
enemy and despatched a strong force from Sterkstroom
for the purpose of rescuing the stores. The movement
was successfully carried out and the flour was brought in
safety to Sterkstroom.
What does it all mean ? —J.B.
* * * *
We have no native cats on the Tweed.
Poultry-raisers have only sparrow-hawks and
carpet-snakes to contend with. Occasionally, if a
gang of Hindu cane-thrashers are camped handy,
a mysterious leak will occur among the chookies.
It can, if noticed in time, be checked by the well-
timed- discharge of a blank-cartridge. Kanakas
'ire also fond of fowls, but never steal.
The little sparrow-hawks will call daily
for a chicken if they can get at the
motherless incubator-hatched broods.
But carpet-snakes are the worst
’ trouble, particularly in summer—
they lie almost dormant in the
“ A GAY GORDON .”
winter. A full-grown carpet-snake will swal-
low eight or ten chickens, and come back in a
few nights for another lot. He will also surround
a full-grown fowl, but usually comes to grief over
it, as, although he almost instantaneously throws
several strangling folds of his body around his
victim, the fowl manages to give two or three
squawks and flaps of its wings. Its mates, too,
are generally disturbed during the death struggle,
and the farmer or his wife comes round with candle
A QUIET CHRISTMAS DAY AT THE
FORTY-MILE (Q.). ,
“Peace on earth and good will towards men.”
and axe. A carpet-snake killed by me a few
nights ago, when cut open, disgorged a lot of
eggs, with two china nest-eggs among them !

Harry Stockdale, re black-trackers :
The belief of the Queensland Police that in a reoent
oase one of their black-trackers deliberately put them on
a false scent because he was himself the culprit recalls
the long-past case in which Fanny Briggs, barmaid of a
bush hotel near Wiseman’s, was outraged and murdered
by black-trackers. When the girl was missed, the native
police followed the tracks of some naked feet to a briga-
low scrub, in which the body of the victim was ultimately
found. The corpse told its own tale, and the trackers
soon made it appear that the crime was the work of the
myall (or wild blackfellows), with the result that a large
party of these were attacked and 50 or 60 shot down.
Soon afterwards, however, the trackers, when mad-
drunk, so ill-used their gins that one of these
unfortunates told the white police-officer “All about
them fella bin kill-’era white Mary (white woman). Then
bin put-’em along-a scrub.’’ The strict questioning
which followed convinced the officer that his own men
had done the deed for which the unfortunate myalls had
paid the penalty. Another batch of black police was
brought upon the scene, and the murderers were dis-
armed and shot. It would be wise to remember, ere
placing too much reliance on native evidence in cases of
outrage upon women, that a pretty general, marriage-
custom amongst the aboriginals involves a carnival of
lust and violence by four of the intended husband’s male
friends prior to the handing over of the “ bride ” to his
keeping.
» * * •
Pigs are perhaps the most out-of-the-way
pest that ever were turned into a squatter’s plea
for rent-reduction, in N.S. W., anyhow. The sta-
tion, not a big one, is in the N. W. ; it was com-
plained half-a-dozen years back that it was over-
run by brutes which consisted mainly of bristle,
tusk, and grunt. Mobs of them are numerous
over a big area out there—as many a teamster
who has found creek waterholes polluted beyond
approach can swear to.

H. J. M‘Cooey on the weary snake-
jumping problem :
Re snakes jumping (“Pelican,” 8., 30/12/99). livery
Australian schoolboy knows that the death-adder jumps
when tormented, and that it will follow its assailant a
chain, or even rpore, in a series of jumps, some of which
cover 6ft. and are very rapidly performed, until the
enemy (unless he, or it, be particularly quick and lively)
is overtaken and bitten—not “ stung,” mind. The “Q.
nigger” referred to by “ Pelican ” is not an authority on
snakes, except only in so far as they concern his melon-
shaped “bingee.” All venomous Australian snakes can,
and do, jump upwards and backwards into the air ; but
no other snake except Acanthophis (mtarctica can pro-
ceed forward in a series of jumps. Re speed of snakes.
I’m not too slow when a snake “ goes for me,” yet I, for
one, should be very sorry to have to depend upon
“ shanks’s pony ” to save me from an enraged brown,
black, or tiger snake down a dry-grassed incline with an
angle of even lOdeg.—not to speak of 15deg. or 20deg.
Supposing the distance to be 10 chains and the angle of
inclination to be lOdeg., I should consider myself sin-
gularly fortunate if I escaped with less than 10 bites.
Near Blayney, N.S. W., in 1887, I saw a smart young man
24 years old pursued, overtaken, and bitten, after a hard
run of about 80 links, by a superb tiger snake (Denisonia
superbus, Gunther), which he had quite unintentionally
cut in half with a scythe while mowing hay. In the
interests of science, I object to the tin-pot pretenders—
the jackals of science—who assert that our largest
venomous snakes, such as the brown, black, and tiger,
are incapable of overtaking an ordinary runner.

“A.E.L.”: Sweet are the uses of advt.
While driving along Woodburn-Chatsworth road
(R. River) overtook an Indian arrayed in the
usual dirty calico head-gear, filthy shirt, and dun-
garee pants, across the seat of which appeared the
legend, in bold type, “Read the Freeman’s
Journal ! ” The thrifty Oriental had borrowed a
oalico poster from a friendly gum-tree to cover his
nakedness.
• * • *
A Townsville paper tells how Mr. Frank
Jardine, when residing at Somerset, C. York, went
across to the Barrier Reef and found there a small
ship’s cannon of curious antique make, also some
cases of Spanish silver and gold coin lodged in the
reef. These latter had become encrusted with
coral and cemented into a block. The Spaniard
has never received full credit for all he did in
opening up the coastways of Australia.
AT SANKEY’S PUB.
Rtood his bluey in the corner, and his patient, vinous eye
YVas the optio of a mourner who’s just watched his
mother die.
I have never seen one quicker
In disposing of a liquor,
Down his funnel like the flicker of a candle coursed the
beer,
And he thirsted like a fiend, and spread a superstitious
fear
Round the drought-beleaguered shanty,
Hoskin reckoned it was Dante
Back from all the realms infernal, squaring matters now
and here.
Still the simple, silent stranger took his drink, and paid
his tin,
And they whispered of a danger of his drowning from
within ;
But he stayed, a thirsty suitor,
Passing in his empty pewter,
Till the fusel-oil diluter said : “ This pub.’s run dry, I
think.”
Then the other rose, and damned him with a curse that
made us shrink.
“ That’s the second I ” cried he. “ Sankey,
Is there in this Land of Blanky
Any public that can give a man a — nice—long—drink ? ”

Oom Paul, it is solemnly alleged by the
unspeakable cable fiend, has taken to dressing bis
spies in kilts. The Mackruger’s gathering ?
Even the knife-grinder has the war-fever
on him these times. His old business yell is now
altered to “Any scissors, knives, or sivords to
grind to-day ? ”
Wherever the British flag floats there is
absolute freedom of thought and speech. The
following Vic. news item (one of very many)
comes in just here :
Merino, Wednesday.
The proprietor of a. large steam thrasher from the
Sandford district started out to-day. When engaging his
men all those having Boer sympathies were summarily
reiected, and he boldly announced that his machine
would sail out under British colours, with the Union Jack
floating at the engine-funnel. His exhibition of patriotism
was heartily cheered.
Query: How much boldness does it require to
hoist the British flag on a threshing machine m
a perfectly peaceful country ?

Kuppers Elberfeld Lager
This is worthy of the “Arabian Nights.”
At a camp on a Tasmanian West Coast railway-
line recently, there was such a big razzle-dazzle on
that towards night the beer in all three shan-
ties ran-out. Early next morning, when
“ coppers be expected to be very
“hot,” shanty-keeper No. 1 filled an
empty barrel with water and left his cabin
for a few minutes, whereupon No. 2 took
the opportunity of sneaking the cask of
supposed beer, which he sold to No. 3
for £2. Then two navvies, cleaned-out
and suffering badly from drought,
watched their opportunity, got posses-
sion of the barrel and planted it to
await nightfall. They had, however, i
been observed by a Scotchman working
on an embankment close by ; and he, as
soon as it was dark, got possession o£
the barrel, and to avoid discovery rolled
it through a culvert about 200 feet long,
against a strong stream of water, emerging,
after half-an-hour’s labor, wet and panting,
but jubilant over his prize. Then he gathered
in his cronies to partake, and the disgust
and disappointment may be imagined when
out of the much-prized barrel flowed the first
pannikin of dirty water.
“Y.” ; Crude practical jokes and drink
are the joys of the far-back W.A. goldfield. Last
Christmas we celebrated in the only way possible
—in hot and muddy beer. Brown acquired his
load earlier than the rest and collapsed ; so he
went (or was taken) home and put to bed. Then
we hired the black man who sells pies to bury
Brown for ss. We told him Brown was dead, and
showed him the tent where the corpse lay breath-
ing heavily. Dead men and pies don’t mix well
together in most places, but little things like that
don’t trouble the wild, uncultured West. All that
afternoon the black pie-man dug and delved out
Brown’s grave under a hot sun, and then he went
to perform the simple obsequies, whereupon the
defunct woke up and smote him frequently. Then
we had more hot beer, and Brown chased the
black pie-man into the bush, and we laughed the
cracked, lost-soul laugh peculiar to the West. After
that the sun went down, and all the world was
dark and smothery like a hot black stove-lid in
Gehenna.
* * * *
“Blister”: Emus have a good big wide
taste, embracing everything solid or fluid.
Writer took two to England a few years ago in
s.s. Gulf of Genoa, and one morning they got
away with three sets of oilskins hung up near their
pen. Broke loose next day and absorbed two
buckets of good green paint—wanted fresh coats
for their stomachs maybe; anyhow, they were
none the worse.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: Notice that some people are
still agitating for rabbit-proof fences —one of the
greatest fallacies existent. But for these fences,
at the present time, there wouldn’t be a rabbit in
the dried-out Western districts. They would
travel north to the Gulf country, where, if they
didn’t voluntarily disappear like the rats, they
could be easily cornered and exterminated. Rab-
bits always travel north, and some western squat-
ters who have noticed this are opening their fences
to let them out of the paddocks !
“ Malle” : Our Ah Cow at
Bingellie was a single Chow.
We hinted that a man possess-
ed of a commodious residence
such as his was simply wasting
without a wife, but Ah Cow
repudiated this with scorn,
saying, “No feah—too muchee
talkee; p’laps get um dlunk.”
To test him, we rigged up a
local imp of darkness, one Jim,
and a small mate of Jim’s, in
female clothes, and they hap-
pened along late in the after-
noon and inquired the way to
Albury. Finding they had no
menfolk, John became interest-
ed and hospitable : “Wha’ fo’
you go Albelly ? W elly long
way Albelly. You come insi.
You have cup-a-tea? Where
you stay to-ni’ ? ” After a coy
refusal, the damsels walked in,
and the billy was set boiling.
Ah Cow didn’t take kindly to
Jim, but to the other he re-
marked, “You welly ni’ gal;
liowoloyou?” Ti en he pin-
ched her cheeks, reiterating his
admiration, and wound up with
a proposal that she should stay
there, and he’d mally her —on
some future occasion. He was
proceeding to put Jim outside
to make for ‘ 1 Albelly ” alone,
when two large blackfellows
rescued the distressed damsels
and chased the amorous hea-
then off the premises. Soon
after the soul-blighted Ah Cow
discovered Jim’s connection
with the fake and wanted to
fight. Jim was about 17, strong
and supple, and had learnt all
that a pretty good district
boxer could teach him, but he
wanted no hostilities. The ag-
gressive celestial insisted, rush-
ed, and was promptly downed.
Somewhat surprised, lieessayed
another go with like results.
This was too much. He bolted to an outhouse,
brought a long-handled shovel and an adze, and
offered to continue the discussion thus : “ Me no
savee fightem you countly fashion; me takem
shovel, you takem adze, fightem my countly
fashen.” But Jim declined to argue on these
terms, and quitted the spot in haste. It was a
glorious victory for Ah Cow, and he was so in-
flated over it that he raised the price of cabbagee
50 per cent, on the spot.
Dear Bulletin. Heard two sundowners
seated on the verandah of a, pub near Orange con-
versing about the war. This was the opinion of
one of them: “ W’y don’t they finish ’em orf ? W’y
’cos they wellcawn’t. The beggars is that ob-
stinate. W’y Gawd blime, when Lord Methuen’s
push give ’em such L in the trenches do you think
the beggars would give way? Not much! W’y
even when they was dead, killed by this Lydd-
ite, they wouldn’t fall, the English had to push
their corpses over. They’re such obstmet’
beggars those Boers, they’ll stand upright when
they are well dead. ” Then this military genius
was interrupted by beer.
THE FATHERLY SMALL BOY.
LITTLE BILLY (patronisingly): “Now, then, mister, he’s ready, but I’ll give him a run round to quiet him afore
you gets on him.”
From all the Australian warriors in South
Africa comes the same sad moan, per private
letters to their relatives and friends. They don’t
get enough meat to keep them warm. Probably,
they are better off for tucker than the average
Boer, and will have to look cheerful on a 50 per
cent, reduction anon, but meanwhile they feel the
inconvenience of fighting for Hingland with ‘ ‘ rats
inside,” and regret to find that Gflory doesn’t
spell three square meals a day.
“ LADYSMITH" TAKEN.
AS PER UABLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They blew us up with dynamite,
They rained down shells with hades filled,
It was a weary awful fight—
And one of us was nearly killed.
The enemy were millions strong
And were by hopes of murder thri’led ;
We slaughtered them the whole day long—
But one of us was nearly killed.
We charged up hills ’mid deadly rain
(The stoutest heart it would have chilled),
We slaughtered all of them again—
And one of us was nearly killed.
The bullets flew so very thick
That like a plough the ground they filled ;
We slew the shooters very quick—
Yet one of us was nearly killed.
A bullet struck one hero’s side.
And through his coat a hole it drilled ;
And now we point to him with pride—
He was the one so nearly killed.

TO THE STREET-BRED JINGO.
You blatant, street-bred braggart, lay your ears back for
Yowbhfnk’you go to Heaven, all others go to Hell;
You shriek about your valor and about the things you do,
And the men who go and do them laugh in their sleeves
at you.
Your Jingoistic Kipling, he’s skiting for you yet;
He writes your Jingo screamers and he writes Lest we
Your great and vasty Empire that you blow so much
Is mostly dirty heathen with its shirt-tail hanging out.
You civilise with bibles and you civilise with shot,
And the heathen that you can’t convert you blow them
. all to pot. , . ,
You hold a wondrous Empire of water, men and sand,
But beware that Empire creeps out from underneath your
hand.
He’s an absent-minded beggar ;
But he won’t forget to pay
For the needs of another in khakki,
Whose gone to where Boers hold sway.
He’s an absent-minded beggar
And (but its rather hard to say)
He might have a son whose beastly hard luck,
Decreed that he should stay.

A somewhat delicate question was raised by Mr.
Moule. Under the Constitution Act, if a member
be sworn in as a Cabinet- Minister he becomes an(
Executive Councillor, and as such is entitled to a’
free railway pass for life. He may remain in a
Government only five minutes, but once a member
of the Executive the fiction is preserved of retain-
ing him in that position in perpetuity unless be
resign.

Sleep When Work is Done.
During the late summer (1896) I passed several
weeks in a foreign city nearly 700 miles further
south than London. We had about ten days
intense heat, and for six nights when it was worst,
I do not think I slept as many hours altogether.
The resulting nervous collapse was simply awful.
My brain reeled and I could neither understand nor
do anything rightly. I walked, as the good Book
says, “in a vain show. ” What a blessed thing is
sleep ; how destructive, how killing to lose it.
In healthy sleep the nervous system is inactive
—more especially that of the brain and spinal cord.
The body lies quiet, the muscles relaxed, the pulse
slower than when we are awake, and the breathing
less frequent but deeper. Then nature —wise
keeper of the House of Life—proceeds to renew
the energies expended through the day. To tniss
this renewal, even in part, is to tread the edge of I
insanity and look into the mouth of death.
“Methought I heard a voice cry, ‘Sleep no |
more; Macbeth doth murder sleep!’” So said
Macbeth himself; but there is a power which
murders more sleep than ever did Duncan’s
assassin ; and to that Mrs. Beattie alludes when
she says of her daughter, “ She got no sleep.”
“ In May, 1891,” writes the lady, “my daughter
Letitia fell into a low, weak condition, and could
not get up her strength. She had no appetite,
and what little food she took gave her great pain
at the chest and side. She got no sleep at night,
and became very nervous and low spirited.
“ As time passed by she grew weaker and more
feeble, and we thought she would never get better.
A doctor attended her but none of his medicines
seemed to suit her case, and for two years she
continued to suffer.
“One day she read in a book about Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup and what it had done for
others. She got a bottle from Mr. Nicholls,
chemist, Donegal Place, and after taking it began
to improve, being able to eat, and her food
agreeing with her. After having used this medi-
cine a short time she was strong and well as ever,
and has since been in good health. For a long
time I myself suffered from weakness and indiges-
tion, and seeing what good Mother Seigel’s Syrup
had done I took it and was completely restored to
sound health, You may use this statement as
you like. (Signed) (Mrs.) Mary Beattie, 21,
trinity Square, Belfast, August 14th, 1896.”
“In the early part of 1894, ” writes another, “ I
began to suffer from indigestion. I had a poor
appetite, and all food gave me pain at my chest
and a gnawing feeling at my stomach. No
matter how light the food I took i had pain and
sick headache,
“For over a year I was tormented like this,
when Mr. John Weir, living in this place, told me
how he had been cured by Mother Seigel’s Syrup,
and recommended me to try it. I did so, and
soon all pain left me, and I have since enjoyed
good health. I know many persons residing in
this district who have often been benefited by
taking the same medicine. Often in my shop I
hear persons say what it has done for them. You
are at liberty to publish this letter. (Signed) Alex
Wilson, General Merchant, Castlewellan, New-
castle, Co. Down, Ireland, Augs*15th, 1896.”
Indigestion or dyspepsia destroys the power to
sleep by starving, and thus weakening the nervous
system. Indeed the whole body starves, and is
thrown into profound disorder. Food may be
taken into the stomach, but. as people constantly
say, “ It does no good. ” No ; but in a sense it
does harm. Fermented, soured, undigested it
develops poisons which act upon the system some-
what as a pestilence does upon a community,
flesh is lost, strength gives away to trembling
weakness, courage is supplanted by fear, and life
is dreary and desolate. That Mother Seigel s
Syrup stiould be able to cure a disease so common
and so terrible is reason for gratitude to us all.
For the healthy work and sleep.
A Common Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
< < j” .. .
“Yes, mother! It’s no use ! I’m going. I’m
sick to death of this. Just look at it. Drizzling
rain on a dull grey plain isn t anything cheerful,
Iis it ? ” ,, , «*
“ But Liz, what would you do J
“Oh mother, I’m all right.”
, “ You don’t know, Liz.” .
“ Yes ! I can take care of myself. There isn b
a man round here but knows chat.”
“ You haven’t wanted for anything, Liz ?
“ No ! but you have—and besides I want to go
out into the world, to live, and make lots of
money.” . , T • ,,
“ You might have married, Liz.
mother. I’ve had three glorious offers.
Keroseney Smith, who smells like a lamp-rag;
Jim Brown—drunk every other day ; and lom
Collins, who owes more money than he 11 make in
a thousand years.” _
“ But there was another, Liz ! ’
“ Dofi’t speak of that, mother ! He s gone. It
was my fault. I sent him away.”
“ But, Liz, I’m —your —mother, — and the two
were crying together ; one for the loss of her only
daughter —trim, neat littleLiz, who had brightened
the lonely bush hotel; the other for a youth-com-
panion, lost by pretended heartlessness and girl-
frivolity, now slaving somewhere in the red-dust
country of the West.
“Now-now, mother —it’s no use. Im goin —
and listen, mum. I’ll come again ; and when I
come, ’twill be with lots of money—and you and
I will live together, where there’s plenty of life
and go, and stacks of fun.”
So Liz made one of the white-faced crew on the
crest of the Westward Wave.
• . • *
“Liz!”
There was the clink of glasses and a rush of
oath-punctuated conversation; the glare of gas-
light on the sanded floor; the rattle of billiard-
balls ; the drunken guffaw ; and all that goes to
make a fashionable hotel in W. A.
The speaker looked keenly into the, girl’s face.
“ You ! When did you come ? ”
“ Just a minute, Harry. What’s your poison,
mister? And yours ? Two whiskies. Yours?
Soda-and-b.”
“ Who’s the favorite now, Liz ? ”
“-You —of course.”
The conventional inflection on the last syllable
told Harry that Liz had learned a lot since he had
last seen her, arid yet he couldn’t quite associate
the Liz of his remembrance with such a scene as
this.
“ A ’’—she looked in the till. “Take
it in there,” pointing to the office door.
“Oh, that’s all right! Keep the change.”
Liz shot a glance at him that satisfied the
watcher’s suspicion—now a certainty. The £5-
note went into her pocket, and the old comradely
Liz advanced to greet her former lover. They
shook hands and talked—with distrust on his
side, reserve on hers ; and a chill seemed to fall
on the memories of the past. To him it seemed
as if he were reading a stray leaf from an old, for-
gotten book. And she—she wondered what he
thought of her—seeing her there. She could ex-
plain it all—would he believe her?
Impossible. He knew the temptations of a bar-
maid’s life ; and as he walked home he called him-
self a fool for thinking Liz could be other than the
rest. All the way home one idea beat time to the
measure of his thoughts—“ I may as well ’
* * * *
There is a little house on Mounts Bay Road
where they lived together as man and wife for
just six weeks —glorious days to Liz, too drunk
with Love to mistrust her self-made God; too
blind to see the grim callousness that had crept
into his heart; and too dull to discern the gradual
repulsion that grew day by day and blossomed
into a hatred. “ With me ! ” he reasoned—“ then
with anybody else ! ” The end came when he left
for the goldfields without telling her—and Liz
went into the world again to earn her own living.
Money —and mother —and peace lay ahead ; and
she would reach all three.
• • • *
She came on board the. boat at Fremantle, a
helpless, shrivelled creature, thin-cheeked and
hollow-eyed. The few returning turned from her
in disgust, and even the more brutal found in the
pale, faded, red-eyed woman nothing worth wast-
ing civility on. Day after day till Cape Borda
was sighted she came on deck alone, and gazed
thoughtfully into the sea—debating the notion of
jumping overboard. The sea was honest, at any
rate. It was always cold and cruel. Men were
only cruel.
J * * * *
It was night when she came back, and she felt
like a stranger when she knocked at the door of
the hotel. Someone opened it, and she stepped
in. There were lights burning—and so late ! Her
mother always went to bed early. Instinctively
she guessed. Her hands went to her head and
she staggered into the room. The faces of the
neighbours sitting there died away in a red mist,
and through the mist gleamed a pale face, the face
of her mother.
“ She took to her bed a fortnight ago, ’ someone
whispered. “ But the doctor says it may be days
yet.”
Sobbing heartbrokenly, Liz fell on her knees
beside the bed, and someone whispered audibly,
“ Thank God ! ”
Vic.
L.M.D.

“C.”: The different “funds,” in Tas-

Dear Bulletin. — Sydney tea-man Inglis,
in a letter to the S. M. Herald, suggests that, as
>he term bush man refers in S. Africa to the miser-
# degraded black dwarfs styled Bosjesmans, the
N.S.W. “Bush Contingent” should be styled
Australian Rangers. What’s the matter with
Australian Bushrangers ? —Gun.
It is a pity that great Dutch military
organisation, the' “Beggars of the Sea,” which
•ought so desperately against the Spaniards a few
ienturies ago, isn’t on deck still to take a hand in
<he present war. A battle between the Absent-
Minded Beggars and the Beggars of the Sea would
oe a Homeric conflict, and a subject to gladden a
poets’ soul.
Maoriland Premier Seddon won’t be drawn
on the subject of defence. “As to how many
rifles we have or how much ammunition there is in
stock, I do not consider it a wise policy to disclose,
and I am not going to be forced into divulging the
information.” Which is probably about as much
as any one wants to know. When the supply is
so low that it isn’t safe to mention it, any in-
terested party can guess the rest.
“Tas.” : Those in the know say that Q.
Premier Philp will rush the first opportunity of
tiring Drake out of the Ministry. He has got
Drake out of the Assembly and buried him in the
Upper House. The fallen one has now no
Assembly seat to go back to, and no party to go
back to. Having blasted Drake’s character, lost
him his seat, and broken up his party, why should
Philp keep on buying Drake ? What has Drake
left to sell ?
London Standard, overjoyed by the folly
of Australia in sending men to fight the Boers,
desires this country to be represented in “the
regenerated British army.” If the regenerated
British army will pay privates 4s. 6d. a day, same
as the local contingents are paid, and feed them
on a scale of great magnificence, doubtless the
3ritish army can be recruited in Australia—just
as it could be recruited on the same terms among
/he impecunious or adventurous of Greece or Peru.
3ut the Standard’s dream of an Australian
Tommy Atkins, thankfully fighting the battles of
:he empire at a ha’penny an hour or thereabouts,
is merely a gay vagary of the Jingo imagination.
A lot of the charges made by-
interested or other parties against

rail through oftener than not. One

cent, all round. This looks heavy

.and all oharge far heavier rates.
Four of them (Queensland,
Westralia, Tasmania and Maori-
land) levy 20 per cent; or more.

oer-centrduty organ of Vic-toria,
aasbeen especially bitter about
she iniquity of the Boer Govern-
anent’s or 11 per cent.—which
is like that paper’s usual ina-
oility to recognise the psychological
moment for taking refuge in a dry

A couple of months ago Sir
■John Gorst told the House of Com-
mons that “A Committee of the
London School Board estimates
r ,hat, at times when there is no
tpecial distress, 55,000 children, in
* state of hunger whichmakes it use-
less to attempt to teach them, are in the city of Lon-
don alone.” Bull is so busy redressing the griev-
ances of the people in Johannesburg that the cry
of the children passes almost unheeded. Yet
here is more pathos in the empty stomachs of
these 55,000 hungry mites than in all the woes and
alleged woes of all the gold-bugs in Africa.
People are shouting “Rule Britannia”
and “ God Save the Queen ” and. swearing that we
are living under the freest constitution beneath
the sun. Yet some insane individual suggests in
he; public press the re-enactment of Jimmy
Martin’s “Treason Felony Act.” Under that
direful piece of legislation a man in a country
;own (Goulburn) was prosecuted for not raising his
hat in a public-house bar when some intoxicated
iout called out “God save the Queen.” At the
srial the prosecution fell through because the
Grown Prosecutor could not find the copy of the
Act. It had been purloined.
Most of the useful unfinished measures of
the Turner Ministry have been thrown overboard
by its successors. At one recent sitting of Vic
Assembly the Coal Mines Bill, Abolition of
Usury Bill Old Age Pensions Bill, and Boilers
inspection Bill, were dropped in a heap. They
were all strongly democratic measures and each
One promised to a & reat want, but the Shiels-
M Bean Ministry hates difficult and intricate
matters.
N.S. W. members of Parliament perspired
freely during the enrolment of the second S
African Contingent. Humpbacked and bow-
legged volunteers who found a difficulty in getting
into the list rushed their Parliamentary represen-
tatives for a push on, and in view of future
elections the representatives interviewed persons
high m authority, and demanded and implored
that a more generous view should be taken of
their constituents. Some of them are said to have
been reasonably successful too.
Is it quite the correct thing that almost
the only industry directly subsidised by the O.
Government, viz., the sugar manufacture as
carried on m Central Mills, should be a very large
employer of alien labor. It was shown in the
Assembly the other day that the Mosman Central
Mill, to which it was proposed to vote £12,000.
had indented no less than 100 Japs., while the
Mulgrave Central Mill employed Japs, almost
exclusively The Govt, should make the grant
to Central Mills conditional on the employment of
white labor.
J . Harcourt Palmer, the pushful Great
Western (Tas.) railway boomster, is standing for
Parliament confessedly in the interests of his syn-
dicate. The man of the Gridiron Railway has an
eye to business and a shoreless sea of assurance.
He has decided to try for New Norfolk, the Tas-
manian Callan Park. He even asked the sitting
member, George Leatham the Silent, resign in
ms (Palmer’s) favor. Leatham’s rep.'v is unre-
corded, but it is feared that his reputation for
silence is no more. Even a hermit of 40 years’
standing m his cave would say something under
the circumstances.

John Forrest is still talking loosely up and
down the east coast of Australia to the effect that
if the eastern provinces will agree to the insertion
m the Commonwealth Constitution of “the amend-
ments demanded by Westralia,” he will call
Parliament together and get the measure passed
lu for .Westralia to be an Original State of
the federation. Westralia never demanded these
or any other amendments—it was never consulted
And the Westralian Upper House not only
rejected the proposed amendments bodily, but
refused to sanction any negotiation with the other
provinces on the basis of these amendments. What
is the good of a big man shambling up and down
the East saying he is ready to call Parliament
together and get it to pass what it has already
rejected ?

Captain E. T. Miles, of Strahan (Tas.)
Marine Board contracts celebrity, publicly an-
nounces his candidature for a Hobart seat at the
forthcoming Assembly elections. Says he has a
complete (though rather long-delayed) reply to the
awful condemnation of the Parliamentary special
committee which last year forced his resignation
from the Braddon Ministry and from the House,
and brought Braddon down in a heap after him.
Present writer’s hero since boyhood has been a
bulldog-ant he once saw fighting a stone-crusher.
He now begins to think the stone-crusher was
perhaps struggling against odds that were heavier
than they looked.
Victorian Premier McShiels says that he
doesn’t see any necessity for reducing the dimen-
sions of the already overgrown State Parliaments
AT THE SUMMER CARAVANSARY.
“Aren't they beastly familiar !”
Yes, you'd never think they were married. ”
after the Federal Legislature has taken more than
a moiety of their work off their hands. As a bid
for office this will take some heating. The
McShiels Goverament says, in effect: “All other
leading politicians propose reducing the State
Parliaments, whereby you will lose your dignities
and your salaries. We are the only crowd who
promise, if retained in office, to preserve to you
your dignities and salaries. Therefore it is clearly
to the interest of one-half of you to support us. It
is also to the interests of the other half—the half
to be sacrificed might be either half.”
Hobart Mercury is easily first among
Australian dailies for frantic jingoism. It re-
printed Balfour’s well-remembered speech the
other day, and editorially proclaimed it as the very
last word to be said in justification of Britain’s
share in the war. The passage particularly
stressed in the Mercury man’s leader was—
If the Boers had altered the franchise law the war
would never have occurred.
A correspondent (W. A. Holman) described Bal-
four’s speech and the Mercury conclusions as
‘ ‘ ingenious sophistry. ” Then the Mercury raved
in a two-column leader about the little Eng-
lander’s unmitigated ignorance, disloyalty, &c.,
and reiterated the statement that there would
have been no war if the Boers had altered the
franchise, and added :
The war was inevitable, because the Boers had resolved
on it more than ten years ago, as their warlike prepara-
tions prove.
Holman is now waiting behind a log until the
Mercury editor goes to sleep again. Then he
will slip another letter under the office door, ask-
ing how an inevitable war could have been pre-
vented by any alteration of the franchise. After
that the Mercury editor will have an apoplectic
fit and other furniture.
Glassey, the one - time democrat and ex-
jeader of the Queensland Labor Party, is fast
going down-hill. If he isn’t offered a portfolio in
the Boodle Ministry very shortly there is no
gratitude in man or beast—especially beast. Here
is a bit from a recent speech of Glassey’s in the
House
Mr. Glassey seconded the resolution. He concurred to
the full with the sentiments expressed by Mr. Dickson
with regard to the splendid manner in which our volun-
teers had behaved and distinguished themselves. When
the news arrived he was sure the hearts of the people
greatly rejoiced. He agreed that the ramifications of the
fund should extend to every part of the colony. ... He
had written to the Courier, making the suggestion that
employers of labor and the heads of various departments
in the public service be requested to take action to
endeavor to procure one day’s pay from every person.
Those who had no employers might donate something
from their incomes. In cases where persons could not
spare a day’s pay in one amount the contribution might
be extended over several months.
This is a time when work is scarce and wages low.
If a movement like this starts, it will undoubtedly
be a brilliantly successful piece of blackmail, for
every workman who refuses to pinch his wife and
children by giving one-sixth of a week’s pay will
be liable to suspicion of the unutterable sin of
“pro-Boerism,” and that is now the shortest road
to the sack. The spectacle of Glassey, the one-
time worker, devising means for blackmailing
what was once his own class in the interests of the
capitalists of Johannesburg is a pathetic spectacle,
and if Glassey doesn’t get his reward there is no
thankfulness in man or beast or Boodle.
The theory that Britain’s unreadiness for
war proves that she didn’t contemplate making
war on the Transvaal if the demand that the
suffrage should be granted to-
aliens was not complied with,
is a very weak proposition at
best. The same argument
proves that the Greeks didn’t
make war on Turkey a few
years ago ; that France didn't
declare war against Prussia
in ’7O ; that neither the
United States nor Spain made
war last year, for they were
both lamentably unready ;
that Britain didn’t declare
war against Russia in the
Crimean days ; that Prussia
never dreamed of the Jena
campaign ; and a lot of other
similar things. Britain may
not have contemplated war,
but her unreadiness doesn’t
prove she didn’t.
Melbourne papers are
now satisfied that Australia
has nothing to fear from
Russia. Formerly, when the
jingo press had an Imperial-
istic axe to grind it yelled
“ Bear ! ” till it was hoarse,
and there were served up
every morning midnight
visions of hordesof Muscovites
swarming south, provisioned
with bags of tallow, and lust-
ing for loot. It was a cold
south-wind day when the Czar
was not prepared to throw a
million troops into Melbourne
and Sydney, scoop out the
banks, and depart with all
our gold, our household gods
and natural advantages. But
now when “ our defences ” are
scattered over far Africa, the
Russian is our dear brother,
the Jap is not to be thought
of seriously, we are out of the
reach of Germany, and the
Lord has delivered us from
the French, and from envy
hatred, and all uncharitable
ness.
Apparently the Lyne (N.S.W.) Ministry
intends to be largely a Public Works Ministry,
earning support by a liberal shovelling-out of
borrowed money. It has called for tenders,
among other things, for the Sydney-North Shore
bridge, and that will-o’-the-wisp of 30 years’
standing looks as if it might at last become a
reality. If Lyne proposed to pay for this largely-
unproductive work out of revenue, or even to
attach a sinking fund to the loan that would
ensure that the structure would be paid for before
it wore out, his idea would be most commendable;
but nothing even dimly resembling that has, so
far, been heard of.
Brisbane Telegraph still plugs away dis-
mally at its dismal little yarn that the Federal
referendum showed an anti-Federal majority in
Queensland. In its leading article on 28th De-
cember it got this remark off its narrow chest: —
The result was a vote nominally for the Bill, but the
voting against it represented 83 per cent, of the total
white population.
Now, if the vote against the Bill represented 83
per cent, of the total white population it must
have represented at least 75 per cent, of the total
population. Will the twaddlesome parochial
organ explain how it comes to pass that the vote
could have been even nominally for the Bill if the
vote against the Bill represented 83 per cent, of
the white population? How can less than a
quarter of the population form a nominal ma-
jority ?
More Melbourne Age :
What would be the fate of representative citizens of
Alsace who in another Franco-Prussian war should be-
have as the Schreiners, Hofmeyrs and Sauers have done?
If M. Ie Marquis de Galliffet were instructed to suppress
rebellion in the north of Cape Colony, would he not
use the very effective methods of repression he adopted
in Paris after the outbreak of the Commune, when he had
tens of thousands of non-combatants shot on suspicion in
cold blood ? Would a Skobeleff hesitate to let loose the
Boer-hating blacks on the country behind the Boer
fighting line?
For a paper of its wealth and standing, the Agb
is pitiably inaccurate in its history. The Pro-
visional Government certainly went through the
Communards with a long sword and a hard heart,,
but the “tens of thousands of non-combatants”
who were “shot on suspicion in cold blood,
is a piece of ignorance which would disgrace a
small school-boy.

Just when (apropos the war, of course) ex-
cited jingo Australians are denouncing “ blanky
Irish” and blankier Germans for their alleged
war views, it is profitable to sort out the
heroes of Eureka Stockade —that uprising in de-
fence of British freedom and fair-play whereof
Australians are so proud. There are 21 names on
the monument at Ballarat. Of these 10 have
“Ireland” after them; three others of the men
were Germans, one was a Canadian Frenchman.
Only four were from England or Scotland.
A small Collins-street (Melb.) hotel-bar
opened a list for the Absent-Minded Beggar,
and a well-known pastoralist, who had been talk-
ing himself hoarse over his loyalty to the empire,
was asked to contribute. And then he turned ok
his loyalty at the meter and uttered an immortal
truth. ‘ ‘ Let the men who’ll benefit by the war
do the paying,” said he. Whereupon the loyal
barman called the pastoralist “a crimson Boer,”
and the man with the deadhead affection for the
Empire left that particular pub. for ever.

With the holiday season coining on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, &c. —at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (back of Paling’s), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rates.*

into consideration in building Red Birds.

* * • •
In these days of numerous “stiffs” in everv
race, few events pan out like the Steward’s Mile at Satur-
day s Canterbury meeting. In a field of 16, all the best
nags were genuinely backed, and the field wheeled into
tne straight like a troop of cavalry. Bar a straggler or
Passe <l the stand thus, the front rank being
f/n tv. T u e ,9 r^ er ’ Good - b ye, Eulong, Annerlieand Lenton ;
and the half-dozen, under the whip, battled out the finish
Byde just getting his nose past the post first, and inches
only separating the others. A finish of this sort is worth
crossing the province to witness.
* * * •
Giimeboy W of the Flying Handicap, on
baturday, is the handsomest and one of the speediest
little horses seen on a Sydney course for some time He
comes of an unfashionable sire—Goodfellow. He is a
Drown colt, small, but a real model in shape, and with a
y i e la ot . moving that takes beating, and his six furlongs
in 1.16, in above race, with 7.9 up, proves that lie’s a hur-
ned mover as wen. He beat a really good field easily,
and, bar accidents, he should have no trouble in getting
himself up among the top-weights. *
* * * •
Sydney’s present collection of hurdle horses is
a sorrowi'ul-looking lot. Several of the seven starters for
the jumping event at Canterbury on Saturday looked
as though they’d been taken out of cabs to make up a
field, and even the winner, John Kidd, isn't the sort of nag
a horse-thief would take many risks to obtain John
who is an aged pessimistic-looking bay, was a good
favorite, however, so the public didn’t object to his
beating the washy Condobolin nag, Brokerage, and
Kelso s Stellarane, although the latter was well backed
and, with luck, should have won. Of the other starters
Aboriginal, a goutv-legged old black, fell over, whilst an
indescribable quadruped named Aberdare cantered feeblv
and arrived by the time the books were paying out.
An application has been made to the A .T 0
committee to re-open the case of Frank Fielder’s 'three
years disqualification over his riding of Mora in the
Christmas Handicap at Rand wick. Perhaps no local racing
case of recent occurrence has caused as much comment
amongst racing men as the one in point, and the general
reeling- is that the punishment was excessive, in face of
the many barefaced cases of roping which occur at the
metropolitan head-centre, and are passed over as coming
under the category of “ reversals of form.” Enquiries
under the A.J.O. are held in camera, but it is an open
secret that the owner of the mare proved his bona-tidex bv
showing that he had backed the mare. Moreover it was
also shown that Fielder’s father had solidly backed his
son s mount, and an outsider would imagine that evidence
which stood for the owner would be equally good for the
jockey, but the A.J.O. thought otherwise, and passed
upon the crack rider a penalty of three years, or practi
callyWe, forattheend of the period of disqualification
Fielder will probably be too weighty to ride. In the in-
terests of the sport the case requires a thorough re-
sifting. °
