A horse called Boer won the Transvaal Stakes
(War Relief Fund) at meeting lately.
George’s River (Sydney), adjacent to the spot
where Larry Foley and Sandy Ross decided their two his-
toric battles in the Seventies, was on Monday mornin«-
the scene of a scrap which was the talk of turf circles for
three weeks past. An ex-jockey (eldest of a family of
prominent riders) and an individual commonly known as
“Curley,” with a reputation that the George’s River
affair proved to be wholly undeserved, were the princi-
pals. The match was the outcome of a dispute on the
Moorefield racecourse between a well - known trainer-
owner and the jock, in question as to whether Skein Dhu
was “on it” that day or otherwise. The “barney’
ended by the jockey offering to meet any middle-’
weight who had not appeared in public as a
fighter. The match was for £lOO a side. Though
a lot of betting took place the affair was kept
very quiet. One noted punter, who has himself figured m
the roped square that is called a “ ring,” wagered an even
£2OO with the backer of “Curley.” The convincing
ground—seven miles up stream—was reached in a launch
which left Sans Souci at daybreak. When the pair toed
the mark, the destination of at least £BOO hinged upon
the result. There was no ring. Conditions called for 2-
mm. rounds with £min. spells. Neither could fight worth
a cent apparently, though the jock is said to be a good
un. “ Curley ” got home on the stomach with a beauty
that shook the recipient up, but latter secured first
blood a little later by means of a left flush on the mouth.
Curley ” experienced a torrid time in the second round
getting his head cut with a heavy right and being the un-
grateful recipient of two or three from the same member
on the “ point.” The last of them stretched him out dead
to the world. Otto Cribb, though no champion, could
hck a paddockful of either.
* * • .
“Old Timer”: The huge crowd (fully 2000) at
interprovincial Tweedie-Peach fight in Metropolitan A C
(Sydney), last week, supplied convincing evidence that"
given the opportunity for viewing a contest under any-
thing like comfortable conditions, the game would com-
mand every bit as big a following as it did in the days
(ten years ago) of the old York-street club. But a few
more such affairs as that referred to would settle all
interest in pugilism for a good part of a century
Peach was palpably short of condition and went
to pieces in the sixth round. lie stood before Tweedie—
reputed a second Griffo—as confident as any man could
be, and time after time got left hon.e on the stomach and
right on the ribs or head without return. Often, too, he
left himself palpably open in various ways, but
nary an effort did the clever Tweedie make to take
advantage of these chances. Then came a series
of stage falls in round seven. From mere taps on
the face Peach would drop to the boards as if knocked
limp, turn round on his elbow until the referee counted
8,” and rise again to go through the same performance
once more. At last his second. Bill Jennings (wily old
chap), cast the rag- into the atmosphere. The go
was a bitter disappointment. If it was fought on its
merits, then the principals were extremely unfortunate
in having appearances so decidedly against them.
* * * *
“ H.”: Middleweight champion pug of the
provinces, Tim Murphy, scored his third successive
victory at Broken Hill (N.S.YV.), Saturday night, by
knocking Queensland crack 11.4 man, Archer, out in the
seventh round. A punch on the solar plexus did the
trick. There’d be big money in a match between Tim
and the mother province crack middleweight, Mick Dunn,
and they’d want a huge structure like the Metropolitan
A.O. (Sydney) to hold the crowd. Murphy contemplates
leaving for Yankeeland in a couple of weeks.
• * * *
Hegarty has made a new departure in pugilism.
He has been giving semi-private full-dress rehearsals, so
to speak, at Seale’s Gymnastic Club, for the gratification
of sundry Ikey s and others of the sporting brotherhood.
He knocked large sections off Alf. Stoneham, out of con-
dition, and much below championship form. Then he
took Wally Weekes on and Wally finished with a substan-
tial bit in hand. Otto Cribb, the M.L. irresistible, was to
have followed, but Tim somehow made a mistake about
the time they had to meet, whereat Otto was chagrined
in various languages. The cognoscenti are considerably
exercised in mind as to what the Hegarty party is at, and
cute ones among them say “ ware hawk,” under the firm
conviction that the game is to influence the betting
against Tim, who, they insist, invariably holds a lot up
his sleeve. Others, however, will have it that he shows
the best that is in him every time, and indulges in the
willing business for no other reason than to keep his hand
m. Writer inclines to the first opinion.
• » • «
Much interest has been excited over feather
premiership boxing struggle to take place in Sydney
Monday night. Melbourne man Tim Hegarty’s crowd
insists that he can’t lose—that the “go” is a “moral”
for him, and he looks like starting a red-hot favorite.
Sydney crack (Napier) has followers who are just as confi-
dent, but they don’t talk so much. When two “ morals ”
meet, obviously one of them must be more or less de-
moralised.
* * • *
Mons. Victor has returned from the West,
fatter and more prosperous than he has ever been in his
life before, and eager to back Bill Doherty in a return
match against Felix. “ Mons.” was an official in the last
fight, and received an incidental punch on the nose him-
self in the middle of a willing rally. He says that
Doherty owes his defeat to an accidental collision with
the nigger’s knee, which cut open his cheek to the bone.
He admits, however, that Felix did some hitting—a thing
that the long man has hitherto managed to get through
his fights without.

“Crank”: Saw a par. in Sydney D.T. t’other
day to the effect that Australian pug. Herb. McKell was
in Canada going to give a boxing exhibition with someone,
after which he intended engaging in mining at Kloiidyke
with erstwhile Maitland (N.S.W.) blacksmith bruiser
Paddy Slavin. Boxing exhibitions are slippery Herb.’s
fame, lie’s not taking any chances at the “willing”
usiness if there’s a possible get-out. His fairy tales are
very amusing. Not long ago he left ’Frisco with a! big
blare stating that he purposed placing himself under Kid
McCoy’s mentorship and taking on all and sundry-
champion 11.4 man Tommy Ryan for choice ; but Herb,
seems to have skipped all this (probably because Tommy
'V al .d? c * the o° orl the level) and is now telling another
thrilling tale to the Canadians. It’s any odds he’ll fight
nobody if he can get out of it. We saw that here when
challenges from Ned Burden and others were ignored
There’s no fear of Herb. McKell taking the Klondylce track
with Paddy Slavm either. It’s too rough a road, and there
aren’t enough comforts when you get there.
* * * *
Looked at in the light of latest news from the
land of the birch and pine-tree ham, that fight between
Australian Bob Fitzsimmons and Yankee Jim Jeffries for
the world’s supremacy was not altogether what it pre-
tended to be. Recent negotiations for a second match
between the same pair set one thinking. Jeffries won’t
consent to another meeting unless he gets 75 per cent, of
the purse—win or lose. Fitz is content to let the winner
take all, which, in face of the fact that he was apparently
badly beaten, is rather peculiar. Comes to mind now
that some papers at the time said Fitz. fought as if he
wanted to be licked, and in the 11th round actually held
his jaw out, as if it was a Christmas box, to get the
decisive punch on the point. Will be remembered, too,
that Australian veterinary dentist “ Billy ” Miller, upon
returning to Sydney a month or two since, said a fishy
odor pervaded the whole business beforehand, and after-
wards the loser (Fitzsimmons) bought a theatre in
Chicago. Taking a line on Fitz’s fight with sailor Sharkey
and the latter’s showing against Jeffries, the present
champion of the world should have been an easy thin°-
for the man he took the title from.

Balmain Swimming Club again secured N.S.W.
Flying Squadron team supremacy at Bondi (Sydney)
carnival, Saturday last, beating Otter and Bondi by zo
and 22yds. respectively. Other teams (West Sydney,
East Sydney and Newcastle) failed to survive the pre-
liminary heats. Individual members of winning quintette
got over their different 100yds. as follows, and in the
order given : Grant, 18 4-5 ; Proudfoot, 17 ; Murden,
170 3-5 ; Byers, 170 2-5 ; Craig (220yds. champion of Aus-
tralasia), l - 5 4-5. Full time of team, 572 3-5. This is
5 2-ssec. faster than when same club won last year. The
personnel of the team was identical with that of Satur-
day, bar Murden, who took Bennett’s place.

There’s a lot of veiled professionalism about the
Australasian amateur. ’Tis said that two M.L. pot-
hunters who assisted at recent A.A.A.U. championships
in Q. have forwarded claims for wages during their
absence from home. A Sydney champion sprint swimmer
got his usual screw (while training) from a club at whose
meeting he was to swim, and the A.S.A. saw no harm in
it.
» * * *
The three crack 22-footers, Effie, Keriki and
Plover, met for the Sydney Sailing Club’s Championship
on Saturday last. The wind was fluky in the extreme,
and after a tiresome drifting match the boats just finished
before the stipulated 7 o’clock. Effie, the crack of the
fleet, had the worst of the flukes and finished last, Keriki
drifting home some 2tmin. ahead of Plover.
* * * *
“ Dick ” Green, a Sydney forty-year-ago cham-
pion sculler, showed himself very much alive last week in
a letter to a Sydney daily, when he offered to bring out
his four sons to row any four amateurs from Victoria
The four young ’uns may be chips off the old block, but
history shows as a rule champions do not beget cham-
pions. Champions at all classes of sport expect their sons
to follow in their footsteps, and start the youngsters at
their respective games as soon as they can crawl, but it’s
no go. Champions are born, not made.

In Sydney Electorate First Grade Matches, on
Saturday last, Central Cumber lands continued their
winning’ career, defeating North Sydney by 86 runs. Pye
with the bail took Quist’s and Duff’s wickets for no runs ;
and Howell did most of the rest of the damage. Pad-
dington (v. Bedfern) closed their innings with 6 wickets
for 417 runs, and as Redfern had no show of winning they
stonewalled and made a draw. Bur wood closed their
innings at 272 for 6 wickets, and disposed of Glebe for
153; Waverley beat South Sydney by 9 wickets; and
Bast Sydney was beaten by Leichhardt.

Cycling isn’t always a paying game in Aus-
tralia, and bad management has much to do with it.
One league recently had to consider the complaint of an
investigating committee that the racing hoard had issued
over 13J0 complimentary tickets! The attendance at the
meeting was under 5000 for the two days.

For down rig-lit ignorance some country racing
stewards capture the crumb. At a recent minor horse-
gallop in M.L., the judge, not liking the result, disquali-
fied a winner right there on his own motion, and on his
own private and personal complaint lodged by himself in-
formally with himself, and awarded the stake to the
second horse. Being remonstrated with, he told the
crowd to cast itself into the eternal ash-barrel below, and
P, 01 „ out that the rules provided “ the judge’s decision
shall be final.” And final it had to be.

M.L. hack trouble seems to have been satis-
factorily settled at last. Hitherto, once in the hack ranks
a horse that could distinguish himself in big company
remained a “ hack”-—if his owner so desirecf—and won
when he liked. Racing conference recently decided that
hacks winning one race worth £BO or races ag^re^atin^
£l5O would have to join more pretentious "company!
Whereupon clubs giving prizes of £BO and over at once
lowered the stake for the benefit of owners who did not
want to go out first pop.

Late news from ’Ome is that strong man Samp-
son, who had the loan of Sydney and Melbourne early
last year, reached London about the middle of December,
lie told an interviewer that at one of his performances
here he broke a blood-vessel in the abdomen and was in-
capacitated for seven weeks ; at St. Petersburg his hand
was torn, and at Paris, in September, he dislocated his
knee-cap. Sampson is said to have introduced a new
turn. He supports a huge revolving globe (in which are
seated 25 men) on his shoulders. How is it done ?
* • • *
After being told fully four months ago that
champion cueist John Roberts had retired for good be-
cause his sight was failing him, word comes from down
under of a big achievement on his part quite recently.
At the Egyptian Hall, London, the old marvel conceded
8000 in 21,000 to Cecil Haverson. Going to the table 13
times John aggregated 942 in lj hours, during which
period llaverson only totted up 272. Roberts scored
some remarkable strokes, and evidenced all his old deli-
cacy of touch and knowledge of strength and position.
Often he essayed shots of exceptional difficulty, apparently
for no other reason than that he was tired of making the
easier ones. Nursing cannons came to him like Hrassey
falling off a horse, while his hazards are said to have been
very brilliant.
* * * •
Sensational six-days’ “ twin’’-cycling grind, at
Madison Square Gardens (N.Y.), wherein competitors
were arranged in pairs—each riding 12 hours out of the
24 daily—concluded Dec. 9. Winners were Miller and
Waller (a German-American couple), who together covered
2733 2-5 miles. Maya and M’Eachern (two Canadians)
were second with 2733 1-5 miles. Gimm and Pierce
(Americans) occupied third place, having travelled 2733
1-10 miles. An amazingly close finish. During the ex-
citement, which is described as intense, a man fell out of
the second tier of seats and broke his neck. Nineteen
teams started, hut only seven finished. Best individual
score was credited to Walthour (an American), who got
over 1402 4-5 miles.
• * * •
According to jockey up, “short of a gallop ” was
the cause of Major George’s Seahorse being defeated in
M.L. Great Northern Derby. Writer has often felt amused
at this excuse ; also that other one—“ a gallop too much.”
Trainers usually try to make it appear that they have
their business down to a science, though most of them
merely drift into it through being too heavy to
ride. Writer has had a long experience, and can remem-
ber few trainers who could tell by appearances whether a
horse was a bit out of sorts or not. He does a trial, is
backed on the strength of that trial, and, failing to run
within seconds of it, is often allowed to go out next time
comparatively unbacked. Then he, perhaps, romps home.
Trainers forget that horses, like men, have their days
when they don’t feel up to the mark. The man can ex-
plain, but the horse can’t, and the trainer, being only a
man who is too fat to be a jockey, doesn’t know. °

George Giffen holds a world’s cricket record.
On one occasion he took all ten wickets, and then, goin"
in first, batted right through an innings, remaining not
out. This was some years ago, for the Norwood Club on
Adelaide Oval.

Speech of one of the South African con-
tingent, farowelled at Hurstville (Sydney) last
week, was very amusing. Following is a verbatim
report of portion of it, delivered to a packed
house:—
Well, chaps, I’m goin’ ter fight for me Queen. When I
reads in the newspapers (and after all we have ter go to
the newspapers for information) how them Boers slaughter
innercent wimmin and children me blood biles, and I want
ter get at’em. I'll go straight for old Krueger; I'll go to
that place where he is now—what d’ye call it? Well,
never mind wherever it is—l’ll go there and get ’old of
im, and bring’im back in a cage. (This with a wither-
ing look in the direction of a crying youngster.) I wanted
to go to the war before, but somebody—l don’t know
who now—persuaded me not to. I’ve passed several
tests, this time, and made up me mind that if I
wasn’t accepted I'd get aboard the ship and address the
people of N.S.W. on the subject. If anyone has any.
thing against iii-e, or if I've done anyone an injury , now
is the time for them to come forward, and I'll shake them,
by the 'ami aiui forgive them from the bottom of my
’art." Wild cheering from all parts of the building.

Is it Kru-ger, or Kru-yer, or Kru-er?
’Tis a name you must learn to pronounce ;
When you start to impute things impure
Against Kroo-jer, or Krugger, or Crow-er,
It’s as well to be certain and sure
That you the right party denounce,
Whether Kru-ger, or Kru-yer, or Kru-er—
’Tis a name you must learn to pronounce.
Tis a word that has troubled us sore,
Of the seeds of dissension a sower—
Is it 80-er, or Boor, or Bore ?
That’s the word that has troubled us sore.
Shall we rhyme it with moor or with more?
Or mate it with “ Land of the Moa?”
That word that has troubled us sore—
Of the seeds of dissension a sower 1

Safe Security.
A well managed bank, in making loans, requires
security. The well managed railway enforces rules
to guard against accident. The thrifty farmer
fences the field, even though law prohibits cattle
from running at large. Those who ship gold in-
sure against loss. Those who build “fire-proof”
buildings insure against fire.
.The cautious man will also take an inventory of
himself occasionally and see if disease in any form
is threatening. Disease, which (if treated at its
inception) would be harmless, unless checked,
threatens life; and the security against liver and
kidney disease is Warner’s Safe Cure.
This is not an unguaranteed statement. It is
simply the verdict of those who have tried it. It
is safe. A Sure Security against liver and kidney
disease, and can be taken under all circumstances,
ki all conditions, at all ages, with perfect safety.
A PERSONAL EXPERIENCE.
The following experience made a profound im-
pressnm among the many friendsandacquaintances
of Mr. Ivempton, Electrical-Engineer to the Mel-
bourne City Council, G Baldwin St., Malvern, Vic.
He says: “I feel it my duty to testify to the
splendid results of Warner’s Safe Cure and Pills.
Alxiut eight years ago I was taken ill, and tried
different doctors. They did me no good, and for
a fortnight I could not turn in bed. The doctor
informed me that I had kidney colic. I had been
reading one of your pamphlets previous to this,
and I t ought I would give the Safe Cure a trial.
A friend of mine procured me two bottles and one
vial of pills, and before I had finished the second
bottle I was up again, and it drove a lot of gravel
out of my system. The doctor seemed to have no
faith in the Cure, and said I would be troubled
with the same malady again in two years, but I
am very thankful to say that I have never had a
return of it since, and I think eight years is enough
time to prove the cure. I have recommended it
to others, with the best results, and shall continue
to do so. In fact, I think it is the safest and best
medicine under the sun, and am confident that it
saved my life. Wishing you every success.”

A. G. Taylor, ex-M.L.A. (N.S.W.), who '
died last week at Callan Park, practised a Theo-
dore Hookish joke once on the Bathurst P.M., ;
Benjamin Lee. This gentleman lived on the Vale
road, a couple of miles out of town. One morning,
early, a number of furniture vans and carts
streamed up in front of the house, and, making
inquiries, Lee found that they had come out to
remove his furniture to a new dwelling. The
drivel’s were much disgusted, and tolerably pro-
fane, on being told that Mr. Lee was perfectly
satisfied with his then residence. On the way
back, the vanmen met butchers and bakers,
drapers and grocers, shoemakers, dustmen, and
undertakers, all pouring towards the Vale-road
residence, with a fixed resolve to do something or
other for Benjamin Lee immediately. The road
was choked with traffic, and a maddened P.M.
was out looking for the gore of parties unknown. I
But it was months before he knew who caused
the trouble, and by that time he had cooled down.
The eagerness of the M‘Lean Shiels Gov-
ernment to appoint its dangerous enemy, Deakin,
as a Federal delegate to Loudon, has led to a fresh
uprising of the Provincial spirit. Lyne also wants
to send away his dangerous ally, Barton ; but
having some shreds of sense he has called a con-
ference of Premier’s to get Barton appointed as a
representative of all the federating provinces. If
two delegates were to be appointed, Barton and
Deakin were practical certainties in any case, but
by rushing things and playing a lone hand the
McShiels Government has only created aggrava-
tion. Also it has created the impression that its
red-hot haste arose out of a desire to get Deakin
hustled on board ship, with his comb in one hand
and his clean shirt in the other, before Turner’s
censure motion (when he is expected to play a big
part) eventuates.
IMPORTANT SALE OF
CITY PROPERTY
With Extensive Frontages to
Pitt and O’Donnell Streets,
Comprising those magnificent buildings now in the
occupation of the BANK OF NEW ZEALAND.
Owing to the Bank’s intention to remove its offices to
George-slreet, the above Valuable
Double Frontage Freehold
Will be offered for sale by Public Auction.
On the PITT STREET frontage are the Bank of New
Zealand premises of stone and brick, and in the true
lonic style of architecture. The accommodation con-
sists of a spacious Banking Chamber, Strongroom,
Boardroom, Manager’s, Accountant’s, and nine other
rooms, and is most suitable both in site and internal
arrangement for occupation by an Insurance, Ship-
ping, or Trading Company.
If desired to increase the office accommodation on the
Pitt street frontage, the building is so constructed
that it could easily be added to.
On the O’CONNELL STREET frontage is a substantial
and attractive block of office buildings, known as
Nos. 27, 29 and 81'O’Connell-street, built of brick,
cement front, and containing basement, GROUND
FLOOR (two suites of company offices), and FIRST
FLOOR (7 well-lighted offices).
The land has the following dimensions:
PITT STREET, 78ft. 4in.
O’CONNELL STREET, 59ft.
The through depth lines being 179 ft. and 128 ft. lOin.
respectively.
The attention of Companies, Capitalists, and others is
invited to this sale, as, considering the limited area of the
city propel-, especially in regard to its breadth from east
to west, it is inevitable that property so well situated as
this must rapidly increase in value with the great future
Sydney has before it.

Hie other night at a concert in Wellington, a German
pro.essor was getting “The Star of the hast ” off liis
chest, when a small child very audibly inquired—
Muvver, big man tingin’ Toldiers of Queen ? ”
The humor of the variety man is often rather incom-
prehensible. For instance, Hunter, at Sydney I’alace,
when gassing one night about his domestic affairs casually
remarked, that after many years of married life he had
come to honestly recognise Solomon as the wisest of men
—he had 700 wives, and slept with his fathers. On
another occasion, he was describing how he would paint
creation red if he had only great wealth, and after
describing many terrific things he would do he paused as
if working up a climax that would hit the audience and
take its breath away. Then he took a long breath and
slowly stated: “ Also, I would have butter on my
sausage ! ” Allusions to sausages are always understood
to be more or less funny, and the Palace audience shrieked.
Why is the sausage such a perennial source of mirth ?
The deceased “Dinny” Gunning was one of the best
second-tenor chorus singers in Australasia, but was so
much given to “wetting it” that once when with
Simonsen’s opera company he had to take the pledge for
six months to save his engagement. When the tiuie was
up “ Dinny ” went on a terrific jamboree ; concerning
which happening an Italian named Torselli made the
immortal observation :—“ I not understan’ dis beesness.
Vot good ? Drinkit noting for six inoiit’ ; den driukit
all in two day.”
Windy Wellington (ILL.) went stark, staring mad over
the festivity given to the South African contingent by
Tom Pollard at the Opera House. The Governor and
Digger Diek were present, and after the performance the
company were driven to the camp for supper. According
to a local paper, “ there was next day f a tired feeling’
noticeable over officers and men.” No wonder Voting
Australasia flies to soldiering. Free drinks, free sprees,
fair women, and the chance of a whole skin after all.
Theatrical managements, seemingly, are not much
addicted to troubling about the comfort of the minor
Stated that for the first time in the history of
the house, the present Melb. Royal management has pro-
vided chairs at the back of the stage for the accomodation
of “ extra ladies ’’ and children in the co.
Melba writes as though she were genuinely astounded
by the genius of little Fritz Mueller, the native-German
boy pianist from Melbourne, whom she met in Berlin.
Fritz is a wonder, no doubt about that, but as he hap-
pened to come to light during the Castles boom, there
was no manager to take him in hand anil run the child
around Australia ere he went to Germany. Therefore
Australia has missed its chance of being the first country
to go mad over the Beethoven of the future —which is not
a miss of much importance, anyhow. Fritz Mueller is
German, and Australia is going to hate the Germans
pretty soon without exactly knowing why.
At a recent performance of “ Faust,” at Dublin Theatre
Royal, when Martha, after the duel scene, was bending
over the prostrate Valentine, and piteously exclaiming,
“ Oh, what shall I do?” a voice from the gallery advised,
“ Unbutton his weskit.” A plain, sensible idea like that
never strikes the stage mourner. Her one inspiration,
when her lover is wounded, is to let her hair down and
throw her white arms in the air, so as to show her bangle.
“ Young Pro.” : When Kemble was playing “ Richard
III.,” one night, the part of Lord Radcliff was badly
hashed up. Radcliff should say (Act 5, scene 3) to King
Richard’s “ Who’s there ? ” “Radcliff, my Lord; 'Tis I.
The early village cock has twice done salutation to the
morn.” But, owing: to Radcliff forgetting his lines and
losing his punctuation, the following painful remarks took
placef—Richard (from his tent): “Who goes there?”
Radcliff : “’Tis I, the early village cock”—(long and ter-
rible pause). Then Richard, after prompting audibly to
no purpose : “ Then whv the hell don’t you crow ? ”
“.Stroller”: Writer was a member of a small co. tour-
ing the West. The manager left three of us stranded 300
miles from Sydney—penniless, and all credit wrecked.
Company had been billed a hundred miles down the line.
We three stranded ones gave a show in the town we were
left in—raising our fares to this second town. Arrived
there again penniless. All hotels required payment in
advance. One of us, a lady, had a brooch, but pawnshops
were unknown. At last, a friendly squash-seller nego-
tiated a loan. Then we took the hall, got the bills left at
the hotel by our absconding agent, billed the town
thoroughly for the large-cast melodrama previously an-
nounced, and opened. I took the money, the other man
the tickets, and the lady jumped up and down and
made turmoil behind the curtain, and battered things on
the floor to represent the alleged co. She also played an
overture behind thie curtain. We raised the latter and
discovered a breakfast table set in a landscape—all we
had, and I and the lady went on for a comedietta—in
deadly fear. They seemed to enjoy it in a bewildered sort
of way. Then the other man went in to explain, and we
gave them songs, recitations, anything for another hour
to a chorus of jeers, hoots, and catcalls. At the end we
lowered the curtain, got out by the back way, made a
detour to our lodgings and counted the money. It
panned out —fares to Sydney. We got away while the
town was still considering whether to tar and feather us
or sool a dog on to us.
The late W. G. Wills (author of “ Charles 1.,” “ Faust,”
and other plays produced by Irving, and brother of Free-
man Wills, the adapter of “ The Only Way,” recently
played at Sydney Royal) was not conspicuous for the
frequency of his ablutions. A friend met him in the
street one day, and, observing some j oke of egg on Wills’s
beard, said, “ I’ll wager I can tell what you had for break-
fast this morning. ’ “ Yes,” said Wills ; “ well, what had
I?” “Eggs.” “There j-ou’re out of it!" said Wills;
•“ I haven t had eggs for three days ! ”
According to the police intelligence, scarecrow dancer
Johnny Coleman was lately hauled up, as if lie were a
large stone going to the top of a tall building, at Kalgoor-
lie court, charged with threatening to remove Emile
Lazerne, of the same company, to a better land. Com-
plainant did not appear. Johnny had better come back
from the weary wastrel West, for the East has need
for comic singers who are comic, and has too many patter-
■rotic bawlers, hardly one of whom is even threatened with
music.
“ Melb.” : I have just been chatting with George
itidgway, as he takes checks at the Alexandria pit, for
the “ Cloches de Comeville.” He has a side-show in the
bill-sticking line, and is not so unlucky as Cerberus Bur-
ford, or Denvil, who took checks at the Drury Lane pit,
while Phelps acted Manfred, Denvil having been the
original of that part in the same theatre. Ridgway came
to Australia 34 years ago with the Lenton troupe, in-
cluding two of his brothers, also Leon Samwells and
Horatio Nelson. George was then the great performer on
the Flying Trapeze. Now he sticks the flying bill, and
does It well, too.
“ Haresfoot ” ; “ The Only' Way ” embodies, to m v idea,
the finest situation on the boards, taken from Dumas’
“ Chevalier de Maison Rouge.” Yet Dumas missed the
thing in the climax to his novel, where the prototvpes of
Dickeus’s Carton and Darnay both suffer under the
guillotine. In- the dramatisation the brilliant idea
occurred to Dumas of making Lorin, the scapegrace,
sacrifice himself for his friend Maurice. The “Chevalier
de Maison Rouge ” was-first acted in Paris not long before
the Revolution of 1848, and is said to have had a large
share in producing it. There was a tremendously suc-
cessful revival of the play on the verge of the Franco-
German war, and it came to the fore again in the
Boulanger epoch Dickens complained of Watts-Phillips
taking the finale of the “ Dead Heart ” from the “ Tale of
Two Cities,” but “ Maison Rouge ” inspired both.
“ S.S.” desires to strongly disagree with “ Melb.” (Bul-
letin, 20/l/’00). He says : “ The Macarte Sisters have
been a very mild success in Melbourne. The family' was
discovered by Rickards during his recent chase through
Europe, and is represented by three stout, silent, Teu-
tonic sisters who do unemotional things on a wire. After
the gifted vision whose feet were beautiful upon the high
way at the Royal when the Flying Jordans hung out
there, the woman posing on an invisible string must per-
form like an angel and have the looks of a pleasant little
devil to touch the popular heart. By the way, it was the
Jordan’s wire-walker, under another name, who knocked
London bj' ascending to a trapeze in full street costume,
and stripping to the skin-tights during picturesque aerial
evolutions.
“ S.S.” : Johnny Sheridan’s Mrs. Block does not knock
as his Widow O’Brien did, probably for the reason that
Mrs. Block is just the Widow 10 years younger, and with
all the humanity rasped off her. I remember the Widow
with respect, in spite of the fact that I never saw her in
decent company, because she was a human document.
The pinched waist, the fashionable Worth gowns,. the
rouge, and the obvious effort to be femininely attractive,
bring Sheridan’s present job down to the level of the
ordinary female impersonator, and often provoke that
tire 1 feeling.

“ Takes two to make a bargain.” That s the rule.
The two will never change—a rogue and fool.
“ N.” : Did you notice that case in the
London newspapers of a few weeks ago ? In the
height of the rejoicing about one of the British
victories over the Boers, a London publichouse-
lceeper of an inferior order refused to serve two
sober English Queen’s soldiers, in their glorious
redcoat uniform, with liquor, on the ground that
Tommy Atkins drove away Respectable custom !
What a commentary on the poor Absent-Minded
Beggar and the real esteem in which he is held, as
contrasted with the lying lip-hypocrisy.
Our own Beggar—not the Absent-Minded
one :
Yes, it's been a strugglin’ Christmas ; things ’as been
uncommon blue.
And yet the ’at was goin’ round orlright
For the families of the soldiers-lucky dorgs ; while me
and you
Ain’t never ’ad no bloomin’ dad to fight.
This ’ere Kiplin’s done it, blarst 'im ;
If I knew the bloke I d arst him
If ’e’d show me ’ow to do it, if it’s easy stuff to write.
I dop’t blame the people givin’ to the widders and the
wives,
It onlv seems quite nat’ral-like and fair ;
But I see they don’t start givin' till some po try stun
arrives
’Bout an “ Absent-Minded Beggar” over there.
Some’ow Kiplin’ finds their purses
With ’is orlright, slap-up verses,
That’s why I’m tryin’ po’try—’av yer any think to spare t
My dad, ’e fought no battles, yet ’e fought most all ’is
life
To bring up me and ’arf-a-dozen more ;
And me mother, ’ow she ’elped 'itn in ’is struggle and is
strife 1 —
Now both of ’em, God bless em’s, gone before.
Pity Kiplin’ ’adn’t known us,
’Cos we’re same in blood and bone as
The widders and the kiddies of the soldiers at the war.
Melb.
J.A.P.


A SHATTERED NERVOUS SYSTEM
Neuralgia, Indigestion, Weak and Hopeless.
compi .ICATIONS ALL YIELD TO CLEMENTS TONIC
An Influential Cure Worthy of Careful Perusal.
The Case of*
Mrs, JOHN GEDDES.
for the Keivloicn Chronicle.)
When the tram cars pass by Enmore they
speed with a rush down a gentle incline,
skirting a park, over which the traveller
catches glimpses of the distant sea. Pre-
sently the train gives a sudden swerve round
a bend, and the guard shouts, “Victoria
Koad 1” It was here our reporter descended,
almost at the door of Mr. Geddes, a well-
known storekeeper of the Marrickville
district. He inquired for Mrs. Geddes, who
was, fortunately, at home, yet who answered
the reporter’s enquiries with some little
hesitation, as she does not like publicity ; at
the same time her feeling of thankfulness for
a remarkable recovery from dire sickness
was so great that she thought it only right
to others, placed as she had been, to put
her hesitation on one side, and make known
the true facts of the case.
“ We were living at Bundanoon at that
time,” said Mrs. Geddes, “and owing to
some unknown cause I got into a bad state
of health, continued to get worse and
worse, till my plight was quite pitiful.
This lasted for two years.”
“ How did your sickness affect you, Mrs.
Geddes ?” asked the reporter.
“My most serious trouble was indiges-
tion, which at last came to such a stage
that I could not keep any food on my
stomach. Even when I tried to drink a
little put-e water I should retch it up at
once ; and the water that came up when I
vomited Would be Quite black to look at.
showing, I think, that my digestive organs
were in a shocking and deplorable state.
And yet all the time I had a strong desire
and craving to eat, and I dared not. There
was ever present a feeling of oppression and
weight upon my chest, and a sharp pain
between the shoulders that pierced right
through my body, so that it filled me with
apprehensions that my lungs were affected,
and that I should soon be a victim to con-
sumption. My limbs were in a constant
tremor, my heart palpitating with distress-
ing throbs. Neuralgia came in my face
and forehead, and was so bad at times 1
thought 1 should go out of my mind. And,
with my general collapse, my eyesight
suffered ; clouds of mist hid my sight when
I tried to use my eyes for any length of
time. The natural colour of my skin
changed to a deep yellow, like one suffering
from jaundice, and I would almost have
pissed for a mulatto. I am sure that my
nervous system was shattered. 1 was
weak, hopeless, and out of heart, and came
finally to that helpless condition that 1
could not crawl as far as the back garden.
As it was, I utterly despaired of getting
better. I became very frightened, the least
noise upsetting me. 1 even dreaded, from
sheer nervousness, to go into the next
room.”
“ Did you not see a doctor or try remedies
of any kind ? ” enquired the reporter.
“ No ; Bundanoon, as perhaps you know,
is nine miles from Moss Vale, and the
charge made by a doctor coming that
distance in the country is quite beyond the
means of all but rich people. Bor my
indigestion J tried a number of the remedies
so much advertised as cures for that com-
plaint, but they did me no good whatever.
And in the same way I tried the local
remedies for the neuralgic pains in my face
and forehead without benefit, for I did
not understand then, as I do now, that my
neuralgia was only one of the symptoms of
a deeply seated malady and impoverished
blood.”
“But you found relief at last, did you
not, Mrs. Geddes ? ”
“Yes; when 1 had got fairly tired of
trying one thing and then another, it
happened that we were talking to a friend
of ours at Bundanoon, and who, having
some medical knowledge and experience,
was much consulted in the district. He
advised me very strongly to try Clements
Tonic ; and it was a happy day for me
when I did so. When 1 had bought a
bottle at the store, and commenced to tako
it according to the directions, I found a
marvellous change for the better after the
first few doses; and as 1 continued to take
Clements Tonic, all my troubles and pains
grew lighter and more easy, till my good
health returned. Before I had finished
taking the first bottle I could eat freely
without vomiting or retching my food up.
The neuralgic pain, the heart trouble, the
impaired vision, the pain between the
shoulders, and the wretched, despondent
feeling, together with my nervous weakness,
all yielded and faded away before the
recuperative influence of Clements Tonic ;
and when I found u hat the remedy was
doing for me, I stuck to it, and persevered
with it, and long after I had recovered, and
felt quite well, I continued to take Clements
Tonic, to make my’ recovery doubly sure,
for, being well again, nothing was so dread-
ful to me as the thought of a relap-e. So I
made sure to properly clinch the cure.”
“ And you found your cure genuine, Mrs.
Geddes, und not a mere flash in the pan ? ”
enquired the reporter.
“ Yes ; and after that I had the best ot
health for several years, so I think my cure
was as genuine as a cure could I e ; and you
may be sure I have recommended Clements
Tonic whenever I had the opportunity, and
this often occurs, for, as you see, my
husband and I are in business.”
“Ate you willing that this testimony
should be made public?”
“ Yes; I feel that it is only right for me
to do so, for it may be the means of telling
some sufferer, with symptoms like my own,
that a sure cure is waiting in Clements
Tonic.”

Pin Lung’s Missus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pin Lung’s garden was an oasis in a wilderness of
sand, sludge, tailings, and broken reef. There
was a battery somewhere up the creek ; the
mellowed drumming of its stamps catne down the
gully in the motionless noons, when the raw sun-
shine smote rock and sand and ridge pitilessly,
and it seemed to soften the silence. Set out in
grim oblongs, green as vegetation still watered by
a widow’s tears, the garden lay. Amid the
drab grass and desolate gums it seemed a fresh,
cool, verdant Paradise, but if you ventured too
near the green graves under that belief, subtle
doubts came up to assail you through the hot,
glassy atmosphere, and you took to your nose and
fled, realising again that beauty is rooted in decay,
and in the midst of life we are in death.
In a corner of the garden furthest from the
creek, piled against the fence, was what seemed to
be an accumulation of miscellaneous refuse thrown
there in the course of years. This was really
Pin Lung’s home. Here Pin Lurig fed and
smoked and slept. From the thick of it he issued
mysteriously, blinking at the sun, a celestial
Adam without an Eve.
Pin Lung grew “ cahhagee ” and “callot for
the miners down the creek and over the ridges,
and for the township up the creek, and prospered
by reason of a brutal industry and an infantile
audacity in the matter of charges. Pin’s habitual
attitude towards bis masculine customers up the
creek was one of conciliation ; he had always the
pose of a man forgetting and forgiving, and, truth
to tell, he luid much to forgive. He smiled inces-
santly and babbled unintelligibly, and pressed
little presents upon the men, and they in turn
paid him through the nose for every thing they did
not steal ; abused him, patronised him, and :l £ r £ed
among themselves that “Paddy” was not a dashed
bad lot for a dashed Chow.
To the women in the township Pin Lung was
quite another Chinaman. To them ho was the
piteous innocent in the land of guile ; he compre-
hended nothing that did not suit his book, and
misunderstood the expostulations of crafty house-
wives with the passionless suavity of his own clay
god.
But the Chinaman was not happy. There was
no compatriot within visiting distance, and Pin s
apparent friendship with the Caucasian was, after
all, only akin to the conversation of a horse with
a human being. It is not good for man to live
alone, even if he parts his hair into a tail and is
the whole support of a god entirely devoted to
his welfare.
Pin thought the matter over for fourteen years,
and then he set to work and built himself a decent
two-roomed slab-hut, which he furnished in
European- at least Dogleg European—fashion,
and then he called in Squint Higgins, and said :
“You come longa my place, min’ my cabbagee,
my callot, dlive ’way fellah what come stealum,
mine give tlen shilly one day, tlen sliilly one more
day.”
“My coagulated oath !” said Higgins.
“ Welly goo’, you come t’mollo’. Me go way
til* while.”
“Righto, Paddy.”
Squint Higgins was the least remorseful thief,
from Dogleg to the Day of Judgment, and Pin
Lung knew it. Squint had instantaneous designs
on Pin Lung's hidden riches, and Pin Long
m lH q BB d it; but the gardener shaved Ins great
sweep of neck, and polished his varnished
head and went on his journey with » con-
fiding smile and the inscrutability of Asia a
the back of his narrow black eyes. Higgins
immediately seized upon pick and shove),
and went digging by and large in Pin. Lungs
unbroken land seeking buried treasure. 1-or two
days lie worked with the ardour of a madman
worked from daylight to dark, and then continued
his quest by candlelight, pausing only to hurl
clods and curses at the wondering men who name
to gaze in solemn amazement over the tone o .
Higgins was the tired man of the creek, and the
popular curiosity was only to be expected, but
Hig! jins was large and powerful, and was energetic
in combat, and it happened that no predatory
hand was laid upon “ cabbagee,” or ‘ callot, or
“onlin ” of Pin Lung’s. . ~
Pin Lung returned to a disappointed and dis-
gusted man on the morning of the third dav—
returned to find £5 worth of work done m his
garden and all the barren land turned up to the
quickening sun, and he smiled a smile in which
Higgins could read nothing to justify an assault,
keen as he was for it. Higgins became discreet:
“ I thought I’d be doing a bit av work t am
me money,” lie-said. ~
“ Welly goo' ! welly goo’! welly ni ! said 1 in.

The gardener had not returned alone. I here
was a lady at his heels —a stout, fair woman of
about. 40, with a beery ruddiness in her cheeks,
and a ready smile denoting that callous cheeilul-
ness indestructible in some fair women.
“My missy,” said Lung. “Me bin mally down
Bailnsdale.”
Pin Lung’s marriage created something of a
sensation along the creek, and that night a tin-
kettling party visited the garden and performed
industriously for two hours, when the bridegroom
rewarded the musicians with five shillings, and
ten men walked four miles to spend it in beer.
For a time it was thought the Chinaman had
made another excellent bargain. Mrs. P. Lung
was industrious and cleanly, and seemed to thrive
in the rank atmosphere of the garden. In her
leisure, she read through amazing piles ot LONDON
Journals, revelling sympathetically in t! e elegant
lives of iong, willowy peers and wonderful
duchesses, and peace was with Pin Lung for many
days. Then, suddenly, the missus exhibited a
new phase of character. One afternoon, the men
at the pnddler were startled by shrill cries of
mortal agony rising from Lung’s Eden, and the
echoes of the Celestial’s grief travelled far and
wide in the hills. The population of the gully
rushed to Pin Lung’s fence, and there watched
the progress of events in appreciative calm.
Mrs. Pin was laboriously pursuing her lord and
master up and down and round about the garden,
armed with a hoe-handle. Pin Lung’s eye was
blackened, and his head was bleeding, and Mrs.
Lung was amusingly drunk. The lady’s habitual

clung to her legs, ruined tomatoes bespattered her,
and, floundering or charging, she repeated, in a
thin, breathless squeal:
“ I’ll murder you ! I’ll murder you ! 111 murder
y °l*in Lung danced before her, dodging her blows,

Scenes akin to the above became quite common

Mrs. Pin Lung remained a peaceful and reason-

Pin Lung had a horse and spring cart to carry
his vegetables to the township, and when his wife

wont to go hortie. - , ,

Mrs. M. A. BISHOP, OF LAUNCESTON.
Household duties should not be a drudgery—
they are not when women are strong and well.
But where backs are aching and heads throbbing
as though they would break, even the joys of life
are turned to drudgery.
Do you know what makes the backaches, and
headaches, and the all-tired-out feeling?
Sick kidneys. They are the filters of the blood,
and cannot stand all the work put upon them.
Backache is the cry of the kidneys for help—and
it is a warning of more serious trouble to follow.
Unless this backache is taken in time, it will run
into urinary troubles, Diabetes, Bright’s Disease,
and —death.
Women do not need to suffer—there is a remedy
for backaches and for all other symptoms of kid-
ney disease —Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.
They should be used to stop the progress of any
and all disorders of the kidneys and urinary
organs, and may be thoroughly depended on.
Mrs. M. A. Bishop, whose picture is given
above, of 153 St. John Street, Launceston, Tas-
mania, said she had suffered from kidney disease
for five years. “My kidneys were very weak,”
she writes. “My back was very bad. It ached
so dreadfully that I felt tired out after doing the
least thing. The pains would at times work up
between my shoulders, but they were worst in the
small of my back. The kidneys were very weak
indeed, as certain symptoms showed. Oh, I tried
everything, liniments, plasters, and many medi-
cines, but nothing ever reached the kidneys like
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills. They seemed to
act directly on the kidneys and on them alone.
They strengthened my kidneys, helped my back,
and have done me a world of good—more than
anything I have ever taken. This is true, and you
may publish it if you wish.” (Signed) Mrs. M. A.
Bishop.
If any woman who reads this has suffered as
Mrs. Bishop did, and thought the symptoms
were due to some female trouble, let her take
heart, for nine-tenths of all so-called female
troubles is really kidney trouble, and should be
treated as such. Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
are a splendid tonic to the whole female consti-
tution.

At about this time Mrs. Lung discovered her
husband in. the act of hiding a peculiar-looking
copper canister in a hole in the ground The
canister when shaken gave forth a grateful jingle
of minted gold. Mrs. Pin Lung watched anxiously
from the cover of some currant bushes, and noted
Pin’s apparent great anxiety. Really Pin had
shown none of his usual cunning and secretiveness
in the conduct of this job ; on the contrary, he had
been singularly wanting in caution and strategy.
Mrs. Pin Lung thought a great deal about her
discovery during the remainder of the night.
At noon on the second day after the planting of
the capister, the butcher’s boy from Dogleg called
over Pin Lung’s fence :
“I say, John —you missy gone away ? ”
|| Huh ! ” grunted Pin.
“You missy gone away—elopee Longa Missa
Higgins.”
“Welly goo’,” said John, sweetly. “Elopee
welly goo’, welly ni’, welly cheap.”
Throughout all that day Pin Lung worked
placidly, amongst his vegetables, with a tender
and benign smile wreathing his lips, and murmur-
ing contentedly: “Elopee Missa Higgins, welly
ni’, welly cheap ! ”
Next day Pin Lung carted his furniture and
ware into Dogleg, and sold the lot, and after that
he rooted up his natty slab hut, and returned to
the rubbish-heap in the corner, where he lives in
apparent content. When Higgins and Mrs. Pin
Lung burst open the copper canister, they found
in it a great quantity of cash—Chinese cash to the
value of one and sevenpence halfpenny.
EDWARD DYSON.
The Blessings of War.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m in favor of the war, and of half-a-dozen more J
And I think we should have had one long before—
There is nothing to deplore ; I’m in favor of the
war
Independent of all statements made by Briton or
by Boer.
’Tis a healthy stirring up of the dregs of sorr. w’-
oup;
’Tis a joyful thing, as I have always held,
For it brings us something new. And I’m looking
forward to
The festive time when Sydney shall be shelled !
Oh, the bush is dull and brown, and it’s duller
still in town,
And the Bush Bard and his reader crave for
change,
For they’re sick of Bill and Jim, and the dusty
Track and grim,
And they’re tired of chasing brumbies in the range.
We need ne’er in rhyme again carry swag across
the plain—
We need never fight the “ ’ploorer,” drought and
flood,
For Jack Cornstalk gone to war (as Bill Shake-
speare said before)
Shall “be copy now to men of grosser blood.”
Shall be copy now at home both in story, sketoh,
and “ pome ”
In the new Australian drama he’ll be great.
Both in letters and in art he will play the paying
part
(And ’tis farewell to the swagman and his mate).
When he’s sick of grubbing stumps, of the posts
and rails he humps,
And the old man keeps him grafting all the while,
We won’t send him off Out Back with a swag or
with a pack,
But we’ll send him to South Africa in style.
And he’ll come back in the drought, and he’ll
farm the old man out,
And he’ll take his mother home to live with him;
And a billet or a grant will be all on earth he’ll
want —
And he’ll wed the squatter’s daughter fair and
slim.
When his life is hard and grim, and his best girl’s
false to him,
And he’s sick and tired and mad about it all—
We will ship him with three cheers and the Dingo
Volunteers,
And his manly breast expanding for the ball.
But he’ll come back with the rest, and his V.C.
on his breast
(Oh we’ll bring him back in glory and in rhyme),
And the girl, her blue eyes dim, who has pined
and longed for him —
She will tell him that she loved him all the time.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Missing Note of the Chord.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After a three years’ confidence, their tempera-
ments, with a most relentless persistency, clamored
for severance.
Their acquaintance had commenced in a
romance, and ended in marriage and the faint
essence of a languishing sentiment.
In the days of their affection he admired her
spirit and independence, and adored her beauty.
She loved the poet in him. But the days come
and go, and constancy is not always as faithful as
the coming of another day. Their mutual admira-
tion had gradually faded, and, as an endeavour to
resuscitate their dwindling passion, their one
month of marriage had been as valueless as a
pastime.
He found her too assertive, and, in many ways,
too uncultured for his lustreless nature ; she con-
sidered him merely as an outline of spiritlessness,
a vaporous quantity that needed replenishing
with audacious tableaux. He was content with
his books, and a quiet, dreamy life, and the halo
of a colourless conscience; she had vigor and
decision, and where quivering dolts shook their
knee-bones in hesitancy, she laughed and stepped
over in delicious insolence. So that their affinities
gaped widely, and were too antagonistic for a
reconciliation.
One night, while he was peacefully reading,
Peggie suddenly became exalted with the spirit of
/
a reckless freedom, and to tantalize him, she
trifled and sported with his repose by playfully
hurling all her private longings and cherished
convictions at his head. These were not delicate,
but strong and unrestrained, and each bore the
burden of a taunt.
He recognized their meaning, and rose to the
occasion. Peggie had never seen him so roused
before, and out of pure devilment, communicated
some more of her persuasions. She teased and
provoked him, and laughed at his displeasure when
he heard that civilized morals were constraints,
and cramps, and gave each the life of a withered
mummy. From an unequal argument they
drifted, as is the nature of mankind, into per-
sonalities. He called her “an animal ” ; she
called him “ a scentless mortal,” and they
became so emphatic that Peggie was in the
seventh heaven of delight, and her husband in a
corresponding fury. At last he wearied, and, as
a surrender, shuffled to his books again, and
Peggie remained victor.
The next morning, as a courtesy to their feel-
ings, they decided to go separate ways. They
became very friendly in their eager acquiescence,
and parted amiably—their farewell a thanks-
giving.
Her wilder nature had swept his finer senses
away, and pitilessly enslaved him, but now, and
he rejoiced, the silence of his dreams would have
no disturbance. Peggie had gone for a freer life,
and he had regained his quiet one, so all things
were in harmony.
The days passed by, and he was content. All
the happiness he desired he found between the
pages of a book. The leaves of a book can make a
religion. Every leaf bestowed its glory, and,
submissive to his natural tendencies, he lost
himself in his reveries.
The three past years had been as disordered as
chaos, and the last month even worse, and, as a
delightful indulgence, he resolved, with every
fresh page, to erase a memory of them. It was as
if his most earnest wishes and ardent prayers had
been gratified, and there needed nothing further
to complete the fullness of his life. And he read
and read, and dreamed the days away. And
Peggie rambled here and there in a perfect
wilderness of pleasures.
The weeks followed in rapid succession. Some-
times, now, he would close and snap his book in a
hasty and foreign impatience. An irritation,
evidently, had manifested itself and needed dis-
pelling, but although he endeavoured with all his
power to banish it, the obstacle still remained—
Peggie’s face still kept intruding.
If he threw his book away and looked into the
fire, then the glowing embers gave her life and
framed her, and every quivering, leaping flame
became a phase of Peggie’s tumultuous nature.
And he heard her singing in every room. Memory
was making his solitude heavy with regret, and
the flimsy solace of his books exasperated him.
One day in a crowded thoroughfare he passed a
group of people laughing and talking. One lady,
the gayest of them all, was dressed in white, with
lace trailing all over her, and a flaming red parasol
shading her face. By moving close up he could
almost hear what she was saying. She laughed
joyously, and her companions laughed with her,
and the open red parasol whirled round and round
in a malicious merriment. Then they moved
away, and although she went down so far that he
couldn’t distinguish her over the heads of the
people, the parasol flaunted its bright and vivicl
defiance back to him.
The months went swiftly by, and a year had
fone, and the calmness of his life was desecrated.
lis regret had changed to a passionate desire,
and the longing for Peggie grew stronger. His
books were deserted, and he spent his time in the
busy streets looking for her. Every red parasol
became a magnet, a delirious hope, but it was
never Peggy’s face it shaded. As the dust settled
more thickly on his books, so his failures kept in
unison. He became embittered and desperate.
His dreamy nature was transformed into a rest-
less hopelessness, a dogged endeavour that sapped
all the tranquilness from him. He had almost
given up in despair when, out of a tangle and
confusion of traffic, an electric tram-car rushed by
him, and brought his salvation. Peggie sat inside
—Peggie so white and thin, that the sight of her
made him half mad in his eagerness to stop the
car. In running, he stumbled and fell, and the
tram was soon lost in the distance.
He walked home in a misery that made him
dizzy. It was torture to think of his unfortunate
slip, for, perhaps, Peggie would have been with
him now ! His unsteady steps trudged up the
stairs to his door. He forgot to wonder why it
was a little open when he had left it shut.
He dropped into a chair. Something in the
aspect of the room astonished him. The chairs
were arranged differently, the curtains had dis-
carded their limp straightness and hung in
soft folds ; his tobacco that he had left on the
table was on the shelf, his books were dusted and
placed in order. A general alteration had taken
place.
He stood up in surprise and looked round.
Perhaps the other rooms had also been visited.
He walked from one to the other, and stopped,
bewildered, for a little while, before he reached
the bedroom-door.
The fragrance of a long remembranoe came to
him. He ran in.
“Peggie ! Peggie ! ”
He clasped the dainty figure, and kissed the
white face.
“ Peggie ! You have come back to me ! ”
She laughed. “Yes, the poor, tired prodigal
has comajiome—for quietness—and—relaxation.”
He dropped her hands.
“Relaxation ! For nothing better than that,
Peggie?”
“Yes, something more.’’
“ For love of me, Peggy ? ”
“ Well ” —with her old mischief—“ I’m afraid it
is really more tangible than that. I came to
give you a-a present.”
“ A present! ”
“Yes. I think you will like it. I brought it
for you because I thought it would be a comfort
for you—for us, as we grow old. ”
“For our old age? What can it be, Peggy?”
his spirits rising. “ A foot-warmer ? ” His levity
was disregarded.
“ You know,” she said, “we two are moulded
so differently—are so contrary that, for fear we
should clash again, I brought this—this present
to keep the balance even.” Her eyes shone as he
had never seen them shine. “Walter, you
haven’t looked at the bed ! ” He turned and
looked; went over —looked again for an incredul-
ous moment—then lifted the little bundle in his
arms.
“Good God!” he said to himself more than
once—“ Good God !—a baby t ”
